de las Municipalidades; (4) Caracterizaci6n deAlgunas ONGs; (11) Evaluaci6n Social en los
Departamentos de Le6n y Chinandega.



23
resources and the environment. The natural resources sectors are plagued
by environmental externalities that spill over the boundaries of local
jurisdictions and local institutions are not suited for dealing with them. A
clear policy and institutional framework needs to be put in place to
minimize these environmental risks.
*  Second the IDA components would contribute to the Atlantic Biodiversity
Corridor: (i) channeling rural development investments and agricultural
intensification to the heavily populated Pacific region, thereby reducing the
causes leading to out-migration and expansion of the agricultural frontier
into the lowly populated Atlantic region; (ii) preparing critical legislation
on natural resource use and environment and supporting the enforcement
of existing and new legislation when enacted; (iii) through legislative
proposals, legal analyses and studies proposing and helping clarify
responsibilities over natural resource management between GON and
autonomous governments and indigenous communities of the Atlantic
region; (iv) strengthening the protected area system (SINAP) and the
institutional capacity of the Division of Protected Areas of MARENA,
charged with its management; and (v) training and strengthening Atlantic
municipal governments in environmental planning and monitoring and in
providing services to indigenous communities and increasing community
participation.
*  Third the GEF supported activities would complement the IDA
components by: (i) identifying priority areas for conservation and
sustainable use in the Atlantic to guide local public spending (the ABC
strategy); (ii) establishing sustainable mechanisms, including for
biodiversity protection, biodiversity monitoring and biodiversity impact
assessment procedures to ensure that local investment decisions
adequately internalize global biodiversity concerns related to the ABC;
(iii) financing local projects in the ABC related to global biodiversity
benefits; (iv) training local governments and indigenous and non-
indigenous communities in methodologies and technologies consistent
with the overall objective of improved natural resource management
including biodiversity conservation; and (v) as part of the Indigenous
Peoples Development Plan developing the legislative framework for
effective application of the indigenous peoples constitutional rights.
D. LESSONS FROM PAST BANK LENDING
4.5    Beneficiary Participation. There is considerable accumulated experience in
the LAC region on demand-driven development activities, which would be the basis
for the proposed project. A recent Bank review on this type of project in Latin



24
America'2 reported that: (i) by encouraging the active involvement of community
groups, such projects are much more likely to meet the community's needs than if they
are chosen to reflect the priorities of a government agency; (ii) once the communities
develop a sense of ownership of a project, they are willing to share in its costs and to
ensure its maintenance; and (iii) once a community group is given responsibility for
implementing a project that it has helped to design, it shows a great interest in
ensuring that the private contractor executing the project does so well and honestly.
4.6    Rural Decentralization. Experience of decentralized rural development
initiatives in Brazil, Bolivia, Colombia and Mexico has been positive. A recent
dissemination note"3 lists the following positive features: (i) local governments have
proven to be effective in identifying and implementing micro-projects; (ii)
decentralization has improved the response time of local governments to community
needs; (iii) local government action has improved transparency and accountability; (iv)
local governments have often been more effective in targeting the poor by involving
communities in project selection; and (v) in some cases, local governments have been
effective in raising local revenue. (See Section 7E for a discussion of risks of
decentralization and Section 7C for a discussion of costs and benefits of
decentralization).
4.7    A study that reviewed the rules governing three Demand-driven Rural
Investment Funds'4 is under completion by LATAD"5. It compares three projects
implemented long enough to have a track record--Colombia's Fondo DRI, Brazil's
PAC/FUMAC, and Mexico's Solidaridad programs--and explores the extent to which
a carefully-devised rule and incentive structure can replace central control and
centrally-imposed constraints, while assuring that "caudillismo" and "clientelismo"
will not become ascendant. The study demonstrates the wide range of possible
solutions that can be designed to foster accountability and efficiency, and highlights
the need for flexible rules that are grounded in the realities of local circumstances and
can be redesigned based on local experience. Specifically, the lessons learned from the
comparative analysis of these projects are: (i) A gradual process of increasing
decentralization allows individual municipalities to take control of the various aspects
of rural development, enhancing community participation. (ii) Targeting mechanisms
12  Glaessner, Philip, Kye Woo Lee, Anna Maria Sant'Anna and Jean Jacques de St. Antoine.
1994. Poverty Alleviation and Social Development Funds: The Latin American Experience,
World Bank Discussion Papers 261, Washington, D.C.
13  Aiyar, Swaminathan, Andrew Parker and Johan van Zyl. 1995. How Well Has Decentralization
Workedfor Rurtal Development? Dissemination Note No 2, Agriculture & Natural Resources
Department, World Bank, Washington, DC.
14  DRIFs are distinguished from social fund-type projects in that they specifically aim to target
rural populations and support activities that are more oriented towards productive infrastructure
and/or natural resources management.
15  Tom Wiens and Maurizio Guadagni. The Design of Rules for Demand-Driven Rural Investment
Funds: the Latin American Experience. LATAD Studies on Decentralization, 1996. Draft.



25
should be carefully designed both among and within municipalities. For example, the
differentiation of cofinancing requirements according to the income level of
municipalities--requiring a lower cofinancing from poorer municipalities--helps
improving poverty targeting. (iii) The enforcement of a complex set of rules is
essential but problematic. Therefore, particular emphasis should be given to
dissemination, monitoring, and enforcement. These lessons were incorporated in the
design of the proposed project.
4.8   Biodiversity Protection. The design and preparation of the proposed project
has drawn on lessons derived from World Bank experience in implementing
biodiversity projects. World Bank support to parks and protected areas has grown
rapidly over the last five years. The Bank's IBRD/IDA portfolio of biodiversity related
activities for FY92-95 includes some 27 projects with a total loan/credit value of US$
287 million. In addition, the Bank has implemented 31 GEF biodiversity projects
worldwide, for which the GEF participants have allocated $244 million between FY
92-95. A recent World Bank report, Mainstreaming Biodiversity in Development: a
World Bank Assistance Strategyfor Implementing the Convention on Biologzcal
Diversity, highlights some of the key factors contributing to successful project
implementation. These include: institutional strengthening, participation of local
stakeholders, financial sustainability, flexible and iterative processes, and decentralized
management of protected areas.
4.9   To date there have been no GEF projects in Nicaragua, but experiences of
bilateral financed projects in the Atlantic region, by DANIDA and the Netherlands in
the South Atlantic and GTZ and USAID in Bosawas in the North Atlantic, confirm
these lessons. These projects have found that small farmer training for the adoption of
appropriate technologies is the single most cost efficient intervention for
environmental protection in the region. They also found that assistance to indigenous
communities for the recognition of their lands and natural resources rights are an
essential condition for project success where indigenous communities are present.
Other highly successful activities include community development activities, such as
schools, which help to stabilize migrant populations, support for land titling for small
farmers, support on environmental matters to local govemments and to regionally
based representatives of the central govemment, and support to inter-institutional and
NGO coordinating mechanisms. The proposed project includes components and
measures that attempt to incorporate these lessons learned.
E. RATIONALE FOR IDA AND GEF INVOLVEMENT
4.10 Consistency with CAS. The Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) for
Nicaragua, presented to the Board on May 27, 1994 (Report No. P-6340-NI), has the
following four key objectives: (i) reviving growth; (ii) reforming the public sector and
strengthening institutional capacity; (iii) improving environmental and natural resource
management; and (iv) alleviating poverty and investing in human capital. The
proposed project is consistent with these four objectives.



26
*  First, it would help foster economic growth by providing for public and
private investment in rural areas, targeting investments towards the
country's most productive agricultural region and the most heavily
populated.
*  Second, it would assist in the reform of public sector institutions involved
in natural resource management, strengthen municipal governments and
emphasize decentralization of investment decisions and project
implementation. GON views the local decision-making mechanism to be
supported by the project as a pilot to be replicated in other municipalities
with the implementation of the fiscal transfers mechanism for
disadvantaged municipalities included in the Partial Reform of the
Constitution of June 1995 and in the proposed Municipalities Law in the
National Assembly.
*  Third, it would improve environmental and natural resource management
through assistance for policy reform and specific subproject investments.
The associated GEF component, in particular, would support conservation
and sustainable use of biodiversity in the Atlantic Corridor.
*  Finally, it would assist in alleviating rural poverty and in improving the
skills of rural people and of local institutions that serve them. The project
would also meet a number of specific recommendations for the rural
sector of the recent Poverty Assessment for Nicaragua (Report No.
14038-NI), specifically: (i) strengthening sector implementation capacity
at the national and local levels; (ii) promoting agricultural growth by
removing information, marketing and infrastructure constraints; and (iii)
strengthening agricultural research, training and extension services.
4.11   Consistency with GEF Strategies. The proposed GEF component is
consistent with the guidance from the First and Second Conferences of the Parties
(COP I and COP2) as it would: address in situ conservation; include capacity
building; strengthen conservation, management, and sustainable use of ecosystems and
habitats, including coastal and marine ecosystems and mountain regions; strengthen
the involvement of local and indigenous peoples; and integrate social dimensions,
including those related to poverty. The proposed GEF component would also be
responsive to the objectives stated for the GEF Operational Program targeted to forest
ecosystems (operational program 2) and is part of a specific GEF sub-program
focused on Meso America that aims to protect a biological corridor from Mexico to
Colombia. Related projects are currently under preparation in Honduras and Panama.
4.12  Consistency with Other IDA Operations. The project would complement
four on-going IDA operations that support development of the rural sector, namely: (i)
the Institutional Development Credit (Credit No. 2690-NI), (ii) the First and (iii)



27
Second Social Investment Funds (FISE) (Credits No. 2934-NI and 2767-NI); and (iv)
the Agricultural Technology and Land Management Project (Credit No. 2536-NI).
*  The Institutional Development Credit (Credit 2690-NI) focuses on
supporting CERAP (Executive Committee for the Reform of the Public
Sector) and streamlining and improving management of GON agencies
through organizational reform, reduction of superfluous personnel and
programs and strengthening of management functions. Both MARENA
and INIFOM are included in the group of first six institutions to be
reformed and have recently negotiated Institutional Reform Agreements
(IRA) that would lay the ground rules for these institutions to receive
financial assistance. The proposed project would complement this Credit
by focusing on strengthening the technical functions of those institutions,
which are not covered by the IRAs, and by advancing the decentralization
process, which is one of CERAP's objectives.
*  The FISE, which is supported by both IDA (FISE I, Credit No. 2434-NI,
and FISE II, Credit No. 2767-NI), the IDB and other donors, started in
1993, and so far a high proportion (52 percent) of its investments has
covered social infrastructure (schools, health clinics, etc.), and only 0.2
percent has been directed towards the environment. The proposed project
would be highly complementary, in that it would focus on technical
assistance, natural resources and the environment, productive activities
and economic infrastructure in poor rural communities and it would place
special emphasis on strengthening local institutions -- municipal
governments, NGOs and communities. In the long-term, this process
should help provide a sustainable alternative to the FISE for channeling
local investments.
*  The Agricultural Technology and Land Management Project (Credit No.
2536-NI) focuses on agricultural research, extension and land titling on a
national scale for both small farmers and indigenous communities. The
land titling activities for small farmers are critical for the success of the
proposed project because more secure property rights would contribute to
reducing rural poverty by facilitating local investments. The land titling
activities for indigenous communities would help conserve natural
resources in indigenous lands and slow down the advance of the
agricultural frontier in the Atlantic. The proposed project would
complement this by supporting applied extension in natural resources
matters targeted at small farmers and by providing additional resources in
a demand driven fashion to assist with the demarcation of indigenous
territories, subjects which are not well covered in the on-going project.
4.13  The project would also complement the Roads Rehabilitation and Maintenance
Project (Credit No. 2871-NI), recently approved by the World Bank, and which aims



28
at strengthening the institutions that are responsible for road transport in Nicaragua
and rehabilitating the Chinandega-Izapa section of the trunk road connecting
Nicaragua with Honduras and Costa Rica, which is in the proposed project main
region. This portfolio of projects in combination with the currently proposed one
would attack not only the social manifestations of rural poverty (poor health services,
sanitation, education), but also its root cause, the lack of technical, managerial and
financial capacity at the local level which underlies much of the rural poverty in the
country and the inappropriate and destructive use of productive resources.
4.14 Consistency with Other Donor Activities. There has been relatively little
leadership in Nicaragua on the matter of donor coordination. However, it is clear that
the proposed project and the on-going IDB/IFAD-financed National Rural
Development Program (PNDR) are central to the GON efforts to address rural
poverty. The PNDR is a newly-created institution through an IDB project which will
concentrate its efforts on the Central Region, providing finance primarily for irrigation,
drainage and economic infrastructure including road rehabilitation. The proposed IDA
project would complement the PNDR in that it focuses on a region of the country not
covered by the PNDR and delegates most decision-making to local institutions. The
proposed project would also support the expansion of successful operations piloted by
bilateral donors and NGOs summarized in Annex 1, if these projects are deemed top
priority by communities or municipalities and are included in municipal plans. The
proposed project would also make use of successful approaches to local government
strengthening supported on a pilot scale by SIDA, GTZ and USAID.
F. BENEFICIARY SUPPORT
4.15  There is evidence of strong support for this proposal. In the workshops
carried out in five departments and 32 municipalities and including NGOs and
community representatives, participants presented a portfolio of un-funded projects or
project ideas of US$31 million, with 38 percent for "green" environmental protection,
26 percent for productive projects of poor farmers, 25 percent for rural infrastructure
and 12 percent for training, research and extension. Past failures in micro-project
implementation were attributed by participants to a lack of trained personnel and to a
lack of adequate financial management, both of which are key constraints to be
addressed by the proposed project.
5. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING PLAN
A. PROJECT COSTS
5.1   The total project cost (excluding the proposed GEF component) is estimated at
US$40.4 million equivalent, including incremental recurrent costs and contingencies
and duties and taxes (US$9.8 million). Base costs are US$38.7 million and
contingencies are US$1.7 million. Project costs are estimated at March 1996 prices.



29
The foreign exchange component would be US$5.9 million. The main expenditure
categories would be goods, works, operating costs (operation and maintenance and
incremental salaries), consultant services, grants for subprojects, training, refunding of
Project Preparation Advance and unallocated. Cost tables are presented in Annex 7.
The costs of the proposed GEF component would be finalized during preparation and
appraisal but are currently estimated at US$10.0 million. GEF funding of US$7.0
million is currently estimated to cover the incremental costs of the project and US$3.0
million would be co-financed by a donor.
B. FINANCING PLAN
5.2   The proposed IDA credit of US$30 million equivalent would finance 74.2
percent of total project costs (excluding taxes and duties) over an implementation
period of 5 years. It would cover 96 percent of foreign costs and 71 percent of local
costs. Local cost financing is justified on account of the high proportion of total costs
accounted for by local expenditures and because of the severe fiscal constraints on
central and municipal governments. At negotiations, assurances were obtained that
US$3.2 million (7.9 percent of the total project costs) would be made available by the
GON over the project period as counterpart funding as follows: $243 million in 1997,
$534 million in 1998, $672 million in 1999, $807 million in 2000 and $943 million in
2001. Other local funding would be met from the following sources: municipal
governments, US$2.8 million (6.8 percent of total project costs); and private
beneficiaries (farmers groups, legally established communities, NGOs), US$4.4 million
(10.9 percent). The complete financing plan is shown in Annex 7.
C. RECURRENT COSTS
5.3   Incremental recurrent costs increase from about US$1.3 million during the first
year of the project to about US$1.7 million during the last year and following years.
Most recurrent costs are associated with the salaries of the staff of the Municipal
Technical Units (US$0.6 million to US$1.0 million per year). The salaries of the
Municipal Technical Units of weaker municipalities (type C) would be financed
through the GON budget until the proposed fiscal transfers to disadvantaged
municipalities become effective. Maintenance and operation cost of subprojects would
be financed through municipal budgets or contributions of beneficiaries. These
incremental costs would be covered by a combination of IDA financing on a declining
basis during the project period, GON counterpart funds, increased local resource
mobilization and the proposed fiscal transfers that are expected to be in place before
the project's end.



30
6. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION AND
MANAGEMENT
A. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION.
Central Government
6.1   Steering Committee. The project's Steering Committee would include the
Minister of Agriculture, representing CONAGRO, the Minister of Finance, the
Minister of MARENA and the President of INIFOM. The Project's Steering
Committee would meet four times a year to: (i) review the project's progress; (ii)
discuss and take action on any development or natural resources issues related to the
project; (iii) coordinate project activities and other donor and GON programs in
natural resources management, rural poverty and rural development; and (iv) support
project implementation activities through its line agencies and representatives at the
regional level, if requested.
6.2   INIFOM. INIFOM would be responsible for the implementation of the Rural
Municipalities Development Component. Its specific responsibilities would include:
*  Managing relations with IDA, CONAGRO and MIFIN; ensuring that
(i) administrative tasks are performed and reporting related to the
project accounts, disbursement applications and audits are carried out;
(ii) legal agreements with implementing municipalities are observed in
the implementation of the component; (iii) adequate counterpart
funding is provided in GON' s budget; (iv) funds are being used for
their intended purposes; (v) agreed procurement procedures are being
followed; (vi) and periodic reports are prepared.
*  Assisting municipalities with the planning and subproject cycle;
overseeing agreements with municipal governments for the execution
of specific subprojects; approving and disbursing against municipal
plans; operating the monitoring and evaluation system; conducting
procurement on behalf of municipal governments; supervising sample
projects and municipal plans; appraising subprojects and/or involving
line agencies or consultants in the appraisal of subprojects beyond the
thresholds; approving subprojects delegating such approval to
municipalities under conditions stipulated in the Operational Manual;
and making decisions regarding increases in the project approval
thresholds of municipal governments.
*  Designing TORs and supervising consultants for the various training
and technical assistance programs under the component, including
assisting municipalities set up the Municipal Technical Units; designing



31
and supervising technical assistance requested by municipalities on
aspects of municipal management; promoting the project with
communities and training community leaders and professionals; pre-
qualifying NGOs and consultants; maintaining updated rosters and
making them available to municipalities; and assisting the
Decentralization Commission of GON. To simplify the technical
assistance tasks, most would be bundled together into a small number
of contracts covering the following three themes: (i) recruitment and
training of MTUs and assistance to municipalities with the financial
control and monitoring and evaluation systems; (ii) promoting the
project and community structures and participatory planning; and (iii)
training of NGOs and professionals and certification of professionals.
Because INIFOM is not a specialized institution it would consult with
GON' s line agencies on aspects related to the training programs and
appraisal of sub-projects beyond the thresholds.
6.3   INIIFOM Project Implementation Unit. To enable INIFOM to perform
those functions, a Project Implementation Unit would be set up in INIFOM in charge
of liaison with IDA and coordinating activities among the various Directorates of
INIFOM involved in project implementation. The INIFOM Project Implementation
Unit would include: the Director of Planning, a Technical Support Group formed by
staff from the Directorates of Projects, Technical Assistance, Financing and
Administration and Regional Delegations of Le6n and San Carlos and other regional
delegations as new regions join the project; and six staff/consultants: implementation
coordinator, planning and technical assistance advisor, participation and gender
advisor, disbursements and accounting officer, procurement officer and internal
auditor and two support staff: secretary and driver. INIFOM's staff working on
project implementation would be located in the various Directorates and would
coordinate project activities through the Technical Support Group.
6.4   MARENA. MARENA would be responsible for the implementation of the
Natural Resources Policy and Institutions Component and the proposed Atlantic
Biodiversity Corridor Component (GEF). Its specific responsibilities would include:
Managing relations with IDA, CONAGRO and MIFIN. This would
involve ensuring that administrative tasks are performed and reporting
related to the project accounts, reimbursement applications and audit. It
would also involve ensuring that agreements, if any, with beneficiary
institutions of the Interinstitutional Assistance Program are being
observed, that adequate counterpart funding is provided in GON's
budget, that funds are being used for their intended purposes, that
agreed procurement procedures are being followed and that periodic
reports are prepared.



32
*  Executing all the activities of the Natural Resources Component
including designing TORs and supervising consultants for the various
training and technical assistance programs, elaborating environmental
and land use plans and involving municipal and regional governments in
the process, disseminating and monitoring them, drafting legislation and
holding public consultations to discuss the proposals, and supervising
studies on natural resources policy matters. To simplify the technical
assistance tasks these would be bundled together into a small number of
contracts covering the following themes: (i) strengthening the
Directorate of Planning and Protected Areas of MARENA; (ii)
environmental and land use planning and monitoring; and (iii) the inter-
institutional assistance program.
  Preparing and implementing the Atlantic Biodiversity Corridor
Component. This would involve finalizing preparation of component
including the ABC strategy in collaboration with municipal and regional
governments, involving communities in preparation activities and
ensuring that all tasks for its implementation are carried out.
6.5   MARENA Project Unit. The MARENA Project Unit would include: the
Director of Planning,; the Director of Protected Areas; and a Technical Support Group
formed by staff from the Directorates of Forestry, Environment and Finance and
Administration. An Implementation Coordinator would be recruited to assist the
Directors of Planning and Protected Areas with reporting requirements and other
matters related to the project. Disbursements, accounting and procurement activities
would be carried out by the relevant Divisions of MARENA under the supervision of
the Component Coordinator.
6.6   Line Agencies. Line agencies such as MAG, INTA, INRA, INAA, and MCT
and special agencies such as FISE and PNDR, would have an important role to play in
assisting with the technical appraisal of sub-projects above the threshold (see Annex 4
and Operational Manual), quality control over and contribution to the training
programs (see Annex 4), and overall coordination of project initiatives with other
activities under their responsibility. The FISE in particular would make available its
computerized subproject monitoring system for adaptation to the proposed project,
rosters of consultants, operational manual and procedures for appraising economic
infrastructure subprojects, and its experience in executing demand driven subprojects
in general. The project would cover incremental travel expenses of staff of these
agencies or the cost of consultants to assist them in appraising projects or in reviewing
or delivering training. The Executive Committee for Public Sector Reform (CERAP)
would assume responsibility for developing the Decentralization Strategy. As a
condition of disbursements: (a) for subprojects over US$50,000 equivalent, INIFOM
would sign (an) agreement(s) with MAG, MCT, MARENA, INRA, INTA, INAA,
PNDR and/or FISE to set forth their respective responsibilities in the revision of



33
subprojects of over US$50,000; and (b) for the preparation of decentralization studies
INIFOM would sign an agreement with CERAP.
Local Government
6.7    Municipal Technical Units (MTUs). The Municipal Technical Units would
be the key project coordinating and implementing structures at the local level. They
would be established or (where they currently exist) strengthened in each participating
municipality to: promote the project, manage the participatory process including
helping communities define needs and identify projects; elaborate municipal plans;
assist communities with recruitment of consultants and NGOs for identification,
preparation, implementation and operation of subprojects in accordance with the
agreed criteria set out in the Operational Manual; appraise subprojects; carry out
procurement tasks on behalf of comrnmunities; supervise subprojects and oversee
agreements with communities; and liaise with INIFOM and other GON agencies.
Each Municipal Technical Unit would report to the Mayor and would include at least
three staff: one professional staff to be nominated Local Project Coordinator (LPC) by
the mayor, subject to no-objection from INIFOM, which would be in charge of all
matters related to the project and which would meet regularly with the Regional
Delegate of INIFOM to review component progress at the municipal level; one
participation specialist responsible for project promotion and conmnunity participation
activities; and one accountant responsible for maintaining project accounts and help
upgrade municipal accounts. These staff would either be municipal staff or would be
specifically recruited for the tasks depending on municipal strength.
6.8    Municipal Council. The Municipal Council, with the assistance of the
Community Supervision Committee and the Municipal Technical Unit, would ratify the
Municipal Investment Plans, approve subprojects included in those plans, ensure that
agreements with INIFOM and communities are being followed and make decisions
regarding unforeseen circumstances affecting subprojects.
Communities16
6.9   Intercommunity Assembly (IA). The Intercommunity Assembly (IA) would
be the key community decision-making structure. It would be formed by
representatives of each comarca and indigenous communities within each municipality
and could include representatives of community organizations such as producers
associations, religious groups, local NGOs, etc. The IA would normally meet three

project implementation. Nevertheless the project would support the legalization of community
structures through (i) the preparation of legislative initiatives; and (ii) technical assistance to
communities in seeking legal status.

times a year to: (i) discuss and define priorities for community needs; (ii) assist with
the elaboration, discuss and make recommendations regarding municipal plans; and
(iii) elect the members of the Community Supervision Committee.
6.10 Community Supervision Committee (CSC). The CSC is a small structure
chosen by the Intercommunity Assembly whose main role would be to ensure that
municipal governments respect community interests during selection of proposals and
project execution. It would: (i) supervise project and subproject execution on behalf
of communities; (ii) issue monitoring reports on local government activities both to
communities and INIFOM; (iii) assist Municipal Councils in making decisions about
municipal plans and subprojects; (iv) ensure that municipal council decisions are fully
disseminated among community members; and (v) keep the Intercommunity Assembly
informed of project execution at the municipal level.
6.11  Project User Group (PUG). The Project User Group would be composed of
those community members with a direct interest in the subproject. The PUG could be
an informal or formal organization such as a local NGO, depending on the subproject.
The PUG would be responsible for providing assistance for or implementing, operating
and maintaining community sub-projects, either directly or through contractors.
Municipal Planning and Subproject Cycles.
6.12 Local Decision-making Mechanisms. A key objective of the proposed
project is to establish a decentralized mechanism for decisions over local investments
for poverty reduction and improved natural resources management. These mechanisms
are summarized in the planning and subproject cycles shown in Diagrams 1 and 2 and
more extensively discussed in Annex 4 and the Operational Manual.
6.13   Restrictions. Eligibility of and restrictions on subprojects are discussed in
Annex 4 and Operational Manual. Among others: (i) no subprojects would be
financed in towns of more than 10,000 people; (ii) 60% of grants in each municipality
for the project period (5 years) would be for subprojects outside the main town/village
in a municipality (cabecera); and (3) subprojects eligible for financing by commercial
lending institutions would not qualify. Subprojects would also have to be consistent
with Environment and Land Use Plans and with the ABC in the Atlantic (Rural
Municipalities Development Component and Atlantic Biodiversity Corridor
Component).
6.14  Municipal eligibility. To be eligible for project financing, municipalities
would have to enter into an agreement with INIFOM (the INIFOM-Municipality
Eligibility and Financing Agreement; sample included in the Operational Manual), in
which they would agree to: (i) follow the rules in the Operational Manual; (ii) create
or strengthen the Municipal Technical Unit; (iii) establish project accounts and bring
financial controls up to IDA standards as per indicators discussed in Annex 10; (iv)
establish and operate a project monitoring and evaluation system; (v) promote the



35
project with communities and bring the level of community participation and public
accountability up to the standards discussed in the Operational Manual and per
indicators of Annex 10; and (vi) develop municipal investment plans respecting
community priorities and the environment and land use plans (ELUP) developed under
the Natural Resources Policies and Institutions Components.

6.15   Subproject cycle. The subproject cycle is summarized in Diagram 2 and
detailed in Annex 4 and in the Operational Manual. Subproject proposals would be
submitted according to guidelines described in the project's training manual (being
developed) and, though simple, would include: (i) subproject background and
description; (ii) description of the executing entity and identification of beneficiaries
and project user group(s); (iii) justification; (iv) expected economic, environmental and
poverty reduction benefits including at least one implementation and one impact
indicator; (v) budget and financial plan; (vi) training and technical assistance plan; and
(vii) operation and maintenance plan. Subproject appraisal would include: (1) a
technical evaluation; (2) an environmental evaluation; (3) an evaluation of poverty
reduction potential; and (4) a recommendation for approval, rejection or redesign.
6.16   Financing Ceilings. Each year there would be a financing ceiling per
municipality calculated on the basis of a formula applied to a total annual budget that
increases from the first to the last year of the project. The formula takes into account
population, rural poverty, size of municipality, participation effort, resource
7  IA - Intercommunity Assembly; MTU - Municipal Technical Unit; CSP - Community
Supervision Comniittee.



36
mobilization effort and fixed costs. The formula and annual allocations by municipality
are detailed in the Operational Manual and in Annex 4.
6.17 Penalties and incentives. Quality control would be ensured more through ex-
post mechanisms than through ex-ante controls. First, those municipalities that are
able to implement participation and accountability practices and receive positive audit
reports after one year of operation will be able to approve subprojects up to
US$50,000. The decision to delegate approval of all subprojects between US$25,000
and US$50,000 to municipalities will be taken by INIFOM subject to non-objection by
IDA and based on information submitted by external financial and technical audits and
by the Community Supervision Committees. Second, a municipality that is considered
non-performing by INIFOM, for example due to dishonest practices or refusal to
follow participatory practices or to target the rural poor, would, subject to IDA's non-
objection, lose access to the IDA financed grants and the municipality's communities
would be informed of the decision.

6.18  Local Cofinancing. All subprojects (with the exception of capacity building
subprojects) would require matching funds from beneficiaries, whether individuals,
legally established communities, cooperatives, producers' associations, NGOs or
municipalities, in accordance with criteria listed in Table 1. Two types of cofinancing
would be allowed: (i) by beneficiaries, which could be in kind or labor; and (ii) by
municipal governments.
6.19   Operational Manual. The eligibility of beneficiaries, the amount of financing
under a grant, the selection, appraisal, approval, monitoring and evaluation procedures
of the grants/subprojects are contained in the Operational Manual and assurances of its
compliance were obtained during negotiations. Adoption of the Manual is required
before disbursements for the Rural Municipalities Development Component.

Technical assistance               100                     100
Note: see Annex 1 for definition of types A, B and C municipalities.
B. POVERTY TARGETING
6.20   The overall project would include four general poverty targeting mechanisms
to ensure that the rural poor are the main project beneficiaries: (i) Subprojects would
be identified, approved or endorsed by community members in the Intercommunity
Assembly, which would ensure that the poor have an opportunity to present their
needs. Since the majority of the population in rural areas is classified as poor, it would
be likely that the needs of the poor would receive higher priority in collective
decisions; and since the opportunity cost of participating in the participatory
structures is likely to be higher for the non-poor than for the poor, it is likely that the
poor would be better represented in these structures than the non-poor. (ii)
Subprojects would be limited to a maximum of US$700 per beneficiary. (iii)
Subproject proposals would have to provide evidence that they benefit the poor; and
community productive subprojects would have to specifically target the poor. (iv)
Municipalities with a higher number of poor people would receive proportionally more
resources.
C. IMPLEMENTATION SCHEDULE
6.21   The proposed project would be implemented over 5 years and is expected to
be completed in 2001. A detailed cronogram of project activities is included in the
project implementation plan in the project file. These are also summarized in the table



38
of implementation indicators in Annex 10. A detailed operational plan for the first
year of the project is in the project file. To minimize implementation complexity the
project would be developed in three phases, the first phase consisting of further
preparation and the second and third phases consisting of implementation.
6.22  First Phase. The first phase (year 0) is being financed through a Project
Preparation Facility (PPF), PHRD Grant and GEF Block B grant and would focus on
capacity building: (i) technical assistance for INIFOM and MARENA including the
establishment of project units; (ii) development of the policy framework for
decentralization and natural resources; (iii) capacity building activities in up to twenty
three municipalities of Le6n and Chinandega; (iv) promotion of the project with
communities, participatory planning on a pilot scale and implementation of two
subprojects per municipality in ten municipalities; (v) training and certification of local
NGOs and consultants to assist municipal governments and communities with the
subproject cycle; (vi) developing municipal environment and land use plans for the
Pacific Region and a regional land use plan and Indigenous Peoples Development Plan
for the Atlantic region; and (vii) delineating the ABC in the Atlantic and complete
preparation of the GEF component.
6.23  Second Phase. The second phase would last about two years (years I and 2)
and would continue the capacity building activities and initiate the investment program
in the twenty three municipalities of Le6n and Chinandega in the Pacific region and
provide technical assistance for the nine municipalities of the Departments of San
Carlos and RAAS in the Atlantic already identified (see Maps).
6.24  Third Phase. During the third phase (years 3 through 5), which would start
after the second year review, the project would continue in Leon and Chinandega and
in the nine municipalities of the southeast and would be extended to any new candidate
municipalities mutually agreed to by the GON and IDA, in particular those of RAAS
and RAAN in the Atlantic. The biodiversity protection activities in the Atlantic, which
are expected to be financed by the GEF grant, would also start in the second phase.
Although a large area, the Atlantic is sparsely populated and thus the proportion of
project funds, other than those assigned to biodiversity protection, channeled to the
Atlantic would be rather small. No subproject would be financed in the Atlantic
region until agreement providing for the financing of the GEF component is effective
and IDA has approved an Indigenous Peoples Development Plan for the Atlantic
region (see conditions of disbursements in paragraph 8.6).
D. PROCUREMENT
6.25  IDA would finance civil works, goods including machinery and vehicles,
technical assistance, consultants and studies, training, grants for subprojects and
incremental operating costs on a declining basis. Procurement arrangements are
summarized in Table 2. MARENA and INIFOM would carry out procurement for
their own institutional strengthening sub-components. During preparation MARENA



39
has been procuring satisfactorily under a Japanese Grant. MARENA and INIFOM
have managed numerous donor projects and MARENA is currently initiating
implementation of an IDB project. Although both institutions already have some skills
on procurement, these would be fuirther enhanced by having staff in both institutions
undergo IDA sponsored procurement training courses. INIFOM would be responsible
for all procurement carried out under subprojects financed by the $23.4 million grant
facility. In most cases, this responsibility for procurement implementation would be
transferred to the individual Municipal Technical Units (MTUs) under the INIFOM-
Municipality Implementation Agreements described below (para. 6.39) which would
specify that procurement would be according to the relevant Bank Guidelines, and as
detailed in the Operational Manual for the project. Staff of Municipal Technical Units
would also receive procurement skills as part of their training.

6.26 Goods and Works other than for Subprojects. IDA's Procurement
Guidelines (January 1996) will govern the procurement of goods and works financed



40
under the project. No ICB is expected under the project. Small works for the
rehabilitation of the offices of INIFOM, MARENA and municipalities are estimated to
cost US$242,000, and may be procured under lump-sum, fixed-price contracts
awarded on the basis of quotations from at least three qualified domestic contractors.
Vehicles and computer equipment will be procured through LIB among suppliers with
established maintenance and service in Nicaragua. Contracts for goods (other than
vehicles and computers) would be procured under NCB. Standard bidding documents
(SBDs) issued by the Borrower and satisfactory to the Bank would be used for all
NCB under the project. Goods estimated to costs less than US$50,000 equivalent per
contract, up to an aggregate amount not to exceed US$70,000 equivalent, of which
the aggregate of goods other than vehicles should not exceed US$40,000 equivalent,
may be procured under contracts awarded on the basis of international shopping
procedures. Contracts for goods valued below US$25,000 up to an aggregate amount
of US$200,000 (US$100,000 for TNIFOM and US$100,000 for MARENA) would be
procured through national shopping based on price quotations from at least three
bidders.
6.27  Goods, Works and Technical Assistance for Subprojects. The average size
of a subproject is expected to be US$10,000. Few subprojects are expected to exceed
US$50,000. Around 500 subprojects (15 per municipality) would be implemented
each year. Each community would receive about one to two subprojects during the
project's five years. Approximately, 30% of these subprojects will require small civil
works, 30% technical assistance and 40% materials and equipment. Where
communities lack the capacity to make the necessary purchases of materials or do not
wish to do so, or for subprojects of municipalities, municipalities would make
purchases packaging the procurement for several projects whenever possible.
6.28  Procurement for works and goods for subprojects would follow: (1) National
Competitive Bidding (NCB) for goods and works over US$25,000; (2) National
Shopping procedures for contracts of goods below US$25,000 up to an aggregate
amount of US$22 million equivalent; (3) lump-sum, fixed-price contracts awarded on
the basis of quotations from at least three qualified domestic contractors, for small
works estimated to cost less than US$25,000 per contract up to an aggregate amount
of US$22 million equivalent; and (4) direct contracting for small works in distant or
scattered rural areas and where competitive proposals cannot be obtained for contracts
estimated to cost less than US$10,000 per subproject, up to an aggregate amount of
US$1 million. Where municipalities are very weak and in the process of being
strengthened the management of procurement activities would be contracted out to a
stronger neighboring municipality, to a private firm or NGO or would be managed by
the Regional Delegation of INIFOM.
6.29  Direct technical assistance to rural communities on all aspects of community
organization and management, training for project operation and maintenance, and
technical assistance for on-farm and off-farm productive activities of the rural poor
would be treated as subprojects. A roster of eligible technical assistance providers



41
would be maintained in INIFOM and in each Municipal Technical Unit. This roster
would be built with assistance from specialized GON agencies and private consultants
and NGOs covering different areas of expertise under criteria acceptable to IDA. It
would include both graduates from the training programs and other eligible
professionals selected on the basis of CV analysis, legal status, past project related
experience and interviews. The community and/or municipality would select a
consultant from this roster, which would be updated quarterly. The Municipal
Technical Units would assist communities with the procedures for selecting local
consultants and/or NGOs.
6.30  Technical assistance, consultants, training and studies would be carried out
according to the Guidelines for the use of Consultants By World Bank Borrowers and
by the World Bank as Executing Agency (August 1981). Technical assistance,
consultants, training and studies under the project would amount to US$ 6.8 million
equivalent excluding those to be provided as separate sub-projects. Consulting
services would consist of: (1) small consultant assignments for individuals and firms
providing direct technical assistance to MARENA, INIFOM and municipalities; and
(2) larger tasks for: (a) recruitment and training of MTUs and assistance to
municipalities with accounting and financial controls and monitoring and evaluation
systems; (b) promotion of project, establishment of community participatory
structures and participatory planning; (c) training and certification of NGOs and
professionals; (d) the inter-institutional assistance program; (e) strengthening of the
Directorate of Planning of MARENA; and (f) environment and land use planning and
monitoring. For small tasks MARENA, INIFOM and municipalities would recruit
individual consultants from a short list of at least three candidates for each post. A
sample letter of invitation, award criteria and a sample contract, all acceptable to IDA,
would be included in the Draft Operational Manual. For technical assistance at the
municipal level in the fields of - inter alia - community organization and management,
subproject preparation, and maintenance for on-farm and off-farrm activities of the
rural poor, INIFOM would: (1) on the basis of minimum criteria acceptable to IDA,
establish a roster of organizations with appropriate capabilities; (2) select at least
three organizations included in the roster to be invited to present technical and
financial proposals for the services; and (3) award contracts on the basis of criteria
acceptable to IDA.
6.31  Prior and Post Review Procedures. Although the level of IDA prior review
of procurement would be low (25%), it would be compensated in several ways. First,
the project physical performance evaluation would analyze procurement issues.
Second, financial audits would include procurement aspects. Third, INIFOM's
Regional Delegations would assign a procurement coordinator in charge of helping
communities and municipalities conform to procurement rules. Fourth, both
MARENA and INIFOM and the MTUs would receive training on procurement. And
finally, during IDA supervision, random reviews would be conducted of subprojects,
including field visits and review of subproject documents.



42
6.32 IDA would conduct a prior review of: (1) all LIB contracts; (2) the first two
NCB contracts for goods and works (other than for subprojects); (3) the first contract
each for goods and works for Subprojects procured under NCB; and (4) consultant
selection documentation (terms of reference, short lists, contract, etc.) for consultants
recruited by INIFOM and MARENA with an estimated contract amount of
US$100,000 or more for firms and US$30,000 or more for individuals; (5) terms of
reference for all other consulting services contracts; and (6) all contracts based on a
single source contracting or of a critical nature.
6.33   During negotiations assurances were obtained from the Government of
Nicaragua that the above procurement arrangements would be followed.

6.34 Post-review would be carried out for other procurement by implementing
agencies (INIFOM and MARENA) and municipalities. For this purpose, both
MARENA and INIFOM would recruit auditors, under terms of reference satisfactory
to IDA, who would not only examine financial implementation, but also procurement
procedures, physical implementation and impact on beneficiaries. These auditors
would carry out annual audits on the basis of a sample not lower than 60 percent of
the IDA financed investments in the first project year, 50 percent in the second project
year and 40 percent in later years. For convenience and to avoid overlaps, the same
auditors would carry out an ex-post review of the expenditures and investments
financed by IDA.
E. DISBURSEMENTS
6.35   The project has a 5-year disbursement period and the Closing Date would be
March 31, 2002. (The associated ABC project would have also a 5-year
disbursement period and the Closing Date would be March 31, 2003). Agreed project
activities begun after February 28, 1996 and would be eligible for retroactive financing



43
up to a maximum of USSDR694,000. The estimated disbursement schedule has been
determined by the estimated availability of counterpart funding (see Table in Annex 7,
page 13), and the implementation capacity of the implementing agencies.
6.36  The Credit would be disbursed against eligible project expenditures at the rates
of: (i) 85% for civil works; (ii) 100% for foreign supplied and 80% of locally supplied
or manufactured machinery, equipment, vehicles, and furniture; (iii) 100% for
consultant services, training and studies; (iv) 100% of the amount of the grants for
subprojects (excluding all taxes); and (v) incremental recurrent costs on a declining
basis (80% first two years, 60% third and fourth years and 40% thereafter; see Table
in Annex 7, page 12). A PPF advance of SDR 1,050,000 has been approved and it
will be refinanced upon credit effectiveness.
6.37  Documentation of Expenditures. Disbursements would be made on the basis
of full documentation for all expenditures made under contracts requiring prior review
by IDA and amendments to contracts for consultant services raising the value of such
contracts above the prior review limits. These contracts are: (i) all LIB contracts for
vehicles; (ii) the first two NCB contracts for goods; and (iii) consultant services
contracts directly procured by MARENA and INIFOM with an estimated contract
amount of US$100,000 or more for firms and US$30,000 or more for individuals. For
all other contracts and expenditures, including training expenditures (tuition, travel
subsistence), disbursements would be made against Statements of Expenditure (SOE)
for which supporting documents would be maintained by MARENA, INIFOM and
Municipal Technical Units and would be available to external auditors and to IDA for
staff review.
6.38  Special Accounts. Two Special Accounts in US dollars would be opened in a
commercial bank acceptable to IDA. Special Account A would be managed by
INIFOM and Special Account B would be managed by MARENA. The initial
deposits in the SA-A will be US$1.7 million and in Special Account B US$400,000
corresponding to about four months of expenditures under the project for each
agency. An initial deposit of up to US$1,000,000 for SA-A and US$200,000 for SA-
B may be requested. Once disbursements have reached SDR2,776,000 for SA-A and
SDR694,000 for SA-B, the balance of the authorized allocation can be requested up to
the authorized limit. The SAs would be replenished monthly on the Borrower's
request (but no less frequently than every three months), or whenever one third of the
authorized amount has been withdrawn, whichever occurs first.
6.39 Flow of Funds to Municipalities. IDA Credit funds would be transferred by
INIFOM on behalf of the Government to municipalities for the financing of
subprojects under INIFOM-Municipality Implementation Agreements to be entered
into. Each municipality would open a bank account. INIFOM would transfer funds
(the grants) monthly from the Special Account "A" to each municipality account
under the provisions of the implementation agreements and based on the financial plan
included in the INIFOM-Municipality Agreement reflecting the investment plan



44
approved by the municipality and agreed to by INIFOM. Initial deposits equal to 20
percent of financial requirements would follow immediately after approval of MIPs
and signing of the agreements to enable municipalities and beneficiaries to cover
project preparation costs. Payments from each municipal account to contractors
would be made on the basis of payment authorizations signed by the mayor and the
MTU (local project coordinator). Funds required for central procurement would be
retained by INIFOM in its Special Account. When municipalities would transfer funds
to beneficiaries legally established, communities, NGOs, etc., for communal productive
activities, INIFOM-Municipality-beneficiary agreements would be signed namely for
transfer of ownership and subsequent maintenance of the goods and works financed.
6.40  Documentation. INIFOM and MARENA would be responsible for
submitting regular replenishment requests with appropriate supporting documents for
expenditures under the project. The documents would include: (i) a standard
withdrawal application (Form 1903) for the total amount of eligible project
expenditures (but not the amount advanced to municipalities) to be replenished into
INIFOM's Special Account with a copy of the monthly bank statement for that
account; (ii) SOE form which will provide the summary of category expenditures
(INIFOM and MARENA) including grants for subprojects (INIFOM); (iii) standard
summary sheets (designed for each subproject and included in the disbursement letter)
and supporting documentation for all expenditures above the procurement prior
review thresholds; and (iv) a reconciliation statement for the special account. The use
of grants by municipalities and communities would be monitored through auditing
procedures, the monitoring systems and project and sub-project supervision
arrangements.
F.  FINANCIAL CONTROLS, AccouNTs AND AUDITNG
6.41  Financial Management Capacity. A diagnostic by professional accountants
of the financial capacity of the two implementing agencies (INIFOM and MARENA)
and of the thirty-two municipalities expected to participate in the project showed that
only MARENA, INIFOM and the two largest municipalities would be able to meet
IDA's recommended project accounting procedures. While all municipalities had a
basic cash accounting system that adequately recorded income and expenditure,
primary deficiencies found were: (i) lack of financial information on programs/projects
and no records of municipal assets; (ii) weaknesses in internal control; and (iii) lack
of adequately trained staff. INIFOM also had weak areas, particularly in internal
control, which are being addressed by the Comptroller's Office as part of the
Institutional Restructuring Agreement supported by the Institutional Development
Credit.
6.42 Technical assistance will be provided to each municipality to help implement
new accounting systems and procedures. INIFOM will also prepare revised standard
accounting procedures for municipalities in coordination with the Ministry of Finance
which is implementing the Institutional Development Credit. During project



45
implementation, additional assistance will be provided to weaker municipalities to
further strengthen financial management, particularly in the areas of budgeting and
cash management, so they can effectively manage the increased fiscal transfers
expected by the end of the project period.
6.43   Project Financial Statements and Financial Reporting. Project financial
statements to be prepared by each participating municipality would include a statement
of sources and uses of funds, and a register of project assets or balance sheet where
appropriate. The funds flow statement would indicate sources (IDA, as well as
counterpart) and expenditures in accordance with main project components and
disbursement categories. Where municipalities have multiple subprojects, data would
be shown separately for each subproject to facilitate project monitoring and
preparation of project monitoring indicators. Project financial statements would show
actual and pending payments against budgeted. Information on sources and uses of
funds would be provided monthly. Information reported would also include the value
of contracts signed, i.e. commitments, relative to actual and pending payments.
6.44 The implementing agencies (INIFOM and MARENA) would maintain separate
records and accounts for project expenditures as well as a register of assets purchased
with project funds. They would also have the responsibility for preparing the project's
financial statements, including sources and uses of funds statements, according to
internationally accepted accounting standards. INIFOM would include balance sheets
in the financial statements. MARENA and INIFOM would also receive technical
assistance to help establish accounting procedures acceptable to IDA. INIFOM
would prepare financial statements for its own managed funds as well as consolidated
project financial statements based on the project financial statements submitted by each
municipality.
6.45   Auditing. A process for selection of auditors and the auditor's TOR were
agreed with INIFOM and MARENA during appraisal. The selection process includes
pre-qualifying audit firms, contracting auditors for one year with a provision to extend
for a further two years based on satisfactory performance, and initiating the process
for selection of auditors during project preparation with the objective of having
auditors in place by the start of loan disbursements. Both INIFOM and MARENA
would contract audit firms to audit the consolidated financial statements for the
components of the project.
6.46  The auditors report would include audits of the special account, an opinion on
the use of statement of expenditures (SOEs), confirmation that project
implementation was in accordance with provisions of the legal agreement and
verification of procurement transactions. The auditor's TORs also include a review of
internal controls and preparation of a management letter. Audit reports would be
submitted to IDA up to within six months of the close of the fiscal year. The first
audit reports would cover the first year's disbursement as well as disbursements under
the Japanese grant under separate TORs. Most project funds are expected to be



46
disbursed for a large number of small subprojects averaging about US$10,000 in at
least 32 municipalities, some of difficult access. It is expected that the external
auditors to a large extent will base their work on that of the internal auditors and will
exercise good judgment in selecting municipalities for direct audit to reduce the cost of
the audit.
6.47 Technical audits would be carried out separately. Technical audits would
consist of simple checks of subprojects ensuring that what is purchased is in fact there
and would exclude any assessment of whether resources were used efficiently or
appropriately or any technical issues.
G. SUPERVISION, MONITORING AND EVALUATION
6.48  Supervision. Day-to-day supervision of the project would be carried out by
MARENA and INIFOM. IDA and GON would monitor the project's overall progress
during joint supervision missions, annual reviews and an Implementation Completion
Review.
6.49 Because of the large number of sub-projects which would be financed under
the Rural Municipalities Development Component, the demands for close supervision
would be high, and the financing plan includes resources for INIFOM to enable it to
meet them. There would be three supervision mechanisms for activities carried out by
municipal governments including subprojects: (i) by beneficiaries: the Community
Supervision Committee (CSC) would supervise implementation of municipal plans
and agreed projects and issue execution reports for INIFOM and the Intercommunity
Assembly; (ii) by municipalities: the Municipal Technical Units would supervise
subprojects executed by contractors and/or communities while Project User Groups
would supervise contractors implementing sub-projects on behalf of communities; and
(iii) by INIFOM: the Regional Delegation of INIFOM would also supervise
municipality and beneficiary compliance with terms of agreements with INIFOM and
municipal plans and subproject implementation.
6.50 Monitoring. Both MARENA and INIFOM would monitor overall project
progress and provide quarterly reports (March 31, June 30, September 30 and
December 31) to the Minister of Finance, CONAGRO and IDA. An outline for
quarterly reports is included in Annex 10. Progress would be measured against the
implementation indicators included in Tables A10. 1 to Al0.6 and Figure 10.1, Annex
10. The reports would be furnished to IDA no more than one month after the
completion of each quarter.
6.51  Project Launch Workshops. Upon project effectiveness, GON would
organize four project launch workshops targeted at: (i) MARENA and INIFOM; (ii)
Municipalities; (iii) Community leaders and local NGO representatives; and (iv) other
GON agencies supporting the project. Details are given in Annex 11.



47
6.52 Regular Supervision. Several times a year GON and IDA would organize
joint project supervision missions. Project supervision would provide implementation
assistance. Before each mission, GON and IDA would agree on the major subjects to
be supervised, which would always include an evaluation of the project's progress
based on the monitoring indicators, compliance with legal covenants, and procurement
and financial management supervision. Immediately after each mission, IDA would
send an Aide Memoire to GON which would contain the major agreements reached
during the mission and recommendations. Annex 11 provides more details on
supervision.
6.53 Annual Reviews. Each year, GON and IDA would carry out annual reviews.
Before such a review would take place GON and IDA would agree on detailed TORs
and staffing of the review teams. In particular the reviews would analyze: (i) status of
community participation and the role of municipalities in promoting it; (ii) status of
technical assistance and training program for municipal governments; (iii) status of
municipal planning; (iv) status of training of consultants and local availability of
trained consultants; (v) functioning of all aspects of the subproject cycle; (vi) status
of environmental planning and monitoring system; (vii) advances in the legal
proposals; (viii) status of the interinstitutional training program; (ix) status of
preparation and implementation when enacted of mechanism of fiscal transfers to local
governments; and (x) procurement, disbursements and financial aspects. Annual
reviews would also analyze the status of development and implementation of (i) the
legal framework for decentralization and natural resources; (ii) the Atlantic
Biodiversity Corridor strategy (see Annex 4 and para. 3.9 and 3.11); and (iii) the legal
framework to ensure the effective application of the rights of indigenous peoples as
expressed in the Constitution and Autonomy Law of 1987. During negotiations
assurances were obtained that these annual reviews would be carried out and that
GON would take remedial measures.
6.54  Implementation Completion Report. Not later than six months after the
project closing date, GON and IDA would carry out a joint project completion review
and produce an Implementation Review Report. Following this review, GON would
present to IDA a plan for the future operation of the project, furnish the plan to IDA
for comments, and carry out the plan taking into account IDA's comments thereon.
7. EXPECTED BENEFITS AND RISKS
A.  SOCIAL IPACT
7.1   Target Population. The project is designed with the capacity to reach, directly
or indirectly, the rural population of the 32 selected municipalities. This coverage
would expand in the second phase with the inclusion of additional areas. The rural
population of the 32 municipalities was estimated in 1993 to be about 477,000. Of



48
these, it is expected that the direct beneficiaries would be around 300,000 people, or
around 50,000 families, mainly small farmers and fishermen. Project investments per
capita per year considering the entire population of the 32 municipalities would
amount to about US$5. This level of expenditure is comparable to that seen in similar
Bank projects in Mexico -- about US$8 per capita per year -- and Colombia -- about
US$6 per year for equivalent definitions of target populations. Since the project
would establish a new system for financing and implementing sub-projects at the
municipal level, it is expected that in the longer term a much larger number of people
would benefit directly as further financing, namely from the proposed fiscal transfers
legislation, enters the system.
7.2   The rural poor. Some 40,000 households, over 70% of the region's rural
population, are below the poverty line. More than 60% are small farmers. On average
it would be necessary to invest between US$ 750 and US$ 4,000 per family to
increase a small farm income enough to pull the household out of poverty. The
expected US$6.7 million of productive subprojects would thus allow between 1,600
and 10,000 of such families to become non-poor (25% of the region's rural poor if the
higher figure is achieved). Comparatively, in 1993 only 720 poor farmers in the region
received some kind of assistance (averaging US$560). The proposed project would
increase the number of beneficiaries between 2.5 and 13 times. The rural poor would
also benefit from the non-productive subprojects: environment, public infrastructure
and capacity building (see Annex 12 for details).
7.3   Women. Because of the different roles that men and women play in society,
the project would potentially benefit men and women differently (see Annex 3 for a
more detailed discussion of gender aspects). First, although the project supports both
men's and women's organizations, the project would specifically promote women's
involvement in local and municipal governments and this involvement would be
maintained (see performance indicators in Annex 10). Second, the project would
attempt to diminish discrepancies in access to information and resources that typically
exist between men and women. Third, the project would support both men's and
women distinctive productive activities through the municipal funds (these would
include the basic grains produced by both men and women as well as agro-industries
and food processing, fruit and vegetable production, apiculture, spices and medicinal
plants, small livestock, and forest-based artesenal activities in the case of women).
Finally, the project would finance natural resource subprojects according to the
priorities of men, which tend to have both a commercial orientation, and women,
which have a commercial and social focus (for example, domestic water and energy
subprojects). A gender advisor would be placed in INIFOM to ensure that gender
concerns are present during implementation of the municipal development component.
7.4   Indigenous peoples. A Social Assessment for the Sutiaba in Le6n and
Chinandega was produced during preparation of the IDA components while an
Indigenous Peoples Development Plan for the Atlantic region is well under



49
preparation.'9 Both the IDA and the proposed GEF components would benefit
indigenous peoples. First, indigenous communities would be able to submit
subproject proposals identified by them to local governments and would participate in
the decisions over local planning and subproject approval through their traditional
representative structures. Second, through subprojects indigenous beneficiary
communities would be eligible for technical assistance (including legal) for community
development including that required for the elaboration of community land titling
proposals. Third, indigenous communities would be the main executors of the
proposed GEF activities where the biodiversity corridor overlaps with their territory
through quid pro quo agreements under which communities would receive
development assistance prioritized by them in exchange for commitments to conserve
biodiversity.
7.5   Institutions. Institutional beneficiaries at the national level would be
MARENA and INJEOM, the legal offices of the Executive Branch, the Legislature,
the Judiciary and enforcing agencies. The increase in their capacity to legislate and to
enforce regulations governing natural resources and the environment would be
indirectly beneficial to all of Nicaraguan society.
7.6    At the municipal level, the most important benefit would be the legacy of
much-improved capacity among local groups and municipal government to identify,
prepare, evaluate and implement subprojects and in particular of much improved
capacity of communities of rural poor to voice their demands and of local governments
to meet them. This would be a valuable long-term asset.
B. ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT
7.7    The project would contribute to the improved management of biodiversity
forests, soils, water and fisheries. In total it is estimated that in the Pacific 25,000
hectares or 13% of current crop area would benefit from less erosion while 3% of
regional mangrove area would receive direct protection (see Annex 12). Additionally
the project would help protect the high biodiversity of volcanic ecosystems, improve
waterflow controls and help stabilize some watercourses. The GEF Component would
specifically support biodiversity conservation in the Atlantic. Because some of the
subprojects, particularly public infrastructure subprojects, may pose modest
environmental risks, the project is rated as a "B". There are two mechanisms for
screening out risky subprojects (see Annexes 4, 6 and Operational Manual): (i)
subproject approval would have to follow the guidelines detailed in Annexes 4 and 6,
in the Operational Manual and in the background study "Sistema de Procedimientos
19 See background reports in project file: "Social Assessment and Indigenous Development Plan:
Consultation, Participation Strategies and Communication Support", "Evaluaci6n Social de la
Comunidad Sutiaba in Le6n ", " Viabilidady Condiciones Legales de Cumplimiento de un
Componente de Legalizaci6n de Tierra a las Com unidades Indigenas de la Costa Atlantica
Dentro del Proyecto de Manejo de Recursos Naturales - IPDP First Report and Final Report.



50
Ambientalespara la Gesti6n AmbientalMunicipat'; and (ii) subprojects would have
to be consistent with priorities and restrictions reflected in the Environment and Land
Use Plans for the Pacific and Atlantic and with the ABC strategy. Additionally no
disbursements for subprojects would take place until the ABC component (GEF) is
declared effective, and until an Indigenous Peoples Development Plan for the Atlantic
is approved by IDA.
C. ECONOMIC AND FISCAL IMPACTS
7.8   Economic Efficiency. There are three dimensions of economic impacts from
the proposed project: (i) from decentralization; (ii) from natural resource and
environmental policy reform; and (iii) from subprojects.
7.9   The expected economic impacts from decentralization are primarily three: (i)
reduction in the costs of delivering public services relative to the costs of these
services if they were managed centrally; (ii) a better optimum (more efficient mix) of
local investments relative to the mix accruing if decisions were taken by a centralized
agency; and (iii) better community structures.
7.10  The expected economic impacts from natural resource and environmental
policy reform are primarily three: (i) economic efficiency gains from eliminating
conflicting regulations on natural resource use; (ii) reduction in the cost of governing
due to increased government credibility from enforcing a clearer set of regulations than
under the without project scenario; and (iii) better internalization of environmental
externalities by the public, private and NGO sectors.
7.11   The expected economic impacts from subprojects are: (i) reduction in the
costs of municipalities and communities in identifying, preparing, implementing and
operating subprojects; (ii) improved decision-making by private farmers and other
rural poor from the capacity building projects; (iii) economic gains from enhanced
environmental protection; and (iv) economic surpluses from economic infrastructure
and higher productivity from on-farm and off-farm economic subsistence activities to
be supported by the project.
7.12  Evaluation of Subprojects. The economic analysis focused only on a subset
of these benefits: the economic impacts of the expected portfolio of environmental,
infrastructure and productive subprojects. These estimates are fraught with
uncertainties and are presented to illustrate orders of magnitude of expected benefits.
Because subprojects are demand driven and it is not known ex-ante what community
and municipality choices would be, two estimates of sub-project demand were
developed by polling municipal governrments, NGOs and beneficiaries directly. These
estimates are summarized in Annexes 2 and 3.
7.13   Accounting for only the expected benefits from a selected number of expected
subprojects (municipal investments), but accounting for all the project investment and



51
recurrent costs, yielded a benefit/cost ratio of 1.04, and a net present value of US$2.3
million (10% discount rate, Table 4). Hence, given that unaccounted benefits are
expected to be large, the project is likely to be highly economically viable.
7.14 Evaluation of Decentralization and Natural Resources Policy and
Institutional Reform Benefits. Although the costs and benefits from decentralization
were not quantified, other Bank and IDA projects confirm that decentralization
typically leads to a more efficient portfolio of investments, which are more likely to be
maintained and sustained and implemented, at a lower cost than when these
investments are channeled through centralized structures (see Section 4.D on Lessons
from Past Bank Lending). Because these other non-quantified benefits may be equal
to or more important than subproject benefits, and because quantifying those benefits
would help maintain GONs commitment to the project, a study of all the economic
impacts of the project, including those arising from decentralization and policy and
institutional reform would be conducted during the first year of the project.
