7.15   Public Sector Role. There is also the question of whether the financing of
proposed categories of investments is better handled by the local public sector or by
the private sector (all would be executed by the private sector). For the subproject
categories of environment, public infrastructure and capacity building the rationale for
public sector involvement is clear since these entail substantial externalities and/or are
public goods. The issue is not as clear cut for collective private projects. The principle
underlying the public financing of collective private investments is that the initial
subsidies can help poor people raise their standards of living above the subsistence
level and help initiate a process of capital accumulation among the rural poor that
ultimately would help pull them out of poverty. If decisions to finance projects of
disadvantaged communities were left to the private sector it would be very unlikely
that the poorest communities would initially be able to attract private financing
(though once the profitability of investments and community management capacity is
demonstrated this may not remain a constraint). Hence public sector support for
collective private projects can be justified as an instrument for poverty alleviation.
There are precedents for this, for example in the Bank projects in Northeast Brazil and



52
in DRD I and II in Mexico which financed similar categories of investments for
disadvantaged communities.
7.16  Fiscal impact. Fiscal impacts refer to the benefits foregone due to the
allocation of counterpart funds for project activities. These are significant only for the
Municipal Development Component. These counterpart funds (US $4.8 million for
GON and municipalities) would be primarilv provided by municipalities from own
revenues or from resources transferred from central government. Municipal co-
financing of subprojects is likely to result in: (i) a smaller percentage of municipal
investments targeted to urban areas (but not necessarily a decline in absolute terms);
(ii) a higher percentage of municipal funds allocated to environment and rural public
infrastructure at the expense of more traditional municipal investments (urban parks,
recreation facilities, urban street asphalting, etc.); and (iii) a decline in local programs
managed centrally (by MAS, INIFOM, Institute of Sports and National Assembly), but
an increase in the efficiency of fiscal resources used in the process. Additional fiscal
resources from GON (US$1.2 million) to support activities being directly implemented
by MARENA and INIFOM would also be needed. These would be justified since (i)
MARENA has already undergone drastic cuts in personnel and needs now to be
strengthened to be able to execute its priority functions in policy-making and
enforcement; and (ii) INIFOM has a key role to play in the short and medium future in
support of the process of fiscal and administrative decentralization.
D. SUSTAINABILITY
7.17  There are four dimensions of sustainability: (i) sustainability of the local
institutional mechanisms, and in particular of whether communities will be able to
continue defining their priorities and causing municipal governments to respond to
them; (ii) sustainability of financial resources for disadvantaged rural municipalities,
which would allow these to meet community demands; (iii) sustainability of
subprojects, that is, their maintenance and replicability; and (iv) sustainability of
mechanisms for intemalizing environment values in public decision-making. There are
also important sustainability issues related to the proposed GEF component.
7.18   Sustainability of local institutional mechanisms --would be promoted by (i)
demonstrating through real experiences and training that both communities and local
government officials and politicians gain from the adoption of participatory methods;
communities gain a better portfolio of projects and local politicians gain a more
satisfied electorate; (ii) making resources available to disadvantaged municipalities
both through the project and through the proposed fiscal transfers legislation to make
it worthwhile for communities to organize themselves in order to cause local
governments to channel those resources to meet community priorities; (iii) making full
information about the project available to communities through project promotion; and
(iv) providing initial support to community and municipality decision mechanisms
(Intercommunity Assembly and Community Supervision Committee) that have low
operational costs and thus are likely to be maintained once their usefulness is proven.



53
7.19  Sustainability of rinancial resources for disadvantaged municipalities--
would be secured through: (i) the proposed fiscal transfers legislation which, in a
budget neutral manner, would, if enacted, put in place a financing mechanism for local
governments equivalent to at least 4% of the GON revenues and which would be
transferred as block grants to those municipalities with greatest need according to the
formula similar to the one discussed in Annex 4; and (ii) technical assistance through
the project to disadvantaged municipalities on local resource mobilization.
7.20 Sustainability of community and municipality investments -- maintenance
and replicability -- would be met through five mechanisms: (i) the proposed fiscal
transfers from the central govermnent would, if enacted, put more resources at the
disposal of municipal governments which could be used to meet recurrent and
operational costs; (ii) the participatory process would ensure that those projects of
highest priority to communities would be financed and these communities would thus
have an interest in ensuring subproject sustainability; (iii) an acceptable operational and
maintenance plan, including definition of responsibilities for these activities, would be
a criteria for subproject approval; (iv) the project would support training to municipal
governments, communities and professionals on subproject operation and maintenance
and cost recovery mechanisms; and (v) in the particular case of productive
subprojects, cost recovery into beneficiary (association, cooperative, legally
established community) managed funds would be promoted as a means to generate
financial resources to replicate the subproject and to reach the largest number of
beneficiaries within a community.
7.21   Sustainability of mechanisms for internalizing environmental values in
public decision-making -- would be ensured through: (i) an active training program
including training of trainers and curricula development on environmental policies
targeted at key institutions with responsibilities over environment policy; (ii)
strengthening the monitoring and watchdog function of MARENA over other GON
agencies and regional and local governments; and (iii) streamlining of MARENA
through the Institutional Restructuring Agreement to be supported by the Institutional
Development Credit.
7.22  Sustainability of conservation activities (GEF) in the Atlantic -- would be
secured through (i) a financial mechanism currently being designed that would raise
additional resources from donors and local sources and would finance the recurrent
costs of conservation activities in the Atlantic and investment subprojects beyond the
closing date of the GEF component; and (ii) information and training for indigenous
and non-indigenous conmnunities and demarcation of territories of indigenous
communities which have a stake in ensuring the sustainability of natural resources in
the Atlantic region.



54
E. RISKS
7.23  There are five main risks in the implementation of the project, as follows:
7.24 The first relates to project complexity and to the capacity of INIFOM and
MARENA to implement the components under their responsibility. This risk would be
addressed by (i) establishing fully staffed Component Implementation Units in each
institution that would manage the INIFOM component and the MARENA
components, respectively, as two separate, but interlinked projects; (ii) processing the
GEF component as an associated, but separate operation with different timing,
documentation, legal agreements and supervision; (iii) grouping most technical
assistance activities in six technical assistance contracts, three to be managed by
INIFOM and another three by MARENA; (iv) phasing in project activities focusing
the first phase on capacity building, the second phase on preliminary investments and
the third phase on expanding the investment program and initiating activities under the
GEF component.
7.25  The second relates to the capacity of municipalities and beneficiaries to
generate high quality rural subproject proposals and implement them satisfactorily
without leakage of funds. These issues and how the project would deal with them are
detailed in Annex 4. This risk would be addressed as follows: (i) as a condition of
municipal participation, local governments would establish a Municipal Technical Unit
with at least two staff with expertise on participation and the subproject cycle and who
would undergo training and receive technical assistance; (ii) all municipalities would
establish project accounts following IDA standards, the project would provide
technical assistance for improving overall municipal financial controls as per agreed
indicators, and the component intemal auditor in INFOM would systematically carry
out audits of INIFOM and municipalities; (iii) the project would invest heavily in
training local professionals (local private consultants, NGOs and local government and
GON staff) in all aspects of the subproject cycle; these professionals would be the
main vehicle for ensuring the quality of subprojects; (iv) specialized line agencies
would be requested to issue a technical opinion for subprojects greater than
US$50,000; (v) both external and internal audits assisted by a social supervision
mechanism through the communities are included in project design; and (vi)
municipalities that misuse project funds would be penalized according to agreed
procedures (para. 6.17).
7.26  The third risk is that the government might weaken its efforts to reform natural
resource and indigenous peoples policies and develop and implement a coherent
decentralization strategy. Some GON agencies in particular may feel threatened by
decentralization. An associated risk is that the legislation on fiscal decentralization
would not be approved. This risk would not impact the project directly but would
make it less likely that it would be sustainable. These risks cannot be mitigated totally,
but efforts would be made under the project to carry out analytical studies of the costs



55
and benefits of reform and decentralization and to conduct study tours to similar
projects in LAC to show GON the economic benefits from these strategies.
7.27 The fourth risk, also related to government commitment, concerns the level of
priority assigned to this project and the consequent security of counterpart funds. All
current evidence suggests a strong political commitment on account of: (i) the urgency
of rural poverty alleviation; (ii) the immediacy of the combatant and refugee
resettlement problem; (iii) the growing pressure from many sources to reverse the
process of resource degradation; (iv) political pressure from local governments which
have been able to push the reform agenda in their direction, for example in the recent
reform to the Constitution; and (v) the advances in proposing legislation on fiscal
decentralization. Moreover, the level of counterpart funding is covenanted in the
project's legal agreement.
7.28  Finally, there is a possible risk of competition with other donor agencies, due
to the rather poor record of donor co-ordination. As far as can be ascertained, the
proposed project would be complementary to other donor efforts. Moreover, this risk
has been minimized through consultations with GON, donor agencies and the IDB,
and IDA supervision missions would continue placing a high priority on coordination
with other agencies active in the natural resources and rural development field and in
decentralization.

NICARAGUA
RURAL MUNICIPALITIES PROJECT
ANNEX 1
BACKGROUND: THE POLICY AND INSTITUTIONAL CONTEXT FOR
DECENTRALIZATION "
1. RATIONALE FOR DECENTRALIZATION
Rationale for Decentralization. Decentralization of decision-making and
responsibility to the local level is considered an imnportant element in improving rural
development programs and natural resource management; a perception shared by
government, NGOs and foreign donors. This derives from the extreme weakness in the
mechanisms which exist to ensure that public investments are efficient and consistent with
rural development and natural resource management potentials at the local level. Where
contemplated, municipal-level priorities and opportunities have been limited to diagnostics
and consultations usually carried out by central GON agencies or outside programs rather
than through ongoing dialogue and shared responsibility. Thus, the distribution of
resources and investment activities heavily reflect central government priorities.'2 In
contrast, the principle underlying decentralization is that national priorities be satisfied
through incentives and broad thematic and zonal targeting of investmnent resources and
local mechanisms be preferred for their specific disposition. Local governments are closer
to the people and problems that result from poverty and often also from resource
degradation and hence internalize those costs better and more rationally prioritize the use
of scarce investment resources. In the limited instances where "local choice" mechanisms
have been functioning in Nicaragua the results are generally positive. The GTZ/INIFOM
Proyecto de Fortalacimiento de la Autonomia Municipal (FAMU) is one exarnple, and
the SIDA/INIFOM development programs for infrastructure and community works in
pern-urban poverty zones is another.!3- There is also an expanding body of evidence
elsewhere on the advantages of decentralized rural development solutions, several of which
are being supported by the World Bank in LAC, for example in Bolivia, Brazil, Colombia
and Mexico.
I/    For a more complete treatment of the subject, see Project Working Papers: (3)
"Capacidad de Las Municipalidades "; (4) "Capacidad de Las Organizaciones No
Gubernamentales "; (25) "Fondos Municipales Para El Manejo y Conservacidn de Los Recursos
Naturales"; (16) "Programa de Generaci6n y Transferencia de Tecnologia Para el Manejo
Sostenible de Recursos Naturales"; (28) "Reglamento Interno - Proyecto PROTIERRA; (18)
"Demanda de Microproyectos "; 'Asignaci6n de Recursos Para Inversirn Municipales",
'Municipal Funds", 'Municipal Finances and Decentralization"
2'    Implementation capacity, in terms of NGO and government's human resources also
shows a "lumpy" distribution in response to donor financing. (Abaunza, B.M., 1993. Las
Organizaciones No Gubernamentales En La Generaci6n y Transferencia de Tecnologia, pg. 12).
3'     The immaturity of existing examples makes impossible useful comparisons of the
efficiency of centralized vs decentralized models in Nicaragua, however, neither is it clear that
such analysis will be pertinent. Given that macroeconomic and public sector restructuring
policies demand a diminished role for central government the creation, implementation and
strengthening of decentralized mechanisms is unavoidable.



Annex 1
Background: The Policy and Institutional
Context for Decentralization
Page 2 of 17
2. THE LEGAL FRAMEWORK
Local Government. The legal framework of municipal governments is
included in the Constitution, the Electoral Law, the Municipal Law, the Law of Political
Administrative Division and the regulations on the Organization and Operation of
Municipalities, approved by executive decree. The foundation for local government has
been until now the Municipal Law of 1988 (Ley de Municipios - Ley No. 40). This law
authorizes local government to provide services and exercise administrative authority over:
(1) environmental protection; (2) regulation of urban development and land use; (3) solid
waste management; (4) reforestation; (5) oversight of natural resource use and protection;
and (6) creation of mechanisms for popular participation in local decision-making. Several
larger and/or better funded municipal governments (e.g., Leon, Chinandega, El Castillo)
have begun to exercise these powers through the establishment of municipal environmental
offices, employing technical specialists, and creating environmental and natural resource
advisory committees. In a number of foreign donor-assisted projects as well, municipal
governments are beginning to function in their expanded roles.'4
The 1995 Partial Reform of the Constitution (Reforma Parcial de la
Constituci6n) brought important implications for local government. Key aspects of the
constitutional reform include: (1) regions were eliminated as administrative units with the
exception of the RAAS and RAAN; (2) municipal govermments were given broad
responsibilities over socio-economic (rural and urban) development within municipalities;
(3) municipal governments must now be consulted regarding natural resource use contracts
in their jurisdictions; (4) mayor, vice-mayor and councilors will all be elected directly for
a period of four years; and most importantly (5) a percentage of the national budget will be
fiscally transferred to those municipalities with lower resource mobilization potential.
Following the constitutional reform a new Municipal Law has been drafted that
would, inter alia, (1) regulate the election process; (2) more clearly define competencies
between municipal and central goverrnments; (3) commit the GON to transfer a percentage
of the national public budget to local governments according to pre-established ceilings.
IDA has kept an active dialogue with the GON on the decentralization process in the
context of the Institutional Development Credit and the preparation activities for the
proposed project. IDA has financed technical assistance on decentralization and local
public finance issues (see list of reports in project file in Annex 13).
3. GOVERNMENT STRATEGY
Sectoral Decentralization Commission. The GON recently created the
Sectoral Decentralization Commission (SDC -- Comision Sectorial de Descentralizaci6n,
4'    Examples of both ongoing and proposed projects include: Brigadas Ecologicas
Municipales and PROSDEL (ASDI); PROTMAREN (DANIDA); Modernizaci6n de Sistemas de
Producci6n Integral Municipal (EU); Desarrollo Rural Regional Municipal del Norte (GTZ);
Fortalacimiento de Capacidad Municipal (JSAID) and; projects financed through the Ministerio
de Acci6n Social.

Page 3 of 17
Decree 44-94) to propose and manage a national decentralization strategy. The SDC
forms part of the Executive Committee for Public Sector Reform (CERAP -- Comite
Ejecutivo para la Reforma de la Administraci6n Ptublica). The SDC is composed of the
President of INIFOM, who chairs the SDC, the Ministry of Finance, the Ministry of
Health, the Ministry of Education, the President of INAA and the Vice-Minister of the
Presidency for State Affairs. The SDC has defined seven overriding principles that should
guide the decentralization process: (1) public participation; (2) political conmmitment; (3)
gradual change; (4) capacity building and technical assistance; (5) transfer of resources;
(6) strong legal framework; and (7) restructuring and modemization of institutions. The
proposed IDA project would specifically target aspects (1), (3), (4), (5) and (6). The five
immediate tasks of the SDC are to: (1) propose a decentralization strategy; (2) propose
the reform to the Municipalities Law including in a system of fiscal transfers for
municipalities, to be approved by the National Assembly; (3) strengthen INIFOM; (4)
develop a plan of technical assistance and capacity building for municipalities; and (5)
implement and test local government models in pilot municipalities. During preparation of
the proposed IDA project some of these actions have been supported with preparation
funds and would continue to be supported during project implementation. The SDC has
already proposed the draft document for the reform to the Municipalities Law (which is an
integral part of the decentralization framework) which would define more precisely the
constitutional reforms introduced recently. Many of the line agencies (Education, Health,
Water, Natural Resources and Environment) are increasingly delegating implementation of
programs and projects to the local level.
This process is at a critical stage today due to the combination between the
weaknesses of local governments, particularly the rural ones, and the demands posed by
the decentralization process. Municipal governments are being given an ever increasing
role in the economy but for the most part are extremely weak in aspects of planning,
administrative and financial management, resource mobilization, delivery of public
services, cost recovery, and participatory processes. Thus while there is a new thrust
towards decentralization at the national level, rural local institutions are at a loss for
assuming their new roles. Capacity strengthening of local governments, including more
community participation, which is consistent with the decentralization agenda of GON,
must proceed at the same pace as the national decentralization agenda for it to be
successful.
INIFOM. The key institution in charge of municipal issues at the central
government level is the Nicaraguan Institute for Municipal Development (INIFOM; see
Chapter 2 of the SAR for a discussion of INIFOM). A recent study supported by IDA's
Institutional Development Credit identified the following main weaknesses in INIFOM:
(1) unclear role once decentralization is accomplished; (2) high number of managers
relative to professional staff, (3) lack of capacity to respond to municipal demands for
technical assistance and municipal studies; (4) conflictive roles in municipal development
and in municipal auditing and monitoring; (5) employment and career insecurity of
INIFOM staff; and (6) weak regional offices.
INIFOM has signed an Institutional Restructuring Agreement with CERAP in
the Ministry of Finance. Under the IRA, INIFOM agrees to implement the actions defined

Background: The Policy and Institutional

Page 4 of 17
in the Reform of the Public Service under the general supervision of the General
Directorate of the Public Service and the financial reforms identified in the Integrated
System for Financial Management, Administration and Auditing under the supervision of
the Ministry of Finance. More specifically INIFOM agrees to implement the following
reforms during the next two years: (1) On Institutional Reform: (a) to define strategic
objectives and the institutional role; (b) to redesign the organizational structure; and (c) to
establish a system of monitoring and evaluation. (2) On Technical Capacity: (a) to put in
place management systems to assist the process of decentralization and municipal
governments; (b) to train staff on decentralization and municipal strengthening; and (c) to
develop instruments for coordinating donor financed projects. And on Office Technology:
to implement a plan for electronic management of databases.
Municipal Financing. One critical issue is municipal financing. Among
Central American countries, Nicaragua allocates the highest percentage of GDP to
municipal financing (3.0% for Nicaragua in 1989 as opposed to 2.4% for Honduras;
1.2% for Guatemala; 1.1% for Costa Rica (1990); and 0.7% for Panama (1987).
However in Nicaragua the fourteen more urbanized municipalities capture the lion's share
of municipal resources. These fourteen municipalities account for 75% of all municipal
expenditures, but include only 49% of the population. While an urban municipality spends
on average more than US$20.0 per capita per year the more rural ones invest less than
US$0.50 per capita per year. The revenue and expenditure gap between the larger and
more urbanized municipalities, usually larger towns where regional offices of central
government agencies and most services are concentrated, and smaller and rural ones is due
to distortions arising from the municipal financing system.
Ninety seven percent of all municipal revenues are generated through various
local taxes and fees. There are very limited central government fiscal transfers (3% of
total municipality revenues in 1992) and in the absence of fiscal transfers municipalities
must rely on programs of line agencies or specialized institutions such as the FISE over
which they have little control. In 1992 74% of municipal revenues originated from taxes
and fees with the bulk coming from (I) the sales tax (3 7%); (2) the property tax (6%); (3)
the vehicle tax (5%); (4) the business registration tax (8%); and (5) user charges and fees
(16%). Sales taxes and business registration taxes, in particular, are predominantly
collected in trading centers where businesses are located, usually cities and towns. Less
urbanized municipalities, which generate important productive activities in agriculture, see
these activities be indirectly taxed (namely through the 2 percent sales tax) by urban
municipalities but do not share in the revenues. As a result urban municipalities have much
larger investment expenditures per capita than rural ones, which do not have the means to
invest in the public services necessary for the productive sectors.
A background paper prepared in the course of project preparation5
recommends that in the area of municipal financing the GON should: (1) strengthen the
finances of municipalities with weak economic bases through a national revenue sharing
system; (2) consolidate the current revenue structure of local governments; (3) eliminate
5      See project's background paper in project file: Garz6n, Hernando. 1995. "Municipal
Finances and Decentralization".



Annex 1

Context for Decentralization
Page 5 of 17
the 50% minimum limit on cost recovery of user fees and encourage sustainability through
full cost recovery policies; and (4) make explicit in the municipal law that municipalities
may pledge financial assets as guarantees for payrnent and make clear the rights of
creditors to seize financial assets. The draft Municipalities Law in the National Assembly
includes a Section on municipal financing proposing a revenue sharing mechanism to
compensate municipalities with a low revenue generation potential. The principle is that
GON would transfer a percentage of its revenues (4%) to municipalities according to a
formula reflecting population, poverty, area, participation effort and own revenue effort.
The transfers would be financed by reallocating resources from local development
programs currently channeled through INIFOM, National Assembly, MAS and Institute of
Sports. IDA supported background studies on this issue (see Annex 13).
4. LOCAL INSTITUTIONAL CAPACITY
A. Local Governments
Project Municipalities
During preparation two evaluations of the capacity of the 32 municipalities
selected for the project were carried out. The first evaluation developed three typologies of
municipality-A, B, and C--according to their revenue resources mobilization capacity (see
Table Al. l), while the second evaluation focused on the adequacy of municipal financial
management (see Figure A 0. 1). These assessments led to the conclusion that municipal
govermnents have important weaknesses, some of them intrinsic to the nature of local
govemments, and others resulting from the particular Nicaraguan context:
*  financial weaknesses that results in part from the limited resources;
*  administrative weaknesses because of the lack of a professional career at
the municipal level which leads to a high tumover of personnel;
*  urban bias: municipal councils have a vast majority of representatives from
urban areas though the share of voters from urban and rural areas is
approximately the same in Nicaragua. Although an urban bias is efficient
because the cost per beneficiaries of service provision is lower when
beneficiaries are concentrated, in Nicaragua this bias is inflated by an
electoral process that does not link elected officials to voters in rural areas;
*  insufficient use of public consultation and participatory approaches;
*  limited planning capacity, due in part to technical weaknesses, and in part
to the lack of resources needed from planning exercises;
*  difficulties in intemalizing regional, national and global enviromental
concems due to the local nature of municipal jurisdictions;
*  weak legal framework for local public finance and lack of revenue sharing
mechanisms that benefit disadvantaged municipalities.

Institutional capacity. Municipal capacity is highly correlated with own
revenues. During project preparation a typology of municipalities was developed based on
this relationship (Table A 1.1). Within the 32 project municipalities already selected for
the project the capacity to assume increased responsibility over poverty natural resource
management and economic development ranges from "extremely limited" to "limited".
Le6n is the only exception. It is the only municipality with a medium-term, strategic
development plan and with the administrative, managerial and technical capacity to
implement it. After Le6n, in terms of administrative and technical ability and financial
status the municipality of Chinandega is the most capable. The remainder of municipal
govermnents are generally weak in financial, administrative and technical ability. They
have little capacity to generate income and make investments. They lack the necessary
technical resources, both in terms of human resources and basic equipment.

Existing capacity reflects the demands which have been placed on municipal
government. With few exceptions (e.g., Le6n and Chinandega), the vast majority of public
investments in social, productive, and environmental infrastructure and services are made
with funds which bypass municipalities. In the few cases where funds do pass through
municipal government, they are maintained outside municipal accounts and donors reserve
responsibility for accounting and oversight. As a result the most relevant weaknesses are
those related to financial administration and management. Without financial resources
there is little incentive to generate capacity for administration and management and without
administrative and managerial capacity it has been difficult to access and increase
financial resources.
Financial Management Experience. Municipalities do have experience (and
thus capacity) where they have been able to raise substantial local revenues. Total 1994
investment for development activities by government (local and national) 16 in project
municipalities was US$9.3 million (see Table Al.3). In the overwhelning majority of
cases investments in developmental activities were very modest and the funds managed by
municipal government represented only a small percentage of the total. A statistical
breakdown shows:
(1) a median investment per municipality of US$139,000; ranging from
US$36,000 (Cinco Pinos) to US$4.5 million (Le6n);
(2) a mean per capita investment of US$8.20; ranging from US$2.82
(Posoltega) to US$23.52 (Leon); and
(3) that funds generated by municipal governments accounted for 41% of
total investments; ranging in individual municipalities' contributions from
less than 1% to 72%. 50% of municipalities contributed less than 10%,
and 90% less than 25%.
All project municipalities have at least rudimentary financial accounting and
control systems (see Table Al .4 and Figure AIO. 1). About 70% of project municipalities
have their defined organizational structure completely staffed. Some 40% of them have
manuals of municipal government functions and 30% have terms-of-reference and manuals
of procedures for municipal employees. All but one project municipality maintains a set of

Municipal Decision-Making. As entities responsible for raising their own
operating budgets, municipal governments have had to develop mechanisms for making
decisions which include some prioritization and conflict resolution functions. All
important decisions are made by the Municipal Council, which has ultimate authority. The
strength of the Municipal Councils, however, lies only in their role as a political decision
making body, i.e. they are not broadly representative of the municipal population's political
or socio-economic diversity. They are elected proportionally from political party lists
(which tend to head the lists with candidates from urban areas) with the result that a
disproportionate number of councilors are urban residents while rural and urban residents
vote in similar proportions. Roughly it was calculated that on average only 13% of
councilors reside in rural areas while rural populations contributed roughly 50% of all
votes. Hence Municipal Councils do not intemalize as well the concems of rural
communities as they do of urban ones and are not well positioned to initiate activities
directed at sustainable rural development, rural poverty the environment, and natural
resource management. This is further underscored in the majority of project municipalities
where the average level of educational attainment among the mayors and councils is low.
There is a rising comprehension in many rural based municipalities, however, that the only
a      The "Financial Management Indicator" was developed on the basis of 27 weighted
variables which assessed the accounting system, staff adequacy, internal auditing, and financial
management of the 32 municipalities

viable source of municipality wealth and hence municipal revenues is the rural sector.
There is thus a renewed effort to develop municipal programs to support further
development of rural based activities often seen as a source of local taxes, although there is
very limited technical capacity to design and implement such programs.
Civic society participation in municipal decision-making. Informal mechanisms
which function to enhance participation in the decision-making process exist in the
majority of project municipalities. (see Table A1.5). The majority of municipalities also
have some type of organized "Community Development" committees. A special case with
a large impact are the Local Development Committees (LDC) created by the Ministry of
Social Affairs (MAS). LDCs are local structures made of sometimes elected and
sometimes non-elected community representatives and notables (priest, teacher, etc.) that
propose local investment priorities for public financing. LDCs are a controversial
initiative among municipal governments for two reasons: (1) there has been little financial
support for the investments identified by LDCs and thus some people claim that LDCs
have lost clout and there is some participation fatigue as the initial efforts led to limited
results; and (2) LDCs are a structure that is parallel to and substitutes for decision-making
functions of local government (municipal council), hence often are ignored by them.

B. Non-Government Organizations (NGOs).
Typology of NGOs. Capacity among local NGOs is critical to the feasibility
of devolving greater responsibility to the local level (see Table Al.6). NGOs play an
extremely imnportant role in most phases of local development and they control substantial
human and financial resources.19 NGOs are required to have a legal identity (personalidad
jurdica) and under the law are considered as civic, non-profit organizations.
Associations and cooperatives (such as for producers) take their legal form and identity
through the regulations enforced by the Direcci6n de Cooperativas in the Ministry of
Labor. NGOs operating within the project area can be divided into four types: (1)
national-level NGOs created before 1990; (2) national-level and local-level NGOs created
since 1990; (3) associations with local coverage; and (4) projects administered by foreign
donors that convert into NGOs:
(1) National-Level NGOs Created Before 1990 includes FUNDE, FACS,
CEPAD, CEPRODEL and CAPRI; all of which manage a diverse
portfolio of programs that include: social assistance, sustainable
development, environment, gender, child care, and emergency
response/disaster relief. Since 1990 they have all been implementing
credit programs for financing productive activities. Since 1992 they have
all also added "green" emphases to their programs.
(2) National and Local-Level NGOs Created Since 1990 developed
primarily specific thematic areas such as woman-in-development, child
care, human rights, sustainable development, environment, etc. Examples
of national-level NGOs of this type include: CIPRES which implements
sustainable development projects, and NITLAPLAN which specializes in
credit intermediation to small and medium farmers. There are numerous
examnples of local-level NGOs; as a group they have diverse roots both
within the municipalities (e.g. SELVA an environmental NGO which
operates only in one municipality) and outside (e.g. Fundaci6n Le6n 2000,
an NGO which developed out of a central government agency program).
The majority of these NGOs provide services to projects, responding to
those areas in which financing is available.
(3) Associations With Local Coverage are organizations with a membership
consisting of individual farmers or farmer cooperatives. The latter type
are second-level associations or cooperative unions (e.g., UNAG,
UROCOOM, and UCOOM) whose clients are service and credit
cooperatives and multi-sectoral cooperatives. They tend to specialize in
facilitation of credit and marketing. The former provide a range of
9' - According to a PRIAG study (Abaunza, B.M., 1993. Las Organizaci6nes No
Gubernamentales En La Generaci6n y Transferencia de Tecnologia), Nicaraguan NGOs may
currently be providing services to about 70,000 households and managing as much as US$ 100

services (e.g., credit, training, extension, and marketing) but only to
affiliated individuals. Many affiliates are farmers who have been
"saneados" for failure to repay loans and are thus no longer creditworthy.
Few women have had access to the associations' credit programs and, as
a result, there is a growing move toward the establishment of women's
associations (e.g. APROAL in Le6n).
(4) Foreign Donor Projects That Convert Into NGOs include the projects
Manual L6pez and CHINORTE (now, "Fondo Del Desarrollo") which
now remain as NGOs with a regional coverage. Their programs tend to
cover health, potable water, women-in-development, and natural resource
management. They also provide credit, acting as intermediaries for both
state and private banks.
NGO Activities. The activities of the NGOs working within the project area
fall within the following categories (see Table Al.6):
(1) sustainable development programs - directed toward small farmers in the
dry and humid tropics, offering technical options to overcome production
constraints (e.g. improved seed, soil and moisture conservation, soil
fertility management);
(2) micro-enterprise development - primarily credit intermediation programs,
these tend to be urban-based programs with little rural presence;
(3) credit intermediation - stand alone projects which operate communal
banks, cajas rurales, etc.
(4) agricultural credit - cooperative programs for production and marketing of
basic grains and some few, other agricultural commodities;
(5) environmental education - generally oriented toward the promotion of
improved farming and land use practices; and
(6) women's programs - these tend to focus on micro-activities (e.g. chickens
and pigs) at the household-level; there is an incipient trend (e.g. APROAL
and UNAG/Chinandega) to recognize women's wider roles in the economy
and environment and provide a broader range of opportunities than the
current.
NGO Capacity. With little exception NGOs are highly dependent on funding
from foreign donors; the exceptions are the credit intermediaries and those that sell
services (technical assistance, training, marketing). Neither national governrnent nor local
philanthropists are a significant source of financing. As a result, the national-level NGOs
have had a good deal of experience in proposal writing and competitive fund-raising with
foreign donors. The local NGOs have much less experience in these areas, mostly due to a
lack of infornation about funding sources and their individual requirements.

The national NGOs established before 1990 exhibit a higher level of
institutional capacity for financial management of projects. They maintain computerized
accounting systems and have well-established, professional sources from which to solicit
institutional assistance. They are also becoming self-sufficient in terms of recurrent costs,
though as yet this does not signify an ability to cover basic administrative costs with their
own funds. Having developed the capacity to access multi-lateral funds (e.g., from IDB
and EU), they have also had to develop professional systems for project administration and
monitoring. As a normal routine, they maintain project funds and the NGO's personal
funds in separate bank accounts, keep up-to-date financial and accounting records, and
submit themselves to annual external audits. The national NGOs established since 1990
also tend to exhibit a reasonable level of institutional capacity, though less advanced given
their shorter institutional life.
The majority of the local NGOs created since 1990 demonstrate potential, but
as yet their admninistrative capacity is limited. Their weakest area tends to be accounting
systems. As noted above, due to the lack of resources for institutional strengthening the
development of capabilities in these areas has lagged behind. The exception to this has
been those NGOs (e.g. Fundaci6n Le6n 2000) which developed out of central government
programs where capacity building funds were provided.
The associations with local coverage could be characterized as having a
capacity lying somewhere between that of the national and local NGOs. The second-level
associations maintain professional accounting and administrative personnel at their central
offices. Where the problems occur is at the level of the cooperatives and groups with
whom they work. At this level the lack of formal education and resources with which to
contract specialized personnel translates into minimal administrative and accounting
capabilities. The problems are exacerbated by the absence of any standardized accounting
systems and minimal access to training. Where exceptions exist, i.e., in the larger
cooperatives that can afford to hire personnel with more education and experience, the lack
of resources for training and office equipment (e.g., computers) is an obstacle.
As regards the NGOs arising out of foreign donor projects, they are well-
trained and professionally equipped, particularly in those cases such as CHINORTE where



Annex I
Background: The Policy and Institutional
Context for Decentralization
Page 16 of 17
an effort was made to leave adequate installed capacity for fund management following
project closure.
C. Local Technical Capacity for Natural Resources Management
On-going Projects. There are a number of initiatives in the project areas
directed at improving agricultural productivity. In the Pacific area, there are two
important bilaterally financed projects (Proyecto Pikin Guerrero (NORAD) and Proyecto
de los Maribios (Netherlands financed, FAO executed)) aimed at developing sustainable
agricultural technology in the buffer zone around and on the lower slopes of the Maribios,
the chain of volcanos that runs parallel to the coast a little to the north-west of Le6n and
Chinandega towns, and a third one (FINNIDA financed) that aims more specifically at
reforestation, to establish windbreaks and thus reduce wind erosion. In the Caribbean,
there are two main groups working in the field of agricultural technology. One, an NGO
"CIPRES" which has substantial experience with soil management (fertility and erosion
control) for small farmers and the other, a bi-lateral project (Dutch-financed) "PRODES"
which has its own research station and outreach program in Nueva Guinea, where it is
developing alternative production systems for the area. Other foreign-financed projects of
interest are DANIDAIManglares (developing mangrove management techniques in the
Estero Real), and CATIE/MADELENA (applied forestry research and demonstrations for
fuelwood, poles and timber production).
Technology Transfer. INTA provides rather limited extension services to
small and medium-scale farmers, and, in keeping with Government's policy to achieve the
gradual privatization of the research and extension agencies, a growing part of these must
be provided by the private sector. Presently, INTA fields about 50 technical specialists
and 200 extensionists countrywide; in the project area in the Pacific zone there are a total
of about 24 extensionists covering 11 municipalities
A study no of 88 entities (NGOs, projects, universities, institutions) active in
natural resource management found that over 60% of them worked in the promotion of soil
conservation and fertility management. The main technical offerings in these areas were
live barriers, organic fertilizers, green manure crops, contour cultivation and reforestation.
Only 21% of these worked directly with agricultural production, though this primarily
implied improved seed and provision of hand tools. Other activities (e.g., family gardens,
IPM, forest and fruit tree nurseries and small irrigation) were promoted by 13% of the
entities studied. Only 4% assisted with commercialization of farm products. One of the
main weaknesses identified by the study was the lack of training for technical personnel;
the overwhelming majority of resources for training were directed toward farmers and
fanner/technicians.
The number of natural resource technical specialists and professionals
(agronomists, agriculturalists, livestock specialists, foresters, biologists, botanists,
ecologists etc) in the project areas (Pacific and Caribbean zones) is uncertain. Best
1o - PASOLAC, 1993. Inventario De Entidades Que Trabajan En Agricultura Sostenible En
Laderas En Nicaragua. Document No.8.

estimates range between 250 and 350 for the total number of governnent, private sector,
and NGO technical specialists in the project's regions. An FAO study "1 found among
688 employees in 9 NGOs that only some 5% were natural resource professionals. The
PRIAG study (see cite in footnote #4) covered 18 NGOs that were estimated to account
for about 75% of technology transfer activities in the non-government sector. Between
them these 18 NGOs employed 668 persons which 119 were natural resource professionals
(50% with licenciatura and 50% tecnicos medios). In addition, these 18 were supporting
a network of 1,170 rural promoters who worked directly with an estimated 11,000 to
15,000 families. The number of natural resource technical specialists outside of
government is assumed to have risen significantly over the last two years since these
studies were completed. Restructuring of the public sector has been steadily reducing the
number of positions in government.
The Non-Government Sector. A great deal of capacity exists within the
NGO sector, however, it also tends to be dispersed and can be quite variable in quality. In
general terms NGOs' main weaknesses lie in their administrative, project formnulation,
project analysis and natural resource technical skills. In terms of logistical support, the
NGOs are relatively better off than the majority of the municipal governments.
Weaknesses in extension methodologies have been noted among a large percentage of the
NGOs which provide extension and other training activities to small farmers. These
include:
(1) little experience with use of participatory diagnostic tools for problem
identification and proposal development;
(2) limited understanding of the technical limits (physical, biological,
chemical) to the technical menu they promote;
(3) frequent improper application of the technical menu (e.g. poor species/site
matching, improperly planted and spaced vegetative barriers; mis-
application of organic inputs, etc);
(4) limited understanding of the conditions for financial viability (markets,
labor, opportunity costs) of the technical menu;
(5) weak training and communication methodologies (e.g., heavy reliance on
farmer workshops and theoretical lectures; unclear focus to messages; too
many messages; training of promoters as broad generalists, etc); and
(6) use of dubious and unsustainable incentives for adoption (e.g., over-
reliance on demonstration fanns, subsidized credit, free inputs and tools,
etc).
- Miranda, Byron. 1992. Nueva Institucionalidadpara el Desarrollo Agricola y Rural en
America Latina. Fao/Managua.






NICARAGUA
RURAL MUNICIPALITIES PROJECT
ANNEX 2
BACKGROUND: NATURAL RESOURCES ISSUES AND THE PROJECT AREA!'
1. NATURAL RESOURCES ISSUES
Nicaragua is a country known both for its large areas of still pristine forests,
watercourses and coral reefs in the Atlantic and for a high level of resource degradation in the
Pacific, Northem and Central regions. Underlying most of the resource degradation are
misguided policies, extreme poverty, the civil war and migratory pressures due to the war and
other factors. The Atlantic region has so far escaped these pressures due to poor access, low
population density and the war period during which various armed groups sought refuge in its
forests. The situation is changing however and pressure from refugees, landless farmers and
legitimate development needs are accelerating the advance of the agricultural frontier.
Soils
While soil erosion is considered to be the most important cause of soil degradation
on a national level, other phenomena may be as or more important on a local scale (i.e.
compactation, salinization, acidification, and sodification). Erosion by water is the main
process of interest, wind erosion is also important in numerous (specific) locations throughout
the westem half of the country. About 7.7 million ha are affected by erosion, of which 52% is
classified as having light to moderate erosion; 33% as having moderate to high erosion; and
15% as high to severe erosion. The main causes of accelerated erosion are: (1) deforestation;
(2) lack of integration of conservation practices into land use practices (including roads); (3)
rapid, uncontrolled expansion of annual cropping and livestock over the last 30 years; and (4)
inappropriate land uses.
At a national level, drops in yield have been noted and ascribed to erosion. Little
empirical evidence exists though one study found a yield decline in com of 82% over a 9 year
period as a result of soil loss (NEAP/NIC, 1994). Areas identified as having priority
erosion/soil degradation problems are: (1) the southern watershed of Managua; (2) the foothills
of the San Cristobal, Chonco and Casita volcanic complex; (3) the foothills of Volcan
Cosiguina; (4) Le6n - La Paz Centro areas; (5) south slope of the Meseta de Carazo; (6)
Volcan Mombacho and adjacent areas; (7) the plains in the Tipitapa - Granada area; (8) the
northwestern portion of the Rio Coco watershed; (9) the Jalapa Valley; and (10) the Nueva
Guinea area.
Forests
Since 1950 forest cover has been reduced from 7 million hectares to an estimated
4.3 million hectares (TFAP, 1992). In 1985, one author (Peters, 1985) estimnated the rate of
1' - For a more complete treatment of the subject, see Project Working Papers: (29) "Ordenamiento
Ambiental del Territorio (PROTIERRA) - Problemasy Recomendaciones",.



Annex 2
Background: Natural Resources Issues and the Project Area
Page 2 of 12
deforestation to be 0.1 million ha/yr. Other authors estimates range from between 0.07 million
ha/yr in 1990 (Utting, 1993) to about 0.125 million ha/yr for the period 1981 to 1990 (FAO,
1993) and 1991 (Merlet et al, 1993). According to the NEAP (1994) this rate has increased in
the last few years to 0.15 million ha/yr. Land classified as suitable only for forestry usage
occupies about 6.2 million ha; divided as 1.8 million ha for conservation and 4.4 million ha for
sustainable use. Some 2.5 million hectares of these lands are deforested and being used for
extensive livestock production or are abandoned and under mixed grass/shrubs. Of the
remaining forest areas about 88% or 3.8 million hectares consists of a humid, tropical forest
cover type with which there is very little positive management experience to draw upon.
The main value of forests in Nicaragua has been in its conversion to agriculture.
As such, the "expansion of the agricultural frontier" is reported to be the principle cause of
deforestation. The greatest pressures are apparently in the Atlantic and parts of the Central
zone. At current rates it is estimated that only 10 to 15 years would be required to eliminate
the remaining areas of productive broadleaf forests (NEAP, 1994). The end of the war and
return of peace to the near-forest zones and demobilization of a large number of soldiers (both
government and contras) in recent years has apparently contributed significantly to an increase
in the rate of conversion. The expansion of agricultural lands and forest conversion also have
roots which lie in: (1) lack of economic incentives for forest management; (2) low productivity
and non-sustainable agricultural practices; (3) poorly defined or missing land tenure; (4) lack
of alternate employment; (5) a strong agricultural tradition in which forests are considered an
obstacle; and (6) little or no tradition of forest management.
The traditional system for forest management in Nicaragua has been to remove
only the valuable species with no subsequent consideration of damage to the residual stand or
for follow up management and/or control. In this system forest was not treated as a renewable
resource, it was simply mined for its value. There are very few examples in the country of
forest management for long term production. In addition, information to manage the forests is
generally lacking. The forests and lands which belong to the state have never been defined or
delimited. National and regional trend data on forest cover and forest use is lacking.
Management data on forest resource abundance, distribution and regenerative capacity is
almost completely missing.
In 1992 about 60% of net energy consumption came from fuelwood (57%) and
charcoal (1 %); almost 99% of which went for residential use (NEAP/NIC, 1994). According
to the TFAP (1992), wood fuel represented about 95% of the average US$ 148.8 million
annual value of forestry products in the early 1990s. Whether or not fuelwood harvesting
constitutes a forest degrading activity in some locations is unknown. What is certain is that
fuelwood production and harvesting is unmanaged and risks exist of it becoming a forest
degrading activity. Harvesting and sales of wood fuels constitutes a significant income
generating activity among the rural poor. Fuelwood is in short supply near densely populated
urban centers and prices have been reported as being high. However, near densely populated
areas in the Pacific zone (i.e. Le6n) there are indications that fuelwood is currently priced as if
it were abundant. Given the large areas of land being allowed to reconvert to woody
vegetation, fuelwood may indeed be abundant.



Annex 2
Background: Natural Resources Issues and the Project Area
Page 3 of 12
Fire is apparently a problem in a large number of areas where recurrent burning
(primarily for agricultural purposes) is a major contributor to forest degradation and failure of
reforestation efforts.
Water
Inappropriate land uses, lack of a regulatory framework and inadequate protection
(e.g. effluent and solid waste treatment, control of contaminants, provision and enforcement of
penalties/incentives, etc) have all contributed to a deterioration of the nation's water resources.
Problems are further intensified by a lack of planming and subsequent conflicts over water
allocation (e.g. between hydroelectric and irrigation). Specific, priority issues include scarcity,
watershed management and water quality.
Scarcity. The Pacific side and Central Zones receive the least rainfall and
commonly, both their rural and urban areas are characterized as suffering water supply
deficits. Conflict over water allocation appear certain to increase given the desire to expand
irrigated areas and divert water for economic development (e.g. agroprocessing facilities).
Drought in some degree is not infrequent in many parts of the Pacific and Central zones and
the current farming systems, particularly of the small farmers appear to be extremely
vulnerable to drought. Land use practices which decrease the infiltration of rainfall (e.g.
frequent burning, up-and-down slope cultivation, etc) may be contributing to reductions in
groundwater and of surface water low flows on some local scale. Other factors contributing to
the deficits undoubtedly include population increases with little or no parallel investment in
upgrading of water systems and in some areas of the Pacific zone possibly includes
uncontrolled exploitation of groundwater.
Watershed management. Watersheds which have been identified as requiring
management for production of surface water include: Managua - Rio Mico; Matagalpa - Rios
San Francisco and Molino Norte; San Rafael del Sur - Rio Jesus; Nueva Guinea - Rio Zapote;
Ocotal - Rio Dipilto; Boaco - Rio Fonseca; and Puerto Cabezas - Rios Taupi and Braquiria.
Those identified as requiring management for groundwater include: Chinandega - Rio Acome;
Santo Tomas - Rio Cuipar; and Bluefields - Rio Escofran.
Potable water. About 20% of the rural population versus 79% of the urban
population have access to potable water (NEAP, 1994). In comparative terns, the percentage
of persons with access to potable water in Nicaragua is the lowest in Central America. Over
80% of rural households suffer from contaminated water (World Bank, 1992. SAR Social
Investment Fund)
Water quality. There are no systematic data regarding water quality therefore a
comprehensive overview of the problems cannot be given. What is known about the
dimensions of water quality consists of:
(1) Lake Managua. The lake comprises the worst case of contamination in the
country. Apart from the natural salinization process which the lake is
undergoing, other causes of diminishing water quality include: the direct
discharge of sewage, industrial wastes, agrochemicals (both from use and



Annex 2
Background: Natural Resources Issues and the Project Area
Page 4 of 12
manufacture), effluents from the Momotombo Geothermal Plant, garbage
dumping on the lakeshores and sediment inflows. It is estimated that over 40
tons of mercury have been discharged into the lake in the last 20 years. Other
lakes reportedly suffering from serious contamination include the Lagunas
Tiscapa and Masaya. Water quality of Lake Managua is being handled
through an IDB project currently under preparation.
(2) Industrial contamination. One of the potentially most serious problems in
the country is associated with Laguna Asososca, which is an important water
supply for Managua. Apparently the laguna receives water from the aquifer
which lies below the industrial zone between Laguna Asososca and Lake
Managua. The aquifer has been contarninated by industrial discharges thus
affecting water quality in the laguna. Relatively good data exists on the
physico-chemical and bacteriological status of the laguna.
(3) Agrochemical contamination. The high rates of pesticide usage in some
agricultural areas, especially the (current and past) cotton growing areas, has
resulted in agrochemical residues in both groundwater and surface water. In
the Pacific zone around Chinandega, El Viejo, El Realejo y Le6n levels of
nitrate nitrogen in groundwater exceed 40 mg/It (or 4 times the amount
allowed in the United States). In the Pacific and Central zones, the
agrochemical contamination of surface water is reported for the rivers Esteli,
Grande de Matalgalpa, Jinotega-Apanas, Acome, Chiquito, Oro, Fonseca,
Mayales and San Juan. The Jinotega and Matagalpa rivers, in addition,
receive significant levels of direct discharges from coffee processing.
(4) Sediment. Accelerated erosion caused by inappropriate land use practices is
reported in all parts of the country. No empirical data is available. Impacts
are reported in all estuaries, lakes and river courses. Sediments would be a
major vehicle for transport of agro-chemicals to water bodies.
(5) Urban Areas. Fecal contamination, non-point source pollution from
agriculture, industrial effluents and deficiencies in the wastewater systems are
the main problems. Managua, among all urban areas, has the greatest
problems.
(6) Mining. The rivers Tunky (or Sucio), Mico, Maiz and Tecomapa are
reported to be contaminated by cyanide and other heavy metals as a result of
mining and processing activities.
(7) Saltwater intrusion. Overexploitation of groundwater in some of the westem
parts of the country (e.g. Chichigalpa, Chinandega, Corinto, and Poneloya)
has caused saltwater intrusion as far as I Okm inland from the coast.



Annex 2
Background: Natural Resources Issues and the Project Area
Page 5 of 12
Coastal Zone and Fisheries
In Nicaragua three of Central America's five largest rivers feed a unique system of
bays and lagoons which serve as critical breeding habitat for major commercial species (e.g.
shrimp and lobster). On the Pacific side, sedimentation and agrochemical residues (e.g. in
Estero Real in the Gulf of Fonseca) are potentially effecting the wild shrimp populations which
breed in these waters. On the Caribbean side rich coral and sea grass resources (one of the
largest sea grass areas in the world) exist which are habitat for the endangered manatee and
almost one-half of the Atlantic's population. The commercial and biodiversity values of these
areas are perhaps the highest in Central America. Priority issues include the fishery, shrimp
culture and mangroves:
Fishery. Fishing pressures are known to be increasing while adequate data is not
available either on the magnitude of the catch nor on the carrying capacity of the resource.
The fishery is little diversified and thus the majority of fishing efforts are concentrated on a
few species (sea bass, lobster, green sea turtle, shark and red snapper). There is some
indication that the shrimp catch along the Pacific coast and the lobster catch along the
Caribbean have reached their limits. In addition, the unregulated use of fish traps and the high
loss of non-target species caused by shrimper's nets are thought to be significant factors
contributing to the observed declines in marine fauna populations.
Shrimp culture. The expansion of shrimp culture is thought to have little
scientific base and to potentially be destroying coastal habitat with unknown effects on zones
which are extremely important as breeding grounds for both marine and avifauna. It is feared
that uncontrolled expansion of shrimp culture will begin along the Caribbean coast in those
areas which are critical breeding habitat for six commercial species of (wild) shrimp.
Mangroves. The main area of mangrove loss is along the Pacific coast. The
losses are the results of: (a) harvesting fuelwood and timber for local use; and (b) shrimp
culture, both from clearing and ponds creating changes in water levels and salinity.
Biodiversity
Less is known about the biodiversity values of Nicaragua than any other Central
American country. Ten principal ecosystems have been identified and described; the
identification work, however, is not necessarily complete. Information on biota is highly
uneven. Some 1,200 species or an estimated 95% of vertebrates have been identified, whereas
an estimated 65% of plant species have been identified (8,550 species with 56 endemic
species). The list of saltwater fish (311 Pacific species and 305 Atlantic species), mollusks
(2,000 species), corals (42 species), and plants (107 algae species and 5 sea grasses) and of
freshwater fish (46 species with II endemic) is considered to be almost complete. The lists of
terrestrial invertebrates is extremely weak and the only insect and mycological information
available is on those with economic importance to the agricultural sector. Specific, priority
issues include loss of biodiversity, habitat loss and protected areas.
Loss of biodiversity. The primary causes of species and ecosystem loss are the
same as those driving deforestation with the addition of conmmercial harvesting pressures to



Annex 2
Background: Natural Resources Issues and the Project Area
Page 6 of 12
supply a growing local and intemational market with specimens and products from wildlife. It
is estimated that 2% of terrestrial vertebrates are in danger of extinction and 10% are currently
threatened. The agricultural frontiers to the north and east are the most dynamic zones of loss.
Habitat loss. The overwhelming majority of tropical dry forest habitat has already
been lost as these areas were the initial zones cleared for livestock and some annual crop
cultivation. Only remnants of this forest type still exist within the Pacific zone. Little
information is available on mangrove habitat and trends in loss. Pressure on mangrove may be
increasing in the westem coastal zone areas near the Honduran border where development of
shrimp culture is of interest (Coastal resources).
Protected areas. Within the approximately 3.4 million hectares of land which
have undergone little modification from human influences there are about 2 million hectares
which are considered in the NEAP to be worthy of protection within the protected areas
system. To date over 85% of this 2 million hectares lies within 71 legally constituted protected
areas; legally constituted by presidential decrees, not by a protected areas system law. Among
these 71 areas are: a) 0.06 million hectares under active protection and/or management; b) 0.25
million hectares under some minimal protection scheme; and iii) the remainder without
management plans, without management activities of any kind, and without any personnel to
guard or monitor the area. The other 1.4 million hectares are classified as production forest or
potential agricultural lands. Under the General Environrnent Law of 1996 new protected areas
will need to be approved by law.
2. LEGISLATION, STRATEGIES AND INSTITUTIONS
Legal Framework
The national environmental action plan (neap) notes that the laws and policies
governing ownership and use of natural resources are out-dated, disjointed and incoherent.
The most critical need is passage of legislation and/or regulations covering eleven natural
resources and related themes: environment, forestry, mining, fisheries, biodiversity, protected
areas, pollution, water, soil conservation, coastal zone management, indigenous land and
natural resources issues, and municipal and autonomous governments. Nicaragua has made
important efforts to rationalize the legal framework for natural resources; the general
environment law was ratified by the national assembly in April 1996. Various laws are in
draft form or already under discussion in the national assembly (the fisheries law, the mining
law, the energy law and the pollution law).
Natural Resources Strategies
In spite of the legislative bottlenecks noted above, some progress has been made
recently in developing a national strategy for the environment and natural resources:
Presidential Decree 246-92 approved (1) the National Forestry Action Plan (TFAP); (2) the
National Strategy for Conservation and Sustainable Development (ECODESNIC); (3) the
Strategy for Environment and Land Use Planning (EOAT). The National Environmental
Action Plan (NEAP) was approved by Presidential Decree 261-93 on December 3, 1993.



Annex 2
Background: Natural Resources Issues and the Project Area
Page 7 of 12
Between them, these plans and strategies provide a reasonable framework within which
priorities have been established for investment and institutional development and that is
consistent with the decentralization agenda to be supported (see Table A2. 1).
