Natural Resources Institutions
Rural Development. The National Agricultural Council (CONAGRO) was
created in 1993 by presidential decree to coordinate policies and activities between the
Ministry of Agriculture, the Institute of Natural Resources (IRENA, now MARENA), and the
Institutes of Agrarian Reforn and Agricultural Technology (INRA and INTA). Since 1994
CONAGRO has grown in importance, having assumed the role of key coordinating GON
structure for rural development and has placed high on its agenda issues relating to the



Annex 2
Background: Natural Resources Issues and the Project Area
Page 8 of 12
management of natural resources. INRA (Instituto Nicaraguense de Reforma Agraria) is the
institution in charge of titling lands. It is being supported by the IDA's Agricultural
Technology and Land Titling and Land Management Project (Credit No.2536-NI) and plans
during the project to award about 25,000 titles in the Pacific, Northern and Central regions.
INTA (Instatuto Nicaraguense de Tecnologia Agropecuaria) is the institution in charge of
research and extension. It is also being supported by IDA Credit No. The PNDR (Programa
Nacional de Desarollo Rural) was recently created as a result of an IDB project of the same
name. The focus of the PNDR was initially on the Central region of Nicaragua, but since then
it has absorbed various donor financed rural development projects that were spread out
through several institutions, namely MAG and MARENA. MAG has seen its role weakened
following the proliferation of specialized institutes and programs. A draft Law on the
Organization, Competencies and Procedures of the Executive Branch of Government (Proyecto
de Ley de Organizaci6n, Competencias y Procedimientos del Poder Ejecutivo) is being
discussed in the National Assembly. In this law the proposed mission of the MAG would be
(1) to manage the agriculture development policy; (2) to promote crop diversification; (3) to
develop agriculture crop zoning; (4) to cooperate in plans and programs for environmental
protection; (5) to establish agricultural schools; (6) to promote the competitiveness of
agricultural production; (6) to collaborate in industrialization and commercialization of
agricultural products; and (7) to manage fitosanitary controls.
Natural Resources and Environment. In January 1994, a new Ministry of
Environment and Natural Resources (MARENA) was created out of the former IRENA by
Presidential Decree 1-94. It now has the authority to: (1) coordinate and direct all public
policy towards natural resources; (2) promote sustainable use of natural resources; and (3) to
propose laws, regulations, standards and strategies for sustainable use and protection of
natural resources and the environment through participatory processes. Currently, MARENA
is, together with INIFOM, one of the first six institutions of Government participating in the
reestructuring contemplated in the Institutional Development Credit. This credit would support
the streamlining of MARENA and the strengthening of its basic functions including financial
management, functional structure and personnel and administration. Recommendations
already under implementation include the reorganization of the Ministry into three inter-
disciplinary directorates (Policy and Strategy, Regulation and Control, and Interinstitutional
Relations) and the devolution of some natural resource projects to the PNDR and
municipalities, along with appropriate technical assistance to strengthen municipal capacity.
The main activities to be financed by the Institutional Development Credit are the following:
(1) institutional reorganization including redefinition of mission, objectives, functions,
organization and administrative procedures; (2) implementing a system for coordinating
externally financed projects; (3) improvement of project management; and (4) develop and
implement a strategy for information technology. In 1996 a new General Environment Law
created the National Environment Comnuission, headed by MARENA, in charge of
coordinating environmental activities and an Environment Fund, to channel resources for the
environment.
Enforcement Agencies. The General Environment Law of 1996 created an Office
of Environmental Affairs in the General Attomey's Office. Additionally, prior to this legal
development which has not yet materialized with the necessary appointments and funding the
Armed Forces and the National Police have been assuming an increasing role in matters of



Annex 2
Background: Natural Resources Issues and the Project Area
Page 9 of 12
environmental and natural resources legislation. The Armed Forces includes in its statutes
(C6digo de Organizaci6n, Jurisdiccion y Previsi6n Social Militar; Ley 181 de 02.09.94)
supporting the branches of the executive with natural resource conservation and renovation,
improving the environment and ecological equilibrium and collaborating in the strategic plans
established by the President, which include the Tropical Forestry Action Plan and the National
Environmental Action Plan. In March 1995 MARENA and the Armed Forces signed an
interinstitutional agreement (Convenci6n Interinstitucionalpara la Conservac6n delMedio
Ambiente y Uso Sostenible de los Recursos Naturales MARENA-EPS, 29 de Marzo de 1995)
which defines the areas and mechanisms of coordination between the two institutions. Under
this agreement the Armed Forces agreed to include in its annual operating plans activities
ranging from monitoring of protected areas to the control of illegal trade of wildlife. The
National Police executes orders issued by the authorities and in particular by MARENA in the
natural resources and environmental areas. The National Police has signed a special
agreement with the Ministry of Health through which the police assumed the role of sanitary
inspectors. A similar agreement for the control of illegal logging is being discussed with
MARENA. Neither the Armed Forces nor the National Police have any special training on
natural resources and environmental aspects and this is considered a major constraint that
prevents these institutions from assuming the roles contained in the agreements they subscribed
to.
Other Environmental Institutions. A number of other institutions intervene in the
natural resources and environmental areas. The Ministry of Health has responsibilities over
water contamination. INAA (Instituto Nicaraguense de Acuaductos y Alcantarrillados)
regulates water use, proposes the establishment of water reserves in collaboration with
MARENA and cooperates with the Ministry of Health on water pollution. The National
Commission of Hydrological Resources (Comisi6n Nacional de Recursos Hidricos), which
depends from the President of the Republic, is in charge of water policy and solving water use
conflicts. The Ministry of Construction and Transport, together with MARENA, has
responsibilities over oil and other fossil fuels pollution. The National Ports Corporation
(Empresa Nacional de Puertos) and the General Directorate of Aquatic Transportation
(Direcci6n General de Transporte Acudtico), together with MARENA, oversee contamination
by ships. The Ministry of Agriculture and MARENA oversee issues of soil conservation. The
Ministry of Agriculture, MARENA, Ministry of Health, Ministry of Construction and
Transport, Ministry of Labor, Ministry of Finance (Customs) and the municipalities all have
responsibilities over agro-chemical contamination, a very serious problem in Nicaragua, in
particular in the cotton rich area of Le6n and Chinandega. Until recently Nicaragua was the
largest producer of DDT in Central America and although DDT is now banned, problems of
contamination by this and other substances persist and are considered a serious health hazard
and one of the most critical environmental issues in the country. The Ministry of Economy is in
charge of issuing licenses and concessions for the fishery, mining and forestry, but MARENA
has a control and monitoring role over both the fishery resource and forests and contamination
from mining. The Nicaraguan Fisheries Corporation is in charge of fisheries policy.
3. NATURAL RESOURCES ISSUES IN THE PROJECT AREA
Some 1.7 million of Nicaragua's 4.2 million people live in rural areas. Among
these rural inhabitants, 55% live in poverty and another 20% live in extreme poverty (IDA



Annex 2
Background: Natural Resources Issues and the Project Area
Page 10 of 12
Poverty Assessment, 1993). For rural people, the natural resources sector (including
agriculture) provides the main opportunities for income generation and employment. Currently
agriculture provides about 90% of employment for an estimated 420,000 persons.
Relative to other economic sectors, the agricultural sector has the lowest individual
income and wages. Agricultural employment is highly correlated with poverty. Despite
limited economic opportunities, the number of persons seeking a livelihood from natural
resources based sectors (including agriculture) has increased. With unemployment at 13%
nationally and underemployment estimated at 44%, the natural resources based sectors serve
as the "employer of last resort"; they are a social safety net where the exploitation of natural
capital subsidizes the survival of the poor and unemployed.
The combination of rural poverty and lack of altemative employment is expressed
on the environment through:
(1) increasing soil degradation from deforestation ' followed by the conversion of
forest lands to marginal livestock and agricultural uses and from the lack of
integration of conservation practices into land uses;
(2) increasing degradation of forest and biodiversity resources due to the
expansion of the agricultural frontier, non-application of control and
management systems in all types of exploitations (fuelwood, poles, timber,
hunting, harvesting of secondary products, etc), and inadequate protection of
parks and reserves;
(3) ongoing deterioration of water resources due to watershed degradation,
sediment loading, lack of treatment of effluent (urban, agroprocessing,
mining), and over-pumping of groundwater; and
(4) over-fishing and destruction of mangroves for domestic purposes and shrimp
pond construction.
Two regions where this situation is of particular concern are: (1) the former cotton
production areas of the Pacific (Departments of Le6n and Chinandega) where the decline of
cotton production has eliminated the region's major employer and forced many ex-agricultural
laborers and their children to turn to subsistence farming seeking land in areas of fragile
natural resources or to migrate to cities; and (2) along the agricultural frontier in the Atlantic
in the Departments of Rio San Juan, Chontales and Jinotega and in RAAS and RAAN. In the
latter, many ex-combatants and displaced persons have moved to the agricultural frontier in
search of lands and livelihood. A significant percentage of the poorest households are female
headed. Many of the rural householders are "new" farmers having had little or no previous life
experience as agriculturalists. Also in the Atlantic, climatic and soil conditions are sufficiently
different from those in Nicaragua's other agricultural regions that even experienced farmers
coming there are at a disadvantage.
2 _ In the Pacific zone much of the deforestation is historic, however there is yet ongoing degradation
of the remnant forest areas for domestic uses (timbers, poles, fuelwood). As the remaining Pacific
forests are generally those found in protected areas, upper watersheds, and coastal zones (mangroves),
the value of the environmental services which they perform as wildlife and breeding habitat and
watershed protection are still significant both locally and internationally (i.e. Golfo de Fonseca and
the forests of the Cordillera Volcanica Central).



Annex 2
Background: Natural Resources Issues and the Project Area
Page 11 of 12
The project area thus comprises 32 municipalities in the Departments of
Chinandega and Le6n in the Pacific and Departments of Rio San Juan, RAAS and RAAN in
the Atlantic zone (see Table Al.9 for list of municipalities already selected for the program
and Maps in Annex 12). During the project's annual reviews the possibility of extending the
project to RAAN and parts of the Department of Jinotega and Chontales would be considered.
Within the 32 municipalities are located a total of 1,498 communities (1,283 in the Pacific
zone and 215 in the Atlantic zone). Total rural population (i.e., potential beneficiaries) of the
32 municipalities is about 477,000 persons (60,000 persons in the nine municipalities selected
in the Atlantic).

NICARAGUA

ANNEX 3
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT:
POVERTY, GENDER AND INDIGENOUS ISSUES IN THE WESTERN REGION'
1. BACKGROUND
The Nicaraguan govemment used several consultative and participatory mechanisms
to prpare the project, including a series of workshops with non-governmental-organizations
(NGOs), community representatives, local governments and local leaders. From the outset
thiese takeholders provided important contributions to the design of the project. In addition,
prcuect prepamtion included a Social Assessment (SA) in the Departments of Le6n and
Chnandega, which are the project target areas during its first phase of implementation. The
SA was carried out between May and September 1995, with support from the FLAHS Fund.
And it included a special investigation of the indigenous group Sutiaba. The next step is to
eend the SA to the Atlantic region and include in it an Indigenous Peoples Development Plan,
to be conpleted before the GEF supported component and activities in RAAS and RAAN are
iniated.
The main objectives of the SA were to: (1) identify key stakeholders; (2) identify who
are the rural poor and what their living conditions are; (3) understand the views of the poor on
the causes of poverty and how to overcome them; (4) understand existing fanning systems and
te particular constraints under which farmers operate; (5) evaluate the main needs and
obstades to income generation among the poor; (6) evaluate the linkages between poverty and
anironment degradation; (7) assess the degree to which the rural poor are organized in NGOs
and/or in Community Based Organizations (CBOs); (8) assess the strength of local
organizations and the kind of services the rural poor obtain from them; (9) evaluate the
stakeholder's perceptions of local governments; (10) evaluate key gender issues and how the
project should address them; and (11) make recommendations on how to eluimnate/mitigate
risks, stabilize migration and strength the capacity of community organizations to better deliver
project services.
The SA included a study of the Sutiaba indigenous group, which is located in the
Department of Le6n. The Sutiabas are a mixture of an ethnically diverse population coming
from different areas of Le6n and rest of the country. Although the majority of residents have
Subtiaba ancestry, many others are not Subtiaba descendants but consider themselves Sutiaba.
This summary is based on background documents produced by Marcia Leite Ariera (consultant,
poverty), Mari Lisbeth GonzAlez (consultant, indigenous people), Marvin Ortega (consultant,
rapid rural appraisal) and Maria Correia (gender specialist, LA2NR). Social Assessment
Coordinator for the project is Valeria Pena (LATEN).

2. SOCIO-ECONOMIC CONDITIONS OF THE PROJECT AREA
Project Stakeholders
The primary stakeholders of the project are: (l) small farmers and other rural
poor-both men and women; (2) other farmers; (3) children and youth; (4) commnunity based
organizations; (5) the Subtiaba indigenous group; (6) NGQs; (7) municipal governments; and
(8) central government agencies. With the exception of some central government agencies it is
expected that all stakeholders will gain from the project. Some central government agencies, in
particular in the rural sector, could oppose the project since decentralization would somewhat
reduce their power.
General Economic and Demographic Features
The agricultural land in Leon and Chinandega used to be the most fertile of the
country-both areas were major producers of corn, beans and fruits. Since the 1950s,
however, production shifted towards export commodities like cotton, sugar cane and bananas.
The shift in production caused a loss of soil fertility over the long-In due to inappropriate
technzologies and over reliance on agrochemicals. The civil war, political conflict, returning
refugees, decreasing export crop prices, inflation and environrnental degradation have since
contributed to produce a decline in the living standards of the population. With a

proportionately large share of the national population (17 percent), the Western region of
Nicaragua today shares a relatively high percentage of both the poor and the extreme poor of
the country, 16 percent and 17 percent respectively. Within the region, about half of all
households and seven tenths of all rural households fall below the poverty line.

Poor and Non-Poor
The Department of Le6n has ten municipalities and a population of about 423,000
whose livelihood is mainly derived from agriculture and livestock. The Department of
Chinandega consists of 13 municipalities and has a population of about 373,000, which relies
primarily on agriculture. In both departments, about half the population live in rural areas. As
is common in poor regions, the population of the Western Region is young and younger than
Nicaragua's population as a whole-45.4 percent of the population consists of pre-school-age
and school age children under 14 years of age. About one fifth of the population is less than
six years old.
The adult population is predominantly female. About 25 percent of all households are
single headed and of those more than four fifths are headed by women. Differences in poverty
levels among single headed households are also apparent. In the rural Western Region, 75
percent of single headed households are poor, compared to 70 percent of conjugally headed
families. Moreover, as shown by the RRA, rural households headed by women are associated
with very small properties, smaller than three and a half hectares.2
Because labor is the most important asset of the poor, households become poorer as
the number of working age adults decreases in relation to the number of dependents. The
dependency ratio3 of the region, 0.84, is higher than the average ratio in rural areas (0.98).
Tne dependency ratio of the poor in the whole Western Region rises to 0.95. Households are

Poor and Non-Poor
Land Tenure
Four types of farmers were identified in the RRA. Small farmers with holdings
smaller than 3.5 hectares constitute the first group-58 percent of farmers are in this category.
Approximately one third of these households are female single-headed families. These small
properties, are more frequently found in the municipalities of El Jacaral, Santa Rosa, Achuapa,
Larreynaga y Quezalaguaque in the Department of Le6n, and in the municipalities of
Morazon, Cinco Pinos, San Francisco, Santo Tomas, San Pedro and Posoltega, in the
Department of Chinandega. Farmers tend to produce for self consumption and survival and
sell any surpluses they may have in local markets. Productivity levels, however, are
T he proportion of individuals over 60 years old is very low: 0.05 in the region and 0.01 in the rural area.



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 5 of 22
constrained by the size of property, the absence of credit, and the low technology available.
These farmers have few or no trees close to the house and typically do not cultivate
vegetable gardens. Many farmers received land through the Sandinista agraran reform and
lack land titles, although the current Government is making efforts to provide secure titles.5
The second group-comprising some 29 percent of farmers-cultivate 3.5-15 hectares
of mostly corn, beans and sesame (ajonjoli). They also usually own a cow to produce milk for
household consumption. The female role is central in the survival strategies of this group,
since women sell domestic produce and are the primary household contact with the public.
A third group, about 8 percent, have larger properties and produce for the extemal
market, usually in association with livestock production. They are found in the municipalities
of El Viejo, Larreynaga, El Sauce, La Paz Central, Nagorote, Le6n, Chinandega, Somotilla,
and Villanueva. Although opportunities are limnited, smaller farmers are a source of labor for
these large farms.
Finally, a fourth group is composed of about 7 percent of farmers who are organized
in cooperatives created during the last decade. Usually, they combine work in the common
fields with work in their own land. Although women participate in cooperatives as family
laborers or as family members, they are often not formally recognized as cooperative members.
3. RURAL POVERTY
Living Conditions of the Poor
LSMS respondents in rural areas of the Westem region and SA participants were
distributed among 31 rural communities. The conditions of the 31 conmmunities are as follows:
*  2 have post offices
*  8 have paved roads leading to their communities
*  13 do not have access to motorized transportation
*  8 have a doctor and 6 a nurse, 13 do not have any doctor, nurse or midwife
* 4 have a pharmacy
* 5 do not have primary schools
* none of the commnunities have a daily market.
All respondents agreed that living conditions had not improved during the five years
previous to 1993. On the contrary, members of 26 communities believed that conditions had
worsened while the other five believed they have stayed the same. Absence of employment
opportunities, inflation, and high cost of living are the reasons given for this situation. In
Puerto Morazan it was reported during the RRA that "we are poorer now than before
because there are no sources of employment'" Very often, the pessimism is expressed in a
5 The Government is receiving support for land titling through IDA's Agricultural Technology and
Land Management Project.



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 6 of 22
kind of religious fatalism, for example, "There are many people saying that this is the will of
God", as was said in Chinandega.
Access to water and sanitation in the project area is inadequate, particularly among the
rural poor. Less than half all households in the Westem Region have piped in-house water, of
which 72 percent of non-poor and 7 percent are extremely poor. Urban residents have greater
access to piped water but differences between poor and non-poor are greater in these urban
zones. Seventy percent of urban households have piped water, of which almost four fifths are
non-poor and 3 percent are poor. The inequality between groups is less dramatic in rural areas
because so few have access to water: 70 percent of the extreme poor, 63 percent of the poor,
and 68 percent of the non-poor do not have in-house piped water in rural zones.
Access to sanitation services is even scarcer. Only about 20 percent of all households
in the Western region are connected to public sewer systems, most of them non-poor
households. The extreme poor simply do not have access to sanitation services. Even septic
tanks benefit only 7 percent of households. In rural areas of the Western Region, 99 percent of
households have no access to public sewer systems, septic tanks, or cesspools. All households
with access to any of these types of sanitation services are non-poor.
The Poor's Perceptions of the Causes of Poverty
According to the local rural population, three major factors cause poverty: lack of
employment, diminishing productivity and limited markets. Participants cited stagnant
agricultural and livestock activities as the main reasons for limited employment and income.
For those who own the land, productivity has diminished and there is no credit available.
Among landowners interviewed during the RRA, 83 percent had not received credit or financial
assistance during the year prior to the interview. Lack of extemal and internal markets
emerged as another primary reasons for poverty. As explained by a small farner in El Viejo,
"The peasants do not have seeds, do not have minimal agricultural inputs, there is no
credit, there is no one to whom to sell, someone who buys instead of robbing us."
Data from the LSMS confirm the people's evaluation of their own poverty.
Altogether, 56 percent of respondents reported not working in the seven days prior to the date
of the interview, and 51 percent reported not having looked for a job, which can be interpreted
as an indicator of hopelessness. Once again, differences between urban and rural areas are
striking. Of those who had worked, 60 percent were urban-based and 40 percent were from
rural areas. During the year prior to the LSMS, 50 percent reported not having worked and of
those who had worked, 81 percent were urban residents. Among those rural inhabitants who
consider themselves employed, 41 percent are self-employed.
Irrational natural resources management is not cited in the RRA as a cause of poverty
and low agricultural productivity. Although there is a broad recognition that this is a
constraint, environment degradation was overwhelmingly identified as a consequence of
poverty as opposed to a cause.
Male temporary migration is now a common feature of the region. Migration is
directed towards Costa Rica more often than to other rural areas of Nicaragua. According to a



Annex 3
Socil Assement:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 7of22
priest in El Jicaral, "As they do not have anything to work with, no seeds, many go to
Costa Rica, lately 2500 have gone. Many also look for work In Ledn, Chinandega and
Matagalpa because in El Jacaral there are nojobs, the banana production does not
provide them employment anymore and the quality of the land is now very bad". Male
outmigration puts a strong pressure on women who are left to care for households in conditions
of extreme disadvantage. Migration, which began as a coping strategy for the poor, often
results in the break down of the family and neighborhood patterns of solidarity.

aural Organizations
In the Western Region people believe that solidarity is an important mechanism to
overcome poverty. According to a participant of the RRA: "only people who belong to some
organization are able to be conscious, the rest of the population, where there are no
organizations, is inditferent'. About 40 percent of RRA participants belong to at least one
organization. Of those who are organized, 42 percent belong to a producer organization, such
as a cooperative; 31 percent to a religious organization; 12 percent to a comununity
organization; 6 percent to a development project; 6 percent to a political party; and 3 percent
to a union. The organizational system which prevails is very intricate, having as key actors
foreign governments and donors, local and international NGOs, and local communities. This
system probably provides the most important safety net for the rural poor as indicated by the
following examples:
*   "There is a Commission for the environment protection in Villanueva and
Somotillo, counting with the participation of Casa de la Mujer, the Asociaci6n de
Ganaderos, the Polos de Desarrollo and the Police" (RRA).
*   The Fondo para Chinandega Norte, which is funded by Switzerland, was
constituted with support from the Secretaria de la Mujer de Chinandega and



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 8 of 22
comprises more than 1000 women who, together with some NGOs, implement
several development projects.
In Chinandega, in the municipalities of Somotillo, Santo Tomas, Cinco Pinos,
San Pedro, San Francisco and Villanueva, the Commission de Desarrollo
Comarcal was formed and funds about ten projects with financial help from the
Swiss government.
According to the RRA, the most frequent organizational structures found in the area
are the following:
*  Comrtes comunales or comites comarcales operate at the level of municipality
and are usually initiated by the communities themselves. Members, who are
elected by the Community, usually consist of the midwife, the person in charge of
health services (brigadista de la salud), the teacher, a representative of the
municipality, and the priest. During the last decade, the Comites served as a
vehicle for demanding land. In Le6n, the Comites are the strongest and most
organized voice of local communities.
*  Cooperatives were organized during the last decade with the objective of pooling
resources, such as land and labor. Today about 61 cooperatives are active in the
area with 1,116 members, working 25,000 hectares of land.
*  Women groups, the most important of which are the Casas de Mujer, are spread
across some municipalities and linked to the National Organization of Women.
The focus of the Casas de Mujer is on professional training. They seldom work
on rural activities.
*  NGOs, are present in the entire region, particularly in Le6n, Chinandega,
Somotillo, El Sauce and El Viejo (refer to Annex I for details on NGOs).
*  Project Organizations such as: (1) Apoyo a la Actvidad Forestal Campesina de
Le6n, funded by Finland; (2) Proyecto de Conservaci6n y Manejo de Recursos
Naturales, funded by Holland and FAO; (3) Proyecto de Cultivos de Camarones
en Estanques, Puerto Morazon, funded by Christian Initiative of Munster
(Germany); and (4) Programa de Apoyo a la Conformaci6n de una Estrategia
de Desarrollo Alternativo para el Campesino Pobre, funded by Austria.
The Poor's Poverty Alleviation Priorities
Despite differences in access to formal education, men and women identify the same
priorities for alleviating their poverty. Support to agricultural subsistence activities was
overwhelmingly considered the highest priority amnong those interviewed by the RRA, followed
by access to health services and clean water (see Box 3.).
The Poor's Perceptions of Local Governments
Results of the RRA indicate that perceptions of local governments are mixed. While
most (75 percent) knew who was the mayor, 44 percent knew the council members, and 27
percent concurred that local governments consulted the population, many complained about
authorities. A common complaint was that although communities had been invited to



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 9 of 22
municipal meetings and workshops, these events lacked continuity and only a few people
participated. Others noted that not aUl communities participate in workshops. Those who said
that the municipal administration does not consult the population, cited lack of community
organization, politics and the mayor's incapacity as reasons.
Participants also acknowledge the limitations of local governments to respond to local
problems. Some participants recognized that the local administration tries to answer their
needs as demonstrated by one response: "they gave landfor twenty years, for reforestation
on a lifelong basis, as a response to community requests, therefore they listen to peasants
and to the community" (male commnunity member, Chinandega). Others thought that the
mayor did not make an effort to meet community demands: "he listens to them either through
their representatives or he sees them in his office, but he does not solve the problems"
(female conmmunity member, Le6n). Activities carried out at by the municipal administration
creates a certain awareness of local government. Fifteen percent of respondents said that the
mayor had helped them with land problems whereas about 20 percent had received help for
seeds. Thirty nine percent recognized municipal support for education, 37 percent for health
and 36 percent for road maintenance.

Participants provided ideas on project implementation mechanisms at the local level.
In terms of the best form of organization for articulating conmnunity demands, 43 percent
preferred a Comite Comarcal, 17 percent wanted a small committee, 11 percent preferred
small groups of interested people, 11 percent preferred individual interaction with municipal
government and 5 percent recommended cooperatives. On the subject of who should be
involved in key decisions regarding subproject approval, 53 percent indicated the mayor, 60
percent recommended council members, 87 percent proposed a local notable (priest, teacher,
nurse, etc.) and 72 percent suggested an NGO. An overwhelmingly number of participants,
(92 percent) said that a community representative should participate in such decisions.

4. GENDER ISSUES
Gender Roles and Priorities
While men's roles in the economy are well acknowledged, women play an important
role in the survival strategies of the poor not only through their partnership with men in
conjugal-headed households, but because they frequently live on their own, heading households
without a male partner. Studies in Nicaragua indicate that while rural men control productive
resources and land, women in poor households-both jointly and single headed-contribute
significantly to household income. Single headed female households depend exclusively on
women's income. Similarly, women in conjugal households are often responsible for providing
fbr the family during the agricultural off-season and periods of scarcity. Particularly when
fann and off-fhrm opportunities for men are restricted, women will resort to selling garden
products and small livestock, petty trading and domestic labor in order to sustain the family.
Women's crops are also a major source of family nutrients year round and therefore represent
a key element of the rural poor's survival strategies.
The survey showed that in terms of addressing poverty, support to agricultural
production is the highest priority of men and women, however, their agricultural technology,
extension and training needs will vary. A division of labor in agricultural and livestock
production exists in Nicaragua. Broadly speaking, basic grains and livestock are primarily the
domain of men while women are the dominant producers of horticulture products, fruits and
small livestock and play a secondary role in producing basic grains. Women also predominate
in marketing farm products.
Men and women use natural resources for different purposes and this, in tum, affects
their priorities and participation levels in environmental projects. The principal causes of
deforestation in the project area are felling for commnercial purposes, the expansion of
agricultural lands and hunting-which are typically male activities and hence male interests.
Women, in contrast, tend to be more concerned about domestic fuelwood. Water emerged as a
prirnary interest of both men and women but while domestic consumption was a female
concem, men showed greater interest in water for irrigation and production. Gender-based
interests in natural resources are reflected in participation rates in environmental projects
which tend to have intrinsic social goals and be domninated by women. Women's decision-
making roles on these projects, however, is usually less prominent than men's.
Gender Differences in Education
Female single-headed households are at disadvantage when it comes to education.
Education plays a central role in an individual's ability to move out of poverty and, therefore,
the fact that female heads of households are much less educated than males places female
single-headed households in a more vulnerable position. The gender inequality pattem is
pervasive in the Western region in which, for each educational level complete, male heads
outnumber women heads on a 2 to I basis up to the primary level and on a 4 to 1 basis from
the secondary level as graph 4 in the next page shows. The pattem of inequality is greater in
rural than in urban areas.



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Westem Region
Page 11 of 22
Gender and Participation in Rural Organizations
The SA indicates that men and women are involved in different forms of organization
with women participating to the greatest extent in service-oriented groups (religious and
community) and men predominating in producer organizations (UNAG, cooperatives and
unions). For example, men comprise 88 percent of members of agriculture and livestock
cooperatives in the region. Men also predominate in municipal governmen. in the region,
comprising 83 percent of mayors and 89 percent of the consejales. While a gender
breakdown of local government committees (comarcales) is not available, it is expected that
women's and men's involvement is more balanced given that these committees typically consist
of the local midwife, teacher and health worker as well as representatives of producer groups
and the church.

A corollary of gender differences in organizations is that, relative to men, women will
have less access to information and resources because producer groups tend to be better
organized and have greater political clout. The SA supports this assertion. It showed that
overall men's participation in organizations was greater than women's and that membership in
organizations was associated with land tenure security and other resources. Women surveyed
also had less knowledge of their municipal leaders than men.

5. THE SUTIABAS
Who Are the Sutiabas?
The Sutiabas are one of thirteen Nicaraguan indigenous groups that claim a "native
background". In the words of a Sutiabas leader, "nativ background is the right that all of
us, native people, have, to respect our own culture and historical lraditions,fornm  of social
organization and the ownership of our communal land". The basis for the Sutiabas ethnic
identity is rooted in their kinship relationships, which provide social and ethnic cohesion. The
Sutiabas kinship system, however, has been eroded as a result of the changes that the country
has experienced through its history. Kinship is no longer restricted to the children of Sutiaba
fathers and mothers, but rather to the identification of individuals with Sutiabas values and
costumes. The migration of ladinos to the Sutiaba region, attracted by the possibility of
fanning communal lands since colonial times, allowed for a reinterpretation of kinship
networks to include the children of the ethnic nix which resulted from migration. Migration
also brought about acculturation, loss of control over communal land property, and periods of
violence resulting discrimination and from the predominance of ladinos.
Sutiaba History
Since Spanish colonization, the history of the Sutiabas is characterized by a permanent
tension on land rights and efforts on the part of Le6n landowners to take increasingly larger
tracts of traditional indigenous territories. Strategies to squeeze the Sutiaba's traditional land
rights included invading their lands, dissolving Sutiaba autonomous forms of economic and
social organization through national government legislation and promoting "ladinizacion" of
indigenous communities. Since the mid-18th Century, the Sutiabas have constituted an
independent political-administrative division of the city of Le6n. In 1850, with the introduction
of coffee plantations, the need for labor led the government to issue legislation on vagrancy and
mandatory labor which justified the forceful and massive recruitment of Indians for farm labor.
The introduction of cotton crops in the 1950s exacerbated tensions and violence.
Some battles have marked the Sutiabas political conscience and have became political
references which, in tum, have strengthened their ethnic identity. Intemal contradictions on the
most effective means of conducting such disputes, on the other hand, have weakened the
community to some extent.
Sutiabas Demographics
The Sutiaba population of about 36,000 constitutes 17 percent of the total population
in the Department of Le6n.6 The majority -approximately 23,000-live in urban areas and
the remaining 12,000 living in rural areas. Rural Sutiabas are spread over nine rural
comarcas which are also populated by non-Sutiaba residents. Rural residents currently
occupy an area of approximately 580 square kilometers, which represents a small fraction of
previous Sutiabas' territory.
' Census Population Projections, Ministry of Health, 1995.



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 13 of 22
Urban Sutiaba live in the traditional historical site of the Sutiaba pueblo, on the
westem side of the city of Leo6n and in twenty-four small urban settlements to the North, South
and West of the Sutiaba pueblo. The Governuent created these settlements in the mid-80s as
part of its overall strategy to counter military aggression originating in neighboring Honduras.
The municipal administration of Le6n, has been using the areas around the Sutiaba community
to establish settlements and absorb the city's natural urban growth. Such settlements
-although created without consulting the Sutiabas-are not a source of conflict because the
Sutiaba community has accepted the new settlers. Overall, it is estimated that around 15
percent of the urban population (approximately 3,500 residents) live in the settlements, with
the remainder living in the historical Sutiaba site.
Although the majority of residents have local Sutiaba ancestry, it common for all
dwellers in Sutiaba territory to identify themselves as indigenous Sutiabas, to share the
Sutiabas cultural traditions, and to make claims to communal land. Ninety-eight of RRA
participants identified themselves as indigenous Sutiaba, although only 71 percent have either
the father or the mother born in Sutiaba. The same percentage of women and men identified
themselves as indigenous Sutiabas, although there are more women (80 percent) than men (64
percent) with at least one Sutiaba parent.
The Sutiabas Land Claims
Presently, Sutiaba lands extend from the Northwest to the Southeast of the City of
Le6n, in the plains between the Maribios Mountains and the Pacific coast. The land is mostly
flat except south of the city of Le6n where slopes are steeper and elevations reach almost 150
meters above sea level. Two rivers, the Telica and the Chiquito cut the region. The Chiquito
River has been contaminated by domestic and industrial waste. Both rivers contain about 40
percent of all river water in the department. Statistical data on Sutiaba land and land
ownership is conflicting and disorganized. Different versions of what constitutes Sutiaba
property makes it difficult to deternine precisely which portion of the land belongs to the
conmmunity, which portion belongs to peasants and which portions were under the control of
other groups. The royal title in the hands of the indigenous community states that the land
belonging to the Sutiabas is equal to 63 "caoallerias" and 12 "atillos", ancient Spanish
measures about which there is no information on the corresponding area.
A key to understanding the Sutiabas land situation is the way land titles have been
distributed and are recognized. Within the 7,000 hectares which are under the direct control of
the conmmunity, many small farmners were given supplementary titles to land.. Only 11 percent
of small farmers recognze that the land they work is part of traditional communal lands.
Fewer than 15 percent of members of local cooperatives are Sutiabas, but these cooperatives
own between 27 and 30 percent of traditional Sutiaba lands (8,000 hectares).
Among rural and urban participants of the RRA, about 44 percent do not own any
property, 45 percent own some property, and the rest work on communal properties. Fifty
percent of land owners own less than 10 manzanas and the vast majority-89 percent-have
registered legal title to the property. Only 3 percent of Sutiabas farmers were cooperative



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Westem Region
Page 14 of 22
menbers. Eighty percent of urban Sutiabas do not own land and 6 percent are small
landowners with properties less than 3 manzanas.
The majority of Sutiabas live in poverty, have few resources for market production
and are exposed to climatic hazards. Farmers with properties of about 15 hectares who live
above the poverty line constitute only 8 percent of farmers. In rural areas, only 6 percent of
residents do not own any land but about 50 percent own properties smaller than 3.5 hectares
and live in extreme poverty. An important segment in rural areas are the 40 percent of
peasants with properties between 7-15 hectares. They are poor peasants who sell an important
part of their crops in the market and who, like their urban counterparts, are very vulnerable to
climatic changes.
Employment and Income Among the Sutiaba
Employment dynamics among the Sutiabas does not differ from the general trends of
the other poor in the Westem region. Unemployment is high and employment, when available,
is restricted to low paid and unqualified jobs. In the urban area, about 44 percent of
participants considered themselves as unemployed and about 20 percent did not have a
permanent job. In total about 65 percent of respondents in urban Sutiaba were unemployed or
held unstable jobs.
Women Sutiabas are restricted to professions such as commnerce, maid services,
teaching and nursing. The majority of Sutiabas women who do not work define themselves as
housewives rather than unemployed. According to one participant: "I haven 't had a job since
1990; Idon 't even lookfor ajob anymore because I can 'tfind any" (Sutiaba housewife). In
urban areas, the economically active population for women is the same as that of men.
Women also have lower incomes relative to men (a little over 50 percent of the price of a basic
food basket for women as opposed to 70 percent for men). The middle-level
technic4l/professional category for women in commerce is the only employment category that
has above average income levels, although these incomes are only close to the price of the
basic food basket. A significant proportion of women work as maids, which is one of the
lowest paid jobs in the economy. In rural areas, the situation for women is even more difficult
because they are not even recognized a farmers although most perform agricultural tasks
alongside men.
The majority of the rural-based economically active population works as small farmers
or rural workers. The latter are mostly seasonally employed for about 5 to 6 months a year.
All rural families grow basic crops such as beans and com usually on lands smaller than one
manzana. Quantities of food produced are not enough to sustain the family, a situation that
forces members of the family to seek alternate survival strategies such as selling firewood and
working as day laborers or in construction jobs in exchange for food.
The Sutiabas are organized in 18 cooperatives which sow about 2,600 hectares of
corn, beans and sorgo annually. The cooperatives, however, do not generate permanent work
year round for their members. Crops are grown using non-traditional technological levels and
generally occupy members 6 months every year, leaving them unemployed for the remaining 6
months.



Annex 3
Social Assessment:

The Sutiabas and the Environment
The Sutiabas have no experience managing forest areas nor has this concem been part
of their tradition in light of the more pressing need for survival. Sutiaba territory lacks
important forest areas except fbr a few patches of tropical dry woods. Forest destruction is
caused mostly by cutting for domestic and commercial firewood. Almost 82 percent of RRA
participants use firewood for cooking.
Social Organization Among the Sutiabas
Social capital among the Sutiabas is high-about 48 percent of RRA participants are
members of community organizations. Although gender differences are small, more men are
organized than women. The largest organization is the ndigenous Community; 55 percent of
RRA participants reported belonging to the Indigenous Community, of which a slightly higher
proportion were urban-based. The second most important form of organization are those
related to development projects-17 percent of participants claimed that they were members of
these types of groups.
The traditional Sutiaba organizations are the Junta Directiva de la Comunidad
Indigena, the Consejo de Ancianos, the Consejos Comunales, and the Juntas de
Cementerio. Characteristics of these organizations are as follows:
   The Junta Directiva is elected by popular vote for a two-year mandate, although
government regulations state that elections should be annual. As a result of the
economic, social and political changes that affected the ethnic composition of the
Sutiaba communities, the Sutiabas require only kinship as a basis for membership
in the community. Thus, voting rights are given to any individual who has been
residing in the comnmunity for more than seven years whenever they accept and
identify with the Sutiabas' indigenous values, even in the absence of Sutiaba
ancestry. From a political and administrative point of view, this is the
indigenous organization with the largest mandate, although it does not constitute a
pure form of autonomous local governrment. Both urban and rural Sutiabas are
under the jurisdiction of the municipality of Leon.
*   The Junta Directiva is not a traditional Sutiaba organization although it was
created by the Sutiabas to deal with issues related to the indigenous community.
Its origins relate to the Sutiabas process of incorporation in Le6n. Most likely the
organizational structure started as a mechanism promoted by government and
local landowners to counter indigenous land disputes which had been led by
traditional indigenous organizations like the Consejo de Ancianos. Basic
activities of the Junta Directiva are: mediating community relationships with the
city administration and the central government; administering community land;
coordinating activities with different indigenous organizations; and representing
the Sutiabas in the Coordination of Nicaraguan Indigenous Communities. It has
also promoted activities in the area of social, economic and cultural development.



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 16 of 22
But the Junta Directiva 's main asset in the moral authority won during land
disputes. The Junta is recognized by the Sutiabas as their moral and political
authority even though this role has been weakened in recent years by political
polanzation.
*   The Consejo deAncianos was the highest authority in the community before the
Spanish colonization and has remained so despite attempts by past governments
and colonial powers to weaken this organizational structure. The council
virtually disappeared in the first half of the twentieth century, but reappeared in
the 80s due to interest among the people and particularly the efforts of the Junta
Directiva. The Consejo de Ancianos is an autonomous moral authority with
approximately 35 members who are elders in the community. Members must be
born in Sutiaba, belong to old famnilies of conununal tradition, be recognized as
an advocate of communal land property, have been a member of the Junta
Directiva, and have an untarnished reputation.
*   The Consejos Comunales are neighborhood organizations with representatives
elected at the vicinity-level. Their rnain responsibility is to respond to social
demands of residents.
*   The Juntas de Cementerio are in charge of maintaining and managing five
community cemeteries as well as observing religious traditions and Holy Week
rituals.
Traditional productive organizations no longer exist among the Sutiaba. The
cooperatlvas, which are presently the most usual model of productive organization in the
community, emerged in 1979. Each of the 18 Sutiaba cooperatives has an average of 17
members, who are mostly urban based. Eleven of the cooperatives are part of the UCAIS
(Uni6n de las Cooperativas Indigenas de Sutiaba), which provide associate members with
technical assistance, storage, marketing and legal and bookkeeping advice. UCAIS has the best
capacity for economic management in the community.
In addition to the advice it provides to associate members, UCAIS operates a number
of other programs including a program for female farmers; the Asociaci6n de Ganaderos de
Sutiaba (ASOGASA), which is a small association of members who own lands outside the
Sutiaba territory limits; and the Fondo Revolvente de Credito Xuchiall, which is revolving
loan fund for productive activities. This fund was created by the Junta Directiva de la
Comunidad and the Nicaraguan Institute for Investigations and Popular Education (INIEP).
Until 1995, it had provided financing to 122 families, 79 percent of which were female single-
headed. A team of indigenous Sutiaba professionals manage the fund.
The two most important organizations that work with women and youth among the
Sutiabas are:
The Fundacion para el Desarrollo Integral de la Mujer Indigena "Xochitl
Acatl " which receives support from European funds and UNICEF to work



Annex 3
Social Asssmnt:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 17 of 22
with women. Funds support informal credit for poultry production, vegetable
gardens, cattle and reforestation. To this date, 47 female single-heads of
household have benefited from resources for productive activities. Funds also
offer educational programs in health, family planning, pre-natal and infant
care, and sanitation. In cooperation with the Universidad Nacional
Aut6noma and cooperating physicians, the Foundation supports clinical
activities which have benefited an average of 290 patients a month in
traditional medicine and 240 in non-traditional medicine. Other programs for
women and youth at risk include education in sexuality, fertility, sexually
transmitted diseases, solidarity and equality.
The Movimiento de J6venes Indigenas "Hringue Nidi Ruis" (Children of the
Sun) offers program in health, water, electricity, education, enviromnent,
culture and sports. Major sources of funding include UNDP and UNFPA for
the project on Recreation and Attention Centers for Sutiaba Adolescents
(CARAS).
Tlhe presence of non govermnental organizations in the Sutiaba territory is weak
except for the ones mentioned above. CARE, CIPRES and CEPAD also support small
susainable projects in water, agriculture and irrigation in a few districts.
6. CONCLUSIONS
The main conclusions of the Social Assessment are as follows:
(a)    Rural areas of the Western region show the poorest social indicators. In
contrast with urban areas, poverty in rural zones is greater, opportunities are
fewer, educational levels are worse, and there is less access to infrastructure
services. Health and education facilities, sanitation, roads, and access to
local markets are unsatisfactory in many areas.
(b)    Rural households are more crowded and have a higher dependency ratio than
in urban ones. The proportion of children in poverty is high in the entire
region, and higher in rural than in urban areas. Children are an at-risk
population group.
(c)    In the project area, the main gender issues are: (i) single-headed households,
which tend to be female, tend to be poorer than conjugally-headed households;
(iii) both men and women play a role in the survival strategies of the poor but
women have a particularly critical position because they are responsible for
most single-headed households and often provide for conjugally-headed
families during periods of scarcity; (iv) the gender division of labor on the
farm and in the household imply that technology, extension and training needs
among men and women vary; (v) men and women use natural resources
differently and therefore also have different priorities in environmental
activities; (vi) educational levels among women tends to be lower than among
men which, in tum, affects women's ability to participate in project activities;



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 18 of 22
and (vii) men and women have different forms of organization which affects
their access to information and resources.
(d)    The rural poor, including the Sutiaba rural poor, are predominantly small
farmers, often landless. Frequently, households live off properties of less than
3.5. Productivity is low and access to markets is difficult.
(e)    Two interrelated factors cause poverty: first, the small size of property
coupled with extremely low productivity from inappropriate technologies and
lack of financing; and second, the absence of off-farm employment
opportunities for the poor.
(f)    According to the poor, poverty results from lack of employment, low
agricultural productivity and limited access markets. Based on their diagnosis
of poverty, the priorities of the poor are: first, projects oriented to support
agricultural production; and second, health services. Water supply is a
priority for both agricultural needs and family consumption. The environment
is not seen a priority because is it not perceived as a constraint to poverty.
(g)    The sense of hopelessness that prevails arnong the poor is often compensated
by a cultural disposition for social solidarity. The poor in the project area are
well organized. They participate in community groups such as the cornites
comarcales, cooperatives, women's associations, Sutiaba cooperatives,
NGOs, and project groups. Foreign donors, however, largely mobilized and
sustain these community organizations.
(h)    The main problems of the Sutiabas are land disputes. However a vast portion
of the Sutiaba land is under conmmunity control and this control cannot be
legally challenged. This includes cooperative land legalized by the
governnent. Indigenous leaders requested that the project prioritize women
because their experience shows these investments produce greater benefits to
the family. Rural projects are of little interest to urban-based Sutiaba because
they are for the most part city dwellers.
7. ACTION PLAN FOR PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
Project implementation would take into account the recommnendations of the Social
Assessment and Indigenous and Gender studies in the following ways.
Rural Poverty
(a)    Municipal Technical Units and INIFOM would actively promote the project in
eligible communities to make information available to the rural poor by using
local meetings, churches and radio in this dissemination process. Considering



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 19 of 22
the low formal education level, particularly among women, the project would
emphasize visual materials such as videos.
(b)    The project would promote and support two key community structures to help
the poor voice their demands and force local governments to respond to
them-Interconrmunity Assembly and Community Supervision
Committee-which include members elected by communities.
(c)    The project would support several training programs for communities directly
and for professionals (private, NGOs, government) that would provide
services to these communities.
(d)    The subproject categories include most priority investments identified by the
poor during the Rapid Rural Appraisals.
(e)    Periodic rural appraisals would assess the results of project implementation
and, in particular, if it is effectively reaching the poor. A annual review would
permit changes in the project to accommodate results from rapid rural
appraisals.
Gender
(f)    Gender Goals. The project would promote male and female participation and
benefits to men and women by including gender as an explicit goal of
municipal development. Gender goals would also be reflected in the
philosophy of the municipal development component of the project and
included in staff training so that gender becomes a part of staff values.
Training on gender issues would also include members of the municipal and
participatory structures involved in the project (municipal governments,
municipal technical unit, intercommunity assembly and community
supervision committee)
(g)    All personnel working on the project would be given the explicit responsibility
of involving men and women in municipal development activities, and this
duty would be reflected in staff terms of reference (including INIFOM
personnel, other project staff and municipal technicians). The project would
avoid establishing special segregated programs for women which tend to
marginalize women and imply that women's programs are an appendage to
mainstream project activities.
(h)    To ensure that gender is adequately internalized in project implementation the
project would recruit a participation and gender advisor to work on staff of
INIFOM as part of the project team. The advisor would act as a catalyst and
resource person and ensure that gender is adequately covered in promotion and
information dissemination activities, participatory microplanning and
monitoring. The advisor would also monitor the effectiveness of the project in



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 20of22
reaching men and women. Tenms of reference for the participation and gender
advisor are in the Project Implementation Plan.
(i)    Training Programs. To enhance men's and women's participation, the
project would train staff on how to detect gender differences and constraints to
participation, and how to develop strategies for overconiing gender barriers in
the project.
(j)    Project Promotion. The project would ensure that promotional material and
communications channels reflect gender differences and constraints.
Specifically, materials would avoid gender stereotypes in visual displays and
text and portray men and women as equal participants in the project. The
project would design a promotional and communications strategy for men and
women based on the following information in project areas: (a) specific local
gathering spots for men and women; (b) frequently used or accessible forms of
mass media for men and women; (c) time that men and women use media
channels; and (d) traditional means of communication. It is expected that the
project will establish a door to door promotional campaign to ensure both men
and women are invited to introductory meetings and microplanning sessions.
If societal norms inhibit women's participation with men, the project will
gather men and women separately and train community men and women on
gender issues to avoid possible conflicts.
(k)    Participatory Planning and Subproject Implementation. The project would
use a participatory planning methodology with a gender perspective to ensure
that both men and women have access to municipal funds and are involved in
determining community priorities and planning and executing subprojects.
The methodology will consider: gender-based social divisions in the
cosnmunity; time schedules of men and women; gender differences in
geographical mobility; literacy, education and training differences in men and
women; and organizational capacity and legal status of men's and women's
groups.
(I)    The project will encourage female participation through their own
organizations in situations where men dominate and have greater influence in
conmmunity organizations, but will work towards mainstreaming women in
community groups by training women in organization, leadership and self-
esteem and training men on gender issues. The project will also schedule
activities to coincide with time and geographical limitations faced by men and
women (e.g. on Sundays following or before religious services).
(m)   Training and Capacity Building at the Community Level. The project will
support training programs to compensate for illiteracy among community
members which in tum impedes participation in the project (particularly on the
part of women). Training and information disseniination will involve means
other than written materials (such as oral communications tools) to avoid
illiteracy obstacles. The project will recruit trainers and intermediary



Annex 3
Social Assessment:
Poverty, Gender and Indigenous Issues in the Western Region
Page 21 of 22
organizations based on their capacity and experience to work with illiterate
people. Training naterials supported by the project will include manuals,
workbooks and learning tools, such as games, that use simple concepts,
illustrations, colors and cartoon figures.
(n)    Project Monitoring and Evaluation. The project establishes gender
desegregated indicators into the monitoring and evaluation process of the
project to measure the differential impact of the project on men and women
and the effect of men's and women's participation on project outputs.
Indicators that will be differentiated by gender include: (a) members of
participatory structures; (b) attendance at community assemblies; (c)
participation in community and municipal planning; (d) number of people
participating in environmental, natural resources and productive subprojects;
and (e) number of direct and indirect beneficiaries of subprojects.
(o)    Special Studies and Review Procedures. The project would finance special
studies to measure the effect of the project activities on poverty reduction, by
gender of the individual involved in the activity. Specifically, the annual
reviews would examine: (i) gender opportunities and constraints to
participation at the family, group and conmnunity level and project strategies
to overcome these constraints and/or create opportunities (i.e. impact of
information campaigns and communications channels used, timing of
introductory and participatory sessions, special training for men on gender
issues, leadership training for women, etc.); (ii) impact of INIFOM staff,
municipal technicians and consultants (used to provide technical assistance) to
promote male and female participation in project activities (impact of training
for staff, staff incentives); and (iii) the strengths and weaknesses of
approaches to integrate men and women in project activities.
Sutiabas
(p)    Resolution of the century long problem of Sutiaba land tenure will involve a
slow process of negotiations and conflict resolution between the Sutiaba and
non-Sutiaba and will not be directly addressed by the project. Land titling
issues would generally be addressed by INRA which is being supported
through the Agricultural Technology and Land Management Project (Credit
No. 2536-NI). The proposed project would help the Sutiaba through two
mechanisms: (i) Sub-project proposals for the preparation of land demarcation
proposals would be eligible for financing and (ii) Indigenous organizations
would be strengtlhened through the training programs and specific capacity
building subprojects that they may propose.
(q)    Regarding participation and municipal investments, the Sutiabas and other
indigenous comrnmunities would be treated differently from non-indigenous
communities. While for non-indigenous people a community is defined as a
"comarca" which is a geographical unit, for indigenous people in the Pacific
and Atlantic, the community would be the indigenous group independently of

their regional coverage. Indigenous traditional decision-making structure
would be the mechanisms for defining priorities and supervising subprojwct
