NICARAGUA
RURAL MUNICIPALITIES PROJECT
ANNEX 4
PROJECT DESCRIPTION1
1. Component I: Rural MuniciDalities Develonment
Background and Rationale
The rural municipalities development component was designed to respond to: (1)
the need to strengthen local institutions in rural areas: local governments, NGOs and
communities; (2) the need to foster economic growth in rural areas where most of the poor are
located; (3) the need to directly provide support to the poorest men and women; and (4) the
need to conserve and restore critical natural resources and protect the local environment.
There were several key considerations underlying the design of this component: (1)
in Nicaragua local institutions are closer to problems of, internalize better and can be more
easily made accountable to the needs of the rural poor; (2) with the proper incentives local
institutions can be more efficient in practicing sustainable management of natural resources;
(3) lagging rural investment is partially due to a local financing system that benefits large
urban centers and that has prevented local rural institutions from developing their decision-
making capacity; (4) given the current assistance programs to reform central government
agencies focusing them on normative aspects there is a need to decentralize the delivery of
local public services and goods; (5) key institutions such as the FISE and NRDP that
presently deliver local investments have a limited time horizon and can only be sustained with a
permanent flow of aid resources; there is a need for long term sustainable alternatives to them;
(6) there is cumulative evidence from Brazil, Bolivia, Mexico and Colombia that if properly
designed, systems for delivering rural development services and addressing rural poverty
through local governments can be quite successful; (7) Nicaragua's strategy as reflected in the
Partial Reform to the Constitution of 1995, in the work of the Decentralization Commission of
Government and in various laws and regulations is to increasingly shift more resources and
decision power to local governments; and (8) local governments already have large
responsibilities over sustainable development in their jurisdictions.
Objectives
The objective of this component is to establish a mechanism based on municipal
governments and community organizations for reducing rural poverty through rural investment
in economic infrastructure, improved natural resource management and small-scale communal
productive activities.
I     This Annex describes some project features that are described in more detail in the following
documents in the project file: Plan de Implementaci6n del Proyecto, Reglamento Operativo, Manual
de Capacitaci6n and Plan Operacional para 1996/1997.



Annex 4
Project Description
Page 2 of 21
Component Description
This component would include the following subcomponents: (A) institutional
development of municipalities; (B) information, participation and training; (C) community and
municipality sub-projects; and (D) institutional development of INIFOM.
(A) Institutional Development of Municipalities (US$5.8 million)
The objective of this subcomponent is to create the minimum technical and
managerial capacity at the municipal level to assist the rural poor, manage natural resources
and protect the environment, to support local economic development through public goods; to
manage the Community and Municipality Subprojects Subcomponent; and to manage the
funds that will be forthcoming later as part of the system of fiscal transfers for disadvantaged
municipalities. The sub-component includes two activities: (1) Creation/strengthening of
Municipal Technical Units; and (2) Training and technical assistance on project management
and municipal affairs.
All project municipalities have been classified relative to each other into one of
three categories (high-A, moderate-B, and low capacity-C) based on their administrative and
financial capacities and the presence of other enabling elements, e.g., NGOs, development
committees, etc. (see Table Al. 1).
Municipal Technical Units
Each municipal government would create a Municipal Technical Unit responsible
for project activities at the local level. The minimnum staffing of the MTU would include (1) a
project's specialist (engineer, agronomist, etc,); (2) a participation specialist; and (3) an
accountant/financial specialist. These staff could be existing staff assigned to those specific
tasks (in type A municipalities), new staff (in type C municipalities), or a combination (in type
B municipalities).(See Table Al. 1 for classification). One of these staff would be nominated
Local Project Coordinator and would be the main contact person for project activities.
The project would finance minor equipment and office upgrading, consultants,
incremental salaries and other recurrent costs to assist municipal with the following activities:
(1) recruitment of staff for and physical establishment of the MTUs; (2) operation of MTUs
for the execution of activities (1) to (13) below; (3) setting-up a subproject monitoring and
evaluation system; and (4) setting-up a system of financial controls and project accounts at the
municipal level.
The functions of the MTU (see Operational Manual), would include:
(1) promoting the project with beneficiaries;
(2) assisting communities set up and operate the Intercommunity Assembly and
the Community Supervision Committee;
(3) promoting, training and assisting Intercommunity Assembly with participatory
planning;
(4) preparing municipal investment plans (MIP) including financial cronogram;
(5) assisting communities and municipalities with project preparation;



Annex 4
Project Description
Page 3 of 21
(6) carrying out procurement activities on behalf of INIFOM, including
maintaining roster of local consultants and NGOs and assisting communities
with their recruitment;
(7) reviewing subproject documentation, appraise subprojects and make
recommendation to municipal council;
(8) detecting technical assistance needs of subproject proponents and assist them
with technical assistance plan;
(9) supervising all subprojects and issuing reports of physical advances;
(10) maintaining the municipal subproject monitoring system;
(11) preparing municipal technical assistance plans and channeling these to
INIFOM;
(12) reporting to INIFOM on the project's progress at the municipal level and
requesting disbursements into municipal accounts;
(13) maintaining project accounts at the municipal level;
Technical assistance and training for municipal government.
The project would finance consultants, seminars, workshops, study tours and other
training expenses for the MTUs and other municipal staff in the following thematic areas:
(1) the subproject cycle, including on the identification, preparation, appraisal,
implementation and operation of subprojects in various thematic areas;
(2) participatory methods including participatory planning;
(3) environment and land use plans and design and planning of rural development,
natural resources and enviromment programs;
(4) gender and indigenous peoples related issues;
(5) accounting, auditing and financial management related to the project and
municipal operation in general;
(6) subproject monitoring, evaluation and reporting;
(7) procurement for works, goods and consultants and NGOs at the local level;
(8) local revenue generation and cost recovery for maintenance activities;
(9) personnel recruitment and management;
(10) municipal administration;
(11) legal assistance
Agreement to receive training and/or technical assistance in the thematic areas (1)
through (7) would be a condition of municipal eligibility for the Community and Municipality
Subprojects Sub-component. These training programs would be organized and managed by
the Directorate of Technical Assistance of INIFOM, which would recruit consultants or NGOs
to provide the services and/or do the quality control over the programs. Training and technical
assistance on the thematic areas (8) through (11) would be demand driven. INIFOM would
receive demand from municipal governments in this area and recruit consultants or set up
training programs, pooling municipalities whenever possible.
(B) Information, Participation and Training (USS2.8 million)
The objectives of this subcomponent are (1) to make the rights and responsibilities
of communities and local governments widely known to increase the level of community



Annex 4
Project Description
Page 4 of 21
control and local transparency and accountability; (2) to increase the supply of local technical
services to support communities and municipalities; and (3) to generate increased demand for
subprojects. This sub-component includes three activities: (1) promotion and dissemination of
project; (2) creation and operation of community participatory structures and participatory
planning; and (3) training of community leaders, NGOs and local consultants.
There would be two kinds of communities eligible for participatory planning and to
propose projects. For most population community would be defined as the "comarca" which
is the smallest geographical unit commonly recognized in Nicaragua. A typical comarca
includes about 100 families, while a municipality could have about 50 comarcas. For
indigenous communities, the indigenous traditional community structures would be treated as
commnunities for project purposes.
Promotion and dissemination of project at the local level
The project would finance consultants, dissemination materials, seminars,
workshops and travel expenses for the promotion and dissemination of the project at the
community level through town meetings, radio, TV, videos, written materials, other infornal
communication devices and seminars and workshops for community members, leaders and
notables. Promotion and dissemination activities would take place periodically and would
include at least the following thematic areas:
(1) Overall project description including objectives, components, resources and
responsibilities, aspects of operational manual and cronogram of
implementation;
(2) Community participation aspects including commnunity participatory
structures and participatory planning;
(3) Women and indigenous peoples issues;
(4) Environmental issues including role of environmental and land use plans;
(5) Examples of project successes and failures in other communities including
types of subprojects approved, procurement arrangements, subproject costs,
etc.
Promotion and dissemination activities would be the joint responsibility of MTUs
and INIFOM, which would recruit NGOs or consultants to assist with the process and develop
dissemination materials. A community member would be designated as project promoter at the
community level and would be the community focal point for promotion and dissemination.
Intercommunity Assembly and Community Supervision Committee and Participatory
Planning
The project would finance consultants and travel expenses to assist with the
creation and/or operation of the Intercommunity Assembly and the Community Supervision
Committee and with participatory planning events.
The Intercommunity Assembly is the key community structure at the municipal
level. It is composed of community representatives (comprising at least 70% of all the
communities within a municipality) and may include also representatives of local NGOs,



Annex 4
Project Description
Page 5 of 21
religious groups, producer associations, and local notables (priest, teacher, nurse, etc.) . The
key functions of the Intercommunity Assembly are:
(1) to priorize the needs of the various communities into an overall expression of
community needs and priorities at the municipal level and to solve conflicts
between different communities for project resources;
(2) to define the general strategy for rural development;
(3) to discuss, endorse and support the Municipal Investment Plan to be financed
by the project;
(4) to elect members of the Community Supervision Committee and to review
their reports.
The Community Supervision Committee is a small structure in charge of ensuring
that community interests and the interests of rural areas are adequately taken into account. It
will have the same number of members as tiue Municipal Council. Each member will be
elected for one year. The project will pay a small fixed fee per member for each meeting
attended to cover travel expenses and opportunity costs of labor. The key functions of the
Community Supervision Committee are:
(1) supervise project execution at the municipal level on behalf of communities;
(2) report to the Intercommunity Assembly and INIFOM on project execution and
issue certificates of sub-project completion to the MTU and INIFOM;
(3) oversee municipality/community agreements and compliance with project
objectives.
The activities related to the establishment and operation of the Intercommunity
Assembly and Community Supervision Committee would be jointly executed by the MTUs and
INIFOM, which would recruit consultants to assist with the process.
Participatory planning would be the process for prioritizing community problems
and needs and start suggesting solutions to these problems. Participatory planning would be
carried out by the community with facilitators which would include both the MTU and
consultants or NGOs recruited by INIFOM to assist with the process.
Community members would meet to carry out participatory planning exercises to
agree on needs and investment priorities and to elect community representatives to the
Intercommunity Assembly (IA). The MTU would integrate priorities of each community
within each municipality into a municipal investment plan (MIP), which could include also
projects identified by one or more municipal governments. The MTUs would also take into
account recommendations from the Environment and Land Use Plans (ELUP). MIPs would
be submitted to the Intercommunity Assembly (IA) for discussion and priorization of
investments among communities. The Community Supervision Committee (CSC) would be
responsible for advising the municipal government, monitoring the implementation of the
municipal investment plan and informing conmmunities of progress. The MIPs would be
approved by Municipal Councils and submitted to the Regional Delegation of INIFOM for
non-objection and confirmation of eligibility for IDA financing or for other financing sources.



Annex 4
Project Description
Page 6 of 21
Training for and Registry of Service Providers
The project would finance equipment, consultants, seminars, workshops, rental of
facilities, training materials, study tours and travel expenses for training programs (Pacific and
Atlantic) to build the capacity among relevant professionals of local NGOs, private sector and
government agencies on:
(1)  all aspects of the subproject cycle including identification, preparation,
appraisal, implementation and operation.
(2)  participatory planning, project identification and preparation methodologies in
the context of the proposed project;
(3)  technical aspects of natural resources and rural water projects subprojects and
on use and interpretation of environmental and land use plans;
(4)  application and transfer of technical systems for sustainable resource
management;
(5)  project and fund administration, management and monitoring and evaluation
The training program is designed to ensure that the professional skills of natural
resource management specialists and sub-project managers are adequate for sub-project needs.
This component is targeted at local NGOs, private sector and government operating at the
local level who must assist communities and municipalities with all aspects of the subproject
cycle from identification to operation and who will ultimately exert technical control over the
subprojects. In the past the provision of opportunity to maintain and/or upgrade the
professional skills of this group has been largely ignored.

The project would also finance consultants for the development and maintenance of
a registry of successful course graduates and other NGOs or professionals qualified for
providing technical assistance to municipalities and communities on all aspects of the sub-
project cycle, participatory planning and the thematic areas associated with the project. This
registry would be maintained in INIFOM and each MTU.
(C) Grants for Community and Municipality Subprojects (US$23.4 million)
The project would finance matching grants for small subprojects proposed by
communities and municipalities in the Pacific and Atlantic regions.
Eligible Subproject Categories. There would be a broad range of acceptable sub-
projects falling in the following categories: (1) environment and natural resources; (2)
municipal infrastructure; (3) community infrastructure; (4) productive; and (5) capacity
building. Examples are given in Chapter 3 of the SAR and in Table A5.2. Prototypes of
several of these subprojects are in the project file.

Restrictions on subprojects. Consistently with the project's focus on rural
development through the provision of local public goods, poverty alleviation and environmental
protection, the following restrictions would apply:



Annex 4
Project Description
Page 9 of 21
(1) subprojects in towns of more than 10,000 inhabitants do not qualify;
(2) at least 60% of funds invested annually are for sub-projects outside of the
head town of the municipality;
(3) activities of second-level organizations (e.g., NGOs, governnent line agencies,
universities, private sector firms, etc) which are not first requested by and
directly linked to municipalities, communities or community-level groups do
not qualify;
(4) activities which could cause irreversible environmental damage (e.g.,
conversion of forest to other uses; drainage of wetlands; channelization of
rivers and streams; use of agrochemicals with a WHO rating of 'A') would not
qualify;
(5) beneficiaries must be community organizations or groups of at least 10
households; individual activities do not qualify;
(6) subprojects otherwise financeable by banks or other lending institutions do not
qualify; compliance with this restriction would be checked during project
audits and supervision;
(7) the maximum cost per beneficiary is US$700.
Subproject cycle. Responsibilities over the subproject cycle would be the
following:
(1) Subproject identification The project ideas would have been defined and pre-
approved in the MIPs. The MTUs would assist communities with further
identification of projects for which the services of local private consultants,
NGOs or government officials qualified through the registry of service
providers could be contracted. The identification phase would also include
defining the Project User Group (PUG), those community members with a
stake in the subproject and that later would supervise or execute its
implementation, operation and maintenance.
(2) Subproject preparation Subproject preparation would be the responsibility
of the MTUs for municipality subprojects or MTUs and/or PUGs for
community subprojects. In most instances MTUs and legally established
communities would hire qualified private consultants, NGOs or government
agencies to assist communities and MTUs with project preparation. Projects
would cover the costs of consultants or travel expenses of government staff.
Projects would be prepared according to guidelines included in the Training
Manual and the project proposal would need to include a (a) management
plan; (b) operation, maintenance and, if the case, cost recovery plan; (c)
technical assistance plan; (d) co-financing p!an identifying sources of
financing; (e) simple monitorable indicators; and (f) the sub-project agreement
and other signed letters and agreements depending on the case and according
to the formats in the Operational Manual (Reglamento Interno).



Annex 4
Project Description
Page 10of21
(3) Subproject appraisal and approval. The Municipal Technical Unit would
review all project proposals and recommend to the Municipal Council
approval, rejection or further preparation. The MC would approve all
subprojects that: (a) meet community priorities as outlined in MPs; (b)
include acceptable management, operation, cost recovery and technical
assistance plans; (c) include criteria for monitoring and evaluation; (d) are
technically sound; (e) include co-financing by beneficiaries and/or
municipalities; (f) meet environmental criteria; (g) fit poverty objectives; and
(h) target rural areas. All projects of US$25,000 or less would not require
further approval. All projects of US$50,000 of graduated municipalities
would not require further approval. Projects not under those thresholds would
have to be approved by INIFOM and environmentally cleared by MARENA.
For the appraisal of projects INIFOM would request the assistance of line
agencies - INTA, NRDP, INAA, FISE, MCT. - and could also recruit
consultants to assist with the appraisal.
(4) Subproject implementation. Subproject implementation would also be the
responsibility of the MTUs and communities. Projects could be either
implemented by legally established communities themselves, by contractors
recruited by the MTUs on behalf of communities, or by a combination of the
two. Project implementation would in specific cases include training on
project operation, maintenance and cost recovery. All projects would be
supervised by the MTU and communities and/or Project User Groups
established. All projects greater than US$50,000 and samples of smaller
projects would be also supervised by the Regional Delegation of INIFOM.
(5) Operation, maintenance and cost recovery. Operation, maintenance and
cost-recovery would be the responsibility of legally established communities
and/or municipal governments depending on the project and the particular
model to be adopted would be described in the project proposal. For the
specific case of productive projects, cost-recovery into community funds
would be promoted and the project would finance technical assistance and
training on the management of these funds. These rotating funds would be the
mechanisms for spreading the benefits of private goods projects over the
largest number of community members in order to avoid conflicts and build up
the social capital in communities.
Municipality eligibility
Municipal eligibility. To become eligible for the sub-projects municipalities would
have to enter into an eligibility and financing agreement with INIFOM (a sample is included in
the Draft Operational Manual) in which they agree, prior to eligibility for sub-project grants,
to:
(1) follow the rules in the Operational Manual;
(2) create or strengthen the Municipal Technical Unit;



Annex 4
Project Description
Page 11 of 21
(3) establish project accounts and bring financial controls up to Bank standards as
per monitoring indicators and criteria and action plan presented in the
background document "Evaluaci6n/Diagn6stico Contable de 32 Municipios,
INIFOMy MARENA" which is in the project file;
(4) establish and operate a project monitoring and evaluation system as described
inAnnex 10;
(5) promote the project with communities and assist communities setting up and
operating the community structures and follow participatory planning methods
as discussed under Subcomponent (B);
(6) develop municipal investment plans respecting community priorities and the
environment and land use plans (ELUP) discussed below
(7) when specific financing is provided, signing imnplementation agreements for
subprojects.
Financing Ceilings. Financing ceilings play three roles: (1) by imposing a budget
constraint on municipal governments and communities they would force these to prioritize and
optimize their portfolio and to develop trade-off analysis and conflict resolution mechanisms,
in the process helping strengthen local decision-making structures; (2) they would avoid
biasing funding toward the larger and better situated municipalities which are more capable of
presenting sound subproject proposals; and (3) they would permit to better target project
resources toward meeting poverty and environmental objectives. Each year there would be a
financing ceiling per municipality calculated on the basis of a formula applied to a total annual
budget that increases from the first to the last year of the project. The results (in percentage)
are presented in Figure A1. 1. These caps will be reviewed and modified annually based on
performance and demand. The indicators and their weights are:
(1) poverty index, using the FISE index of "unsatisfied basic needs" - 42%;
(2) necessity index, using municipality's rural population as a proxy for necessity
- 34%;
(3) economic efficiency index using as proxies: municipal tax collections - 14%;
and the total community counterpart (in-kind and cash) from previous year's
municipal development investment program - 10%.
Penalties and incentives. See para 6.17 of the SAR for the proposed penalties and
incentives.
Local co-financing. All subprojects (with the exception of capacity building sub-
projects) would require matching funds from beneficiaries, whether farmner groups,
communities, NGOs or municipalities, in accordance with criteria listed in Table I of the SAR.
Environmental review of subprojects. The process for environrmental review of
sub-projects is discussed in the background document "Sistema de Procedimientos
Ambientales para la Gesti6n Ambiental Municipal" The environmental review of subprojects
would be carried out by the MTU for all subprojects and by the MTU and MARENA for
projects greater than US$50,000 or subprojects included in risk 1II category (see below). At
the Municipal-level technical unit staff would review preliminary proposals against pre-
determined inclusion and exclusion criteria and ascertain the proposal's coherence viz. the

Page 12 of 21
municipal environment and land use plans. Those passing this stage would be required to
complete a simple form which describes project activities, geographic location of each activity
and the presence of sensitive areas (rivers, protected areas, forests, etc), and which explicitly
defines the projects consistency with the ELUP and national environmental legislation; and
how subproject interventions would be maintained following subproject completion.

Page 13 of 21
MTUs would categorize the subproject based on Decree 45/95. Projects would be
classified as: (1) Category I - subprojects without negative environmental impact; does not
require an EA; (2) Category II - subprojects with limited potential negative impact; requires
an EA; explicit approval not required; (3) Category III - requires a full ETA and environmental
management plan, completed by a qualified professional and approval by MARENA. For
category I and II proposals, the MTU could assign up to 20% of total estimated microproject
cost to contract the required work. In the case of microprojects with environmental analyses
and/or need for MARENA approval, the MTU would be responsible to obtain that approval.
Table A1. 13 lists the main environmental risks associated with subproject categories.
Subproject Operation Phase: The major environmental issues during operation
would relate to soil erosion, soil nutrient management, agrochemical usage, natural forest
management, and overexploitation of resources. The following measures would be taken to
reduce risks and avoid or mitigate the potential negative impacts:
(1) a project-wide, four year, ongoing training and technical assistance program
targeted at all natural resource professionals and higher/mid-level technicians
in the project areas on the subproject cycle would include compliance with
environmental norms and regulations and best management practices to meet
them. This would include a review of implementation experiences and
systemization of best management practices for dissemination by INTA and
project activities;
(2) subprojects with environmental risks would include a TA plan;
(3) subprojects would include (a) financing of formal/informal environmental
education programs to raise key local issues to the level of public debate and
educate populace on altematives and trade-offs; and (b) financing of applied
research activities to address specific concerns such as: adapted crop varieties;
soil/crop specific fertilizer trials to rationalize usage; validation of IPM
practices; and monitoring of regenerative capacities of managed resources;
(4) annual independent reviews of selected microprojects in each municipality,
which include technical and environmental audits.
(D) Strengthening of INIFOM (US$3.3 million).
The objectives of this sub-component are to help develop: (I) the conditions in
INIFOM to implement the component and (2) a legal framework to ensure project
sustainability.
Strengthening of INIFOM
The project would finance minor office works, equipment, consultants, studies,
seminars and workshops, study tours and incremental salaries and other recurrent costs for the:
(1) Directorate of Planning which will be responsible for implementation of the
Rural Municipality Development Component; the project will cover the costs
of a Planning Advisor, Procurement Advisor, Accountant and
Disbursements Officer and Project Auditor.

Component II. Natural Resources Policy and Institutions
Background and Rationale
The Natural Resources Policies and Institutions Component was designed to
increase the capacity at the central government level to ensure that public and private
actors internalize environmental concerns in their decisions. This is particularly
important in the context of the decentralization process to local governments and
communities supported by component I. Local institutions are known for inadequately
considering the costs of their actions on other jurisdictions, and this is particularly so in
the case of the environment where there are substantial regional, national and global
externalities. Hence it is necessary for central government to keep track of these effects
and through a variety of instruments prevent local jurisdictions from taking actions that
cause more harm on others than the benefits that accrue to them.
The second important consideration in the design of both this component and
of component III - the associated Atlantic Biodiversity Corridor Project to be financed by
GEF - was to assist government in putting in place a legal and institutional framework
that would create the necessary conditions, as stated in Bank policies, for future Bank
supported investment projects in the natural resources sectors, and in particular in
forestry and coastal zone management.
Objectives
The key objective of this component is to ensure that GON and local
institutions and communities take into account important regional, national and global



Annex 4
Project Description
Page 17 of 21
environment aspects in their decisions by improving the policy and institutional
framework for natural resources.
Component Description
This component consists of a small technical assistance program covering
two different areas: (A) strengthening of MARENA; and (B) interinstitutional
assistance program.
(A) Strengthening of MARENA (US$3.0 million)
The objective of this subcomponent is to increase the institutional capacity of
MARENA in the areas of (1) policy; (2) environmental planning and monitoring; and (3)
protected area management. The environmental planning and monitoring activities would
help develop an information system identifying critical environmental risks at the local
level that will be used to assist municipal governments with their investment decisions
under component I and that will enable MARENA to object to risky investments for the
environment in the context of components I and III. The project would finance
equipment, minor office upgrading, consultants, seminars and workshops, study tours
and incremental salaries and operating costs for:
(1) Strengthening the recently created Directorate of Planning and within it
the Divisions of Policy, Planning and Interinstitutional Relations and
the Directorate of Protected Areas. The project would finance
advisors in international agreements, participation, indigenous issues,
and NGOs, policy and protected area management.
(2) Advice, studies, consultations and dissemination for the elaboration of
legal proposals, regulations and studies covering the following themes:
(a) regulation of General Environment Law; (b) proposal and regulation
of a Protected Area and/or Biodiversity Law; (c) proposal and
regulation of a Forestry Law; (d) contributions to the proposal and
regulation of a Fishery Law; (e) legal or regulatory initiatives for the
harmonization of decentralization, natural resources and indigenous
peoples legislation; (g) analysis of relations between rural poverty and
environmental degradation and other unidentified studies.
(3) Strenghtening the Regional Delegations of MARENA in Le6n and
Chinandega and in the Atlantic region to enable them to perform their
functions under the project in support of components I and III.
(4) Elaboration, consultations and dissemination of Municipal
Environment and Land Use Plans (3 plans) for Le6n, Chinandega and
San Carlos. The ELUPs are an important contribution to the Municipal
Development Component as they will make information available to
local governments and communities regarding environmental risks and
threats and areas of highest potential productivity.
(5) Elaboration, consultations and dissemination of an Environment and
Land Use Plan for the Atlantic including the delineation of the Atlantic
Biodiversity Corridor (see Component III). The Atlantic ELUP will be a

critical zones. The output from this process would be an instrument to be utilized in the
local microproject approval process.
(B) Inter-institutional Assistance Program (US$2.1 million)
The NEAP identified lack of capacity to design, implement and
enforce environmental policy as the key problem underlying environmental
degradation in Nicaragua and called for an integrated program of multi-
institutional support to increase capacity of several agencies in Nicaragua to
handle environmental issues. The objective of this component is to increase the
capacity of MARENA to assist other government agencies with implementation
and enforcement of environmental policies.
The project would finance consultants, seminars and workshops, study

Component III. Atlantic Biodiversity Corridor
Background and Rationale
The Atlantic slope of Nicaragua is biologically an extremely rich area of
lowland tropical rain forests, coastal wetlands, pine savannas, and higher altitude pine-
oak forests. These ecoregions and ecosystems have high global importance on their own
merits, but in addition, they form part of a critical link in a larger Mesoamerican
Biological Corridor (MBC) linking North America, Central America, and South
America. Although this area in Nicaragua is one of the most intact natural areas
remaining in Central America, there is no assurance that key components will be
protected in the long-term as part of a viable protected areas system. It is now threatened
by colonists spilling over to the Atlantic from Nicaragua's more densely inhabited Pacific
slope.
The Atlantic Biodiversity Corridor (ABC) represents the Nicaraguan portion
of the Mesoamerican Biological Corridor. Support for an ABC has long been a national
priority in Nicaragua. The Tropical Forestry Action Plan and the National
Environmental Action Plan, both completed in the early 1990's, explicitly recognized the
importance of strengthening protected areas in the Atlantic slope and maintaining the
intactness of this last large area of natural habitat in Central America. Nicaragua's
Ministry of the Environment (MARENA) has issued a draft version of the country's
Biodiversity Strategy and this document is now undergoing a process of public
consultation. The Strategy document identifies the ABC as a top national biodiversity
priority.
There is widespread concern among national, regional, and local governments
that the current patterns of frontier development on the Atlantic slope are unsustainable,
non-economic and are the source of substantial social problems. Moreover, there is
insufficient control over logging and mining concessions in the region, in part because
Nicaragua has not yet put in place the legal and institutional framework to minimize
environmental risks from these activities, which could become potentially important in
the Atlantic. More importantly there are serious issues of indigenous land and natural
resources tenure.
Large portions of these ecoregions in the proposed ABC are presently
included in protected areas. However, in most of these protected areas, there are no
management activities, protection is weak or non-existent, the legal status is unclear,
there is legal confusion over indigenous rights and responsabilities and the actual
boundaries on the ground are ill-defined and unmarked. Support for protected areas and
their buffer zones in this part of Nicaragua is being provided by a variety of donors and
aid agencies including USAID, GTZ, the European Union, the Danish, Dutch, Canadian,
and Austrian governnents, The Nature Conservancy (TNC), World Wildlife Fund
(WWF), the Caribbean Conservation Corporation, and others.



Annex 4
Project Description
Page 20 of 21
Objectives
This component (associated project) seeks to ensure the long-term
conservation and sustainable use of the biodiversity resources of an Atlantic Biodiversity
Corridor (ABC) through the Atlantic slope of Nicaragua. Such a corridor would be a
critical link in a larger Mesoamerican Biological Corridor (MBC). The Nicaraguan
ecoregions and ecosystems within the proposed ABC have high global importance on
their own merits and as a whole, represent the most intact natural habitats remaining in
Central America.
Component Description
To achieve the objectives outlined above, the proposed project is organized
into three components. The definition of the ABC itself is one face of a multi-faceted land
use strategy for the entire Atlantic slope. This planning exercise, taking into account both
biological and socioeconormic considerations, is currently being undertaken with GON
and GEF Project Development Fund (PDF) resources. The three components are:
(A) Involvement of Local Communities, Indigenous Groups, NGOs and
Governments in Biodiversity Management
The project would finance consultants and training for: (i) development of and
promoting the project with indigenous and non-indigenous communities in the Atlantic
region; (ii) training indigenous and non-indigenous communities in sustainable
biodiversity use and protection; (iii) assisting indigenous committees in pursuing formal
recognition for their land and natural resources rights; (iv) training and technical
assistance to NGOs, and municipal and regional governments for the implementation of
the ABC strategy; and (v) training of central government agencies on the ABC strategy.
An Indigenous Peoples Development Plan (IPDP) is currently under preparation.
(B) Biodiversity Planning, Information, Monitoring and Evaluation
The project would finance equipment, consultants, studies, workshops, training
and dissemination materials, travel expenses and incremental salaries and other recurrent
costs for (i) continuing the ABC planning activities; (ii) implementing a biodiversity
monitoring system in coordination with communities and local and regional governments
to warn of threats to biodiversity; and (iii) establishing a system for evaluating potential
impacts on the ABC of development activities such as roads and other infrastructure and
logging, mining and fishing concessions and for proposing and enforcing mitigating
measures.
(C) Financial Mechanism for the Sustainability of the ABC2
The project would finance equipment, consultants, training, workshops,
seminars, studies and study tours for: (i) the operation of a financing mechanism to
promote the long-term sustainability of the ABC including fund rising; and (ii) grants for
sub-projects covering the following themes:
2      The nature of the financing mechanism Nill be defined during preparation of this
component.

* Management of protected areas, including infrastructure, training,
management plans and recurrent costs related to the maintenance of the

* Subprojects of sustainable use of biodiversity submitted by indigenous and

NICARAGUA
RURAL MUNICIPALITIES PROJECT
ANNEX 5
INDIGENOUS PEOPLES STRATEGY FOR THE ATLANTIC REGION
1. OVERVIEW OF INDIGENOUS PEOPLES
In 1993, the estimated indigenous population of Nicaragua was 231,727
inhabitants, representing 5.2 percent of the total. The greatest concentration of indigenous
people is found in the Atlantic region where Miskito, Sumu, Rama and Garifona peoples live.
The Miskito, with over 110,00 people, are the most numerous of indigenous peoples. In fact,
in 1993, they represented over 58 percent of the population of the North Atlantic region and
close to 48 percent of the total indigenous population of the country. In contrast, in the Pacific
region, there are also several indigenous peoples, among them the Subtiaba, Monimb6,
Matagalpa, Sebaco and Jinotega, but they are not as numerous as those in the Atlantic.
In the heavily populated Pacific and Northern regions indigenous groups have had
for a long timne to adapt to the dominant non-indigenous populations and have lost access to
some of their traditional lands and cultural identity. In the Atlantic Coast, on the other hand,
with a low population density and vast areas of pristine habitats, indigenous peoples for the
most part still have access to their traditional territories and have been able to maintain the
essence of their traditional lifestyles.
A fundamental issue that needs to be solved in Nicaragua relates to indigenous land
and natural resources rights. Clarifying these rights could go a long way in contributing to
economic growth in Nicaragua. Until indigenous land and natural resources rights issues are
solved in the resource rich Atlantic it will be impossible for Nicaragua to pursue careful
exploitation and sustainable use of its minerals, forests and fishery. Potential investors will be
scared away, resource values will not be maximized, and conflict and gridlock will prevent
indigenous communities from benefiting fully from resource use and GON from enforcing
important environmental regulations.
The problem is not one of Nicaragua's willingness to advance in the recognition of
indigenous land and natural resources rights. To the contrary, public statements and recent
initiatives demonstrate that there is good will in working towards a solution to these problems.
But due to the less acute social problems in the Atlantic and the formidable task of building a
democracy in a war ravaged and very poor country, indigenous issues have not been a top
priority and efforts have been channeled instead towards land tenure and titling, poverty
alleviation and demobilization of soldiers elsewhere in the country. Moreover, given the
limited capacity of specialized institutions to address the many policy and legal inconsistencies
affecting indigenous peoples rights, advances have been slow.



Annex 5
Indigenous Peoples Strategy for the Atlantic Region
Page 2 of 8
2. OBSTACLES TO THE RECOGNITION OF INDIGENOUS LAND AND
NATURAL RESOURCES RIGHTS IN THE ATLANTIC'
MARENA commissioned two studies to (i) identify main obstacles for advancing
with the recognition of indigenous land and natural resource rights contemplated in the
Constitution; and (ii) to propose a strategy/action plan for the government for removing those
obstacles. These studies were commissioned as part of the Indigenous Peoples Development
Plan for the Atlantic which is required before appraisal of the GEF component (see below).
The main conclusions/recommendations of these studies are briefly summarized below.
The Legal And Institutional Context
There are good prospects for the recognition of indigenous land and natural
resources rights in the Atlantic:
*  There is a solid constitutional and legal basis in the Constitution and
Autonomy Law of 1987 that guarantees the rights of indigenous communities
of the Atlantic to their traditional territories and natural resources contained in
them.
-  There is a clear political will, expressed by various GON agencies and the
President of the Republic, to advance with the recognition of the traditional
rights of indigenous communities over land and use and management of natural
resources in those lands.
*  There is a high level of awareness in the indigenous communities themselves
over their territorial rights as a historical and legal commitment of the State
and an understanding that these rights entail substantial autonomy in the
management of community matters and use and disposition of natural
resources.
*  There is a clear political and administrative conscience of regional governments
of the importance of recognizing traditional indigenous land rights consistently
with historical commitments and existing norms.
*  There are solid indigenous organizations and indigenous representations at the
level of municipal and regional governments that will facilitate the process of
recognizing indigenous land claims.
Main Limitations and Difficulties
There are legal, admrinistrative, technical, socio-economic and cultural obstacles to
advancing with the recognition of indigenous land and natural resources rights.
I      See background report in project file "Viabilidady Condiciones Legales de Cumplimiento de
un Componente de Legalizaci6n de Tierras a las Comunidades Indigenas de la Costa Atldntica
Dentro del Proyecto de Manejo de Recursos Naturales - IPDP" by Roque RoldAn, December 1995.



Annex 5
Indigenous Peoples Strategy for the Atlantic Region
Page 3 of 8
Legally the main obstacles are the following:
*  There is no legal scheme defined in the agrarian or indigenous legislation prior
to the Constitution and Autonomy Law of 1987 for the administration of
community lands, other than the one specified under the Civil Code and in
various laws and decrees published since the beginning of the century and
which were applied to the different situation of indigenous conmmunities of the
Pacific. This Civil Code model (communal property) represents a contract
between community members as co-owners of an asset, but is insufficient for
the administration of a territory historically belonging to a culturally
differentiated group, as is the case with communities in the Atlantic. The 1987
Autonomy Law, on the other hand, contemplated the indigenous community
land property right as inalienable and indescriptable and not subject to sale,
donation, seizure or attachment.
*  There is no clarity as to the competencies over property rights, use and
management of natural resources between Central Government, regional
governments and indigenous communities. There are, however, dispersed
throughout various pieces of legislation, enough elements to permit a
clarification of most legal issues affecting indigenous property and natural
resources. Unfortunately this process of legal clarification has not yet taken
place in Nicaragua.
T [NRA is the public entity in charge of titling of agrarian lands and of
redistributing national lands and "tierras baldfas". The agrarian reform titles
issued by INRA have all the restrictions applicable cable to agrarian reform
(size, intended use, etc.). However, in no agrarian legislation is INRA
explicitly given responsibility over assigning commnunity lands to indigenous
communities under community titles. Hence, although the 1995 Constitution
and the 1987 Autonomy Law recognize indigenous rights, there is no agency
explicitly in charge of executing community titles, nor have procedures been
defined for demarcation and titling or certification.
*  There may exist discrepancies between the municipality, a
geographic/administrative unit, and the indigenous territory, subject to its own
community government, once these are created.
*  There may exist conflicts between community natural resources rights obtained
through the process of recognition of community lands and the restrictions
associated with various types of protected areas when these overlap with
indigenous communities.
*  There are no clear procedures for granting juridical personality to communities.
Administratively, lack of procedures for recognizing indigenous land titles creates
a climate of uncertainty that affects key GON agencies with responsibilities that interact with
indigenous communities in the Atlantic, namely INRA, MARENA, MAS and regional
governments. Although all these institutions need to participate in the process of recognizing



Annex 5
Indigenous Peoples Strategy for the Atlantic Region
Page 4 of 8
community lands, the responsibilities and administrative roles of each one have not been
defined.
Technically, there is no definition of the traditional communal lands of each group
nor an inventory of the Atlantic regarding its resources and most appropriate land use. This is
important to estimate the size of the territories needed by communities to ensure subsistence.
There are different views regarding the types of territorial or land rights that should be
recognized to communities: by community, by ethnic group, by watershed. There is no
definition of the most appropriate methods for measuring territories: traditional topography,
aerophotography, mapping or a combination of these.
Socially, there is no clarity over how to handle the cases of minority communities
that have already lost some of their territories, either because traditional lands were assigned to
settlers or occupied by other indigenous communities. There is an incomplete inventory,
although some inforrnation does exist, about conflicts between different indigenous
communities or groups of communities in particular regarding the use of trails and natural
resources in the areas claimed. Such an inventory is essential for the definition of conflict
resolution mechanisms as part of the procedures for legal recognition of indigenous land titles.
Culturally, there is lack of information about the community organization and
government that would facilitate the definition of the management model for lands under
commnunity and the customary law management.
Key recommendations for government
The study's key recommendations for GON were the following:
(1) To finalize the collection and analysis of legal instruments related to
indigenous land and natural resource rights, including (i) treaties and
agreements with foreign governments; (ii) Constitutional texts, laws, decrees
and other legal initiatives of the State that affect the lifestyle of indigenous
communities, and traditional forms of government and land tenure; (iii)
decisions of the judicial system related to the rights of indigenous
communities; (iv) official opinions of central government on the matter; and
(v) agrarian legislation applied to indigenous communities of the Pacific.
(partially done in background study)
(2) To carry out a complete inventory of all land titles granted in the Atlantic for
both indigenous and non-indigenous inhabitants and in particular titles granted
under the Harrison-Altmirano Treaty, Agrarian Reform titles, and titles
granted by the judicial system.
(3) To carry out a compilation of customary law.
(4) To conclude the diagnostics of the ethnic groups of the Atlantic initiated by
INRA and MARENA.
(5) To carry out an inventory of territories available to sustain the livelihoods of
minority conmmunities such as the Sumus, Rama and Garifonas.



Annex 5
Indigenous Peoples Strategy for the Atlantic Region
Page 5 of 8
(6) To propose a law defining the property regime for indigenous conmnunities
referred to in Article 107 of the Constitution (tentatively referred to in here as
the Indigenous Land Law) and clarifying the rights that indigenous
communities in their lands have over forests, water, wildlife, etc.
(7) To clarify the responsibilities and obligations of indigenous commnunities, the
regional government and MARENA in the administration of natural resources
in indigenous lands and include these responsibilities in the proposed
Indigenous Land Law and in legislation affecting natural resources.
(8) To review various legislative initiatives related to natural resources (forestry,
water, fisheries, protected areas, energy, etc.) to more precisely define aspects
with implications for indigenous peoples and ensure consistency with their
constitutional rights.
(9) To define the procedures for legally recognizing indigenous land rights,
defining responsibilities, community participation, etc., including procedures
for legally recognizing indigenous communities.
(10) To create a special structure involving several agencies under INRA
leadership and including regional support committees and a technical support
group in charge of preparing and reviewing proposals for decisions over the
various indigenous land cases and to ensure coordination between the various
GON agencies and regional governments in the process.
4. GOVERNMENT'S STRATEGY FOR DEALING WITH INDIGENOUS LAND
AND NATURAL RESOURCES RIGHTS
Government's Action Plan
GON is presently discussing the results of the studies mentioned above as well as
other similar studies commissioned by INRA and plans to carry out workshops to generate a
broad consensus over priorities for action and assignment of institutional responsibilities for
their execution. This Action Plan, expected to be completed before appraisal of the GEF
project, and to constitute an integral part of the Indigenous Peoples Development Plan being
prepared under the GEF component, would be supported by IDA through country dialogue in
the context of the CAS due in 1997, and by on-going and proposed operations namely (i) the
Agricultural Technology and Land Management Project (Credit No. 2536-NI); (ii) the
Nicaragua Indigenous Peoples IDF Grant No. 028902; (iii) the proposed IDA financed
project; and (iv) the proposed GEF financed project. Preparation funds for the GEF project
would help finance the workshops for the preparation of the Action Plan.
Country dialogue
The issue of how to support GON in the medium-term (1997-2000) in advancing
with the recognition of indigenous land rights would be discussed in the context of the Country
Assistance Strategy due in FY97. If needed and agreed by GON and IDA the FY97 CAS
would more specifically detail the future IDA strategy for assisting GON with this issue.



Annex 5
Indigenous Peoples Strategy for the Atlantic Region
Page 6 of 8
Agricultural Technology and Land Management Project (Credit No. 2536-NI)
INRA, one of the two implementing agencies of this on-going project, is completing
an action plan for solving technical, legal and procedural obstacles to recognition of
indigenous land rights and awarding of indigenous titles and is also carrying out a diagnostic
study of indigenous people in the Atlantic region. INRA is likely to be the lead agency chosen
for advancing with this issue although it needs to be supported through a higher level
comrnmission including other GON agencies, narnely MARENA, MAS, INETER and Regional
Governments and representatives of key stakeholders. INRA is presently reprogramming its
activities in the context of the on-going project to give a higher priority to indigenous land
titling during its second phase, which is about to start.
Indigenous Peoples IDF Grant No. 028902
The Indigenous Peoples Grant being implemented by MAS includes training and
workshops for indigenous peoples on a variety of issues. Aspects of indigenous land and
natural resources rights are being included in the thematic topics covered.
Proposed IDA and GEF projects
As discussed elsewhere in this report the proposed project includes two distinct
operations to be processed under separate project cycles: the Municipal Development
Component and the Natural Resources and Policies Component, to be supported by IDA, and
the Atlantic Biodiversity Corridor Component, to be supported by GEF. For ease of
exposition the IDA components will be referred to as the IDA project and the ABC component
as the GEF project. Geographically the proposed IDA project is split between the Westem
Region (Le6n and Chinandega), where 90% of the community investments would take place,
and the Atlantic Region with 10% or US$2.5 million ear-mark-ed for community development
activities. The GEF project would take place entirely in the Atlantic and would be the main
mechanisms for channeling funds for this region. The reason for the split of the IDA project is
because IDA project funds are needed for the Atlantic for assistance to indigenous
conmmunities with their development needs. Only 10% are allocated to the Atlantic because the
region is sparsely populated, people are less poor than in the Western region, and local
implementation capacity is more limited.
Consistently with the proposed calendar for the IDA and GEF projects, GON
decided during preparation to carry out a Social Assessment/Indigenous Peoples Development
Plan for the IDA project focused on Leon and Chinandega. The Social Assessment/Indigenous
Peoples Development Plan for the Atlantic would be carried out as part of preparation for the
GEF project. This split was necessary in light of the different timing of the two operations and
the need to better define the GEF project, including the delineation of the biodiversity corrioor,
before a meaningful Social Assessment/IPDP could be developed. IDA disbursements for
subprojects in the Atlantic would be conditional on the GEF project becoming effective,
including the subrnission to IDA/GEF of an acceptable Indigenous Peoples Development Plan
for the Atlantic.



Annex 5
Indigenous Peoples Strategy for the Atlantic Region
Page 7 of 8
Proposed GEF project
The GEF project would benefit indigenous communities as well as benefit from
their activities. Sustainable use of natural resources by indigenous peoples is consistent with
conservation of biodiversity and indigenous peoples would benefit from increased
environmental protection since this would reduce the threats on their resources from logging,
mining and fishing interests. The GEF project, which includes protected areas in the Atlantic,
raises some issues, however, related to indigenous peoples traditional natural resource use
rights in the context of biodiversity protection inside and outside of protected areas, and thus
includes two specific components to address these issues.
First, protection of biodiversity in indigenous traditional territories inside or outside
protected areas in the Atlantic would be achieved through quid pro quo agreements with
indigenous communities which have historical rights to vast areas to be included in the
biodiversity corridor. Second, a project component would finance activities to involve
indigenous communities in biodiversity protection and to assist them with community land
demarcation and natural resource management plans.
The Social Assessment/Indigenous Peoples Development Plan for the Atlantic
would comprise four main parts: (i) an analysis of the legal framework affecting indigenous
peoples, review of their organizations and institutional responsibilities, already completed (see
report entitled "Viabilidad y Condiciones Legales de Cunmplimiento de un Componente de
Legalizacion de Tierras a las Comunidades Indigenas de la Costa Atl6ntica dentro del
Proyecto de Manejo de Recursos Naturales" by Roque Roldan summarized above); (ii) an
Action Plan being developed by GON through workshops for prioritizing next steps in
addressing the legal constraints mainly for land titling and assigning institutional
responsibilities for their execution (see above); (iii) a participation plan to fully involve
indigenous communities in the design and implementation of the GEF component; and (iv) a
social assessment including rapid rural appraisals of non-indigenous communities in the
Atlantic and a participation plan for these communities.
Proposed IDA project
The proposed IDA project does not pose noticeable risks for indigenous
communities. On the contrary, although not specifically targeting indigenous communities, it
has built in mechanisms to give indigenous communities equal access to the project's resources
as non-indigenous communities and to support and strengthen all rural communities.
Nevertheless a Social Assessment/Indigenous Peoples Development Plan for the Sutiaba
community in Leon was carried out during the preparation of the proposed project to improve
the design of the participatory mechanisms included in the project. The results of the Social
Assessment/IPDP for Leon and Chinandega were summarized in Annex 3.
The technical assistance program to be managed by MARENA would include
improving the policy framework for natural resources including contributions to legislative
proposals under discussion in Nicaragua consistent with the constitutional rights of indigenous
communities and the constitutional principles of decentralization. The results of the studies
and analysis to be supported by the project would be disseminated, consulted and discussed
through workshops and other events with civic society. The IDA project would support a



Annex 5
Indigenous Peoples Strategy for the Atlantic Region
Page 8 of 8
person in MARENA in charge of participation and relations with NGOs and an indigenous
expert responsible for interaction with indigenous commnunities.
The technical assistance program (IDA and GEF) would also finance a land use
plan for the Atlantic that would include land use potentials and a preliminary identification of
indigenous traditional territories (IDA project budget:USS 120,000). This plan, which would
be approved by INETER, the agency officially responsible for demarcation of indigenous
territories, would be extensively consulted and discussed with indigenous communities and
regional governnents. The program also includes budget for studies of social and indigenous
issues to be carried out during the project's first year (total budget US$155,000).
The possibility of extending the IDA project investment activities (Rural
Municipalities Development Component) to the entire RAAN and RAAS for the benefit of
indigenous communities was included in project design (total budget: US$2.3 million) subject
to the GEF project becoming effective, and the submission to IDA of an Indigenous Peoples
Development Plan. An Environmental and Land Use Plan acceptable to IDA is also scheduled
to be completed by December 31, 1997. Although financial resources are not the key
constraint to the process of recognizing indigenous land and natural resource rights, the
proposed IDA project would make funds available to communities for a wide range of
subprojects to be proposed by the communities themselves, including technical and legal
assistance for the elaboration of community demarcation and natural resource management
proposals. These funds would be available both to indigenous communities in the Atlantic
region and to the Sutiaba in Le6n. This project component also includes funds for training
indigenous and non-indigenous commnunities on a wide range of topics and for participation,
dissemination and information activities for which indigenous communities would be eligible.
Project annual reviews would include assessments of progress made in the
preparation/implementation of the IPDP.



NICARAGUA
RURAL MUNICIPALITIES PROJECT
ANNEX 6
SUMMARY OF ENVIRONMENTAL ASSESSMENT AND MITIGATION PLAN
The proposed project is essentially beneficial to the environment since its objectives
include improving the policy and institutional framework for natural resources, improving
natural resource management at the local level, reducing the pressures on the Atlantic frontier
and protecting biodiversity in the Atlantic through the associated GEF project.
Environmental risks posed by the project, although minor, are associated with some
of the sub-project categories to be financed and the decentralization process to be supported by
the project. A mitigation plan for sub-projects was included in the sub-project cycle and is
described in Annex 4 and in the Operational Manual. An important instrument for managing
environmental risks would be the Environment and Land Use Plans (ELUP) to be developed
during the project's first year both for the Pacific and the Atlantic regions. These plans would
identify areas of high environmental risks and contain other information necessary for
MARENA and the MTUs to make decisions regarding environmental aspects of subprojects.
To minimize environmental risks from decentralization, an environmental
assessment process and mitigation plan at the municipal level, detailing the procedures that
municipalities should adopt to screen out investments that pose high environmental risks or to
include mitigating measures, was developed by INIFOM as part of the preparation for the
proposed project. The procedures are described in the project's background document in the
project file: " Sistema de Procedimientos Ambientales para la Gesti6n Ambiental Municipal".
These procedures, once implemented, would apply not only to the municipalities eligible for
the project, but to all municipalities in the country.
Activities in the Atlantic pose higher environmental risks, thus they would only be
initiated once the Environment and Land Use Plan for the Atlantic, including a delineation of
the proposed Atlantic Biodiversity Corridor, is completed. Subprojects in the Atlantic would
have to be consistent with this plan and with the Atlantic Biodiversity Corridor Strategy and
would only start once the associated GEF project becomes effective.

NICARAGUA
RURAL MUNICIPALITIES PROJECT
ANNEX 8
PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION ARRANGEMENTS
1. INSTITUTIONAL RESPONSIBILITIES
Project institutional responsibilities are discussed in the SAR and in the Project
Implementation Plan and Operational Manual which are in the project file. Diagram A8. 1
summarizes the institutional arrangements.
2. IMPLEMENTATION SCHEDULE
The proposed project would be implemented over a period of 5 years and is expected to
be completed in 2001. Activities would begin in 1996 following effectiveness and would be
eligible for retro-active financing in the amount of one million dollars. A cronogram of project
activities is included in the project implementation plan in the project file. These are also
summarized in the Table of implementation indicators in Annex 10. A detailed schedule of
activities for the first year of the project is in the project file.

NICARAGUA
RURAL MUNICIPALITIES PROJECT
ANNEX 10
PROJECT MONITORING, REPORTING, AND EVALUATION
1. MONITORING
The establishment of an efficient project monitoring system is instrumental to
assure the consistency of project activities, and in particular of the investment program, with
the stated objectives. A set of monitoring indicators, based on the project's logical framework
analysis, is shown in Table A 0. 1 through AI 0.7 and Figure 10. 1. The project monitoring
system is separated in two distinct units: one dealing with the municipal development
component implemented by INIFOM; and a second one focused on natural resource
management and protection implemented by MARENA. The project monitoring system would
be based on a Management Information System (MIS) with a database which organizes
pertinent physical and financial information.
A. Municipal Development Component
This component relies heavily on a decentralized system of decision-making and
management, involving a large number of small, diverse subprojects. Hence, since the ex ante
determination of the pattern of project investment and other key parameters is not possible,
effective supervision depends on the existence of a capable monitoring system. This system
will provide information regarding the progress of the subproject in its different stages (e.g.,
subproject approval, procurement, implementation, and disbursement), types of projects
approved and implemented, and verify that project activities are consistent with the guidelines,
targeting methodology, and selection criteria included in the Operational Manual (Reglamento
Operativo). This MIS would be managed with a decentralized database to be established by
the MTU, and aggregated at the INIFOM regional and central levels. INIFOM would also
disseminate lessons learned through monitoring among municipalities and communities. For
each subproject, the MIS would record at least the following information:
(1)  a brief subproject description;
(2)  location by community and municipality;
(3)  number of beneficiaries and among them number of women and indigenous
people;
(4)  cost of the subproject and co-financing including community contribution;
(5)  date of contract signing;
(6)  starting and finishing dates for the subproject;
(7)  an index of physical work carried out at each monitoring visit and a physical
measure at completion (e.k. kms. of community road rehabilitated; wells
built, area under erosion control, etc.);
(8)  acceptance by the community of the finished subproject;
(9)  name and address of community contact person;
(10) subproject sustainability, effective use, and replicability.



Annex 10
Project Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation
Page 2 of 11
Responsibilities. Responsibilities for monitoring will be shared between local and
central levels. Local monitoring would involve the community members involved in each sub-
project (Project User Group - PUG), the Community Supervision Committee (CSC), and the
Municipal Technical Unit (MTU). INIFOM's Regional Offices would assist in monitoring
some of the larger and special projects and could request assistance from specialized line
agencies such as MAG, INTA, INRA, MAG, MARENA, PNDR, INAA, FISE and MCT.
Local monitoring (supervision) of the cost and efficiency of works under implementation would
increasingly be carried out by the MTU, with support from the INIFOM Regional Offices,
which at present does the majority of this work. The central office of INIFOM would be
responsible for organizing the data in the MIS and supervising its regional offices.
Technical assistance in monitoring would be provided during the project pilot phase
(year 1). While some working experience exists in these areas, it is essential that for a period
of time the units responsible for planning, monitoring and evaluation receive assistance from a
qualified consultant, in order to assist in the design and operation the system. TORs for a
consultant to assist INIFOM and MARENA in reviewing and fine-tuning the planning and
monitoring and evaluation functions and procedures is included in the Project Implementation
Plan.
B. Natural Resources and Biodiversity Components
Monitoring of activities in this component can be handled using MARENA's
existing monitoring and reporting systems, except for the Associated Atlantic Biodiversity
Corridor Project (GEF). This component would require a specific system currently being
designed.
2. REPORTING
To the extent possible, reporting procedures within each component would follow
existing institutional reporting systems, in order to avoid duplication of effort. The complete
project reporting system would produce different types of reports, depending on the user level
involved. Two different project progress reports will be submitted to IDA: one from INIFOM
on municipal development; and one from MARENA on natural resources policies and
institutions and biodiversity protection.
MTUs would issue simple monthly reports (one or two pages) to INIFOM, signed
by the mayor, listing subproject progress, work carried out, implementation difficulties and
main actions programmed for the following month. Information would flow from this level to
the Regional Delegations of INIFOM, which would issue aggregate monthly reports. These
monthly reports would be sent to the Directorate of Planning of INIFOM in Managua.
INIFOM's Directorate of Planning would be responsible for bringing together the
data from both Regional offices, and other information on the project's activities managed
centrally (training, technical assistance, etc.), to be included in the reports for the senior
management of INIFOM, CONAGRO, MIFIN and for IDA to be issued following the end of
each quarter (March 31, June 30, September 30 and December 31). The quarterly reports
would contain the current update of the MIS, with all data provided in a suitable magnetic
base.



Annex 10
Project Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation
Page 3 of 11
The Directorate of Planning of MARENA would also issue separate quarterly
reports (March 31, June 30, September 30 and December 31) listing accomplishments,
problems and next steps, for senior management of MARENA, CONAGRO, MIFIN and IDA
The general content of each of the two quarterly reports (INIFOM and MARENA)
would include:
(1)  A summary of project implementation. This section would provide an
overall assessment of project progress, indicating major advances and
problems identified, in relation to targets set out in the annual operating
plan, as well as in regards to important unplanned events. Progress
regarding problems and proposed actions to resolve them from the previous
reporting period should be included.
(2)  Local and central government activities. Given the project's focus on
developing local institutions, the reports should clearly differentiate activities
and problems at the municipal/community level, from those carried out
directly by INIFOM and MARENA.
(3)  Indicators. Reference must be made to the main monitoring indicators (see
Tables AI0. I through AI0.5). These indicators would allow report
recipients to gauge project progress in important physical aspects. Where
changes in the indicators occur from one quarter to the next, these should be
reported and conunented upon. Where no change in an indicator takes place,
this should be clearly stated. Indicators of use of funds (financial
indicators), would be provided to indicate resources provided and used by
financing source (the IDA, Government of Nicaragua, Municipalities,
beneficiary communities)
(4)  Status of legal covenants. A summary of compliance with the project's
legal covenants as established in the legal documents (loan agreement,
INIFOM agreement, municipality agreements) would be an annex to each
report.
The two reports from INIFOM and MARENA pertaining to the final quarter of
each year, would include, in addition to the above information, accumulated data on the full
year's accomplishments and other information required to provide a complete picture of
component development. Tables of indicators would be designed in order to facilitate
comparison of the current year's data with that of previous years.
3. EVALUATION
The project would develop and implement a range of evaluation studies to measure
the impact of the subproject investments and to provide appropriate feedback to improve
project methodology and implementation. INIFOM would be responsible for drafting the
Termns of References (TORs) -- in accordance with IDA -- and contracting the consulting
services for the following evaluation studies:



Annex 10
Project Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation
Page 4 of 11
( I) Annual Reviews to assist the GON and IDA in the identification of positive
experiences and implementation problems and bottlenecks, in order to
recommend modification to project methodology and procedures for
incorporation during the second half of the project implementation period.
Among the various aspects of project implementation to be reviewed, the
annual reviews would evaluate the efficiency and impact of the different
components, the effectiveness of institutional development activities, the
level of community participation, and the ability of the project to target and
reach the poor. The annual reviews would evaluate the success of
subprojects in terrns of their specific objectives, and the quality, efficiency,
and sustainability of the investments. Within a statistically significant
sample, the study would better analyze the following points: (i) beneficiary
participation in the selection process; (ii) physical design adequacy, unitary
costs, and efficiency of procurement procedures; (iii) the targeting of
subproject benefits, and (iv) evaluation of sustainability, effective use, and
replicability of the investments after their physical implementation,
particularly in terms of capacity building.
(2)   Project Impact Evaluation. The project would finance a comprehensive
evaluation, based on a statistically significant sample, to assess project
impact. The impact evaluation would be carried out by independent
consultants and contracted under TORs and procedures acceptable to the
Bank.
