NICARAGUA
RURAL MUNICIPALITIES PROJECT
ANNEX 11
PROJECT SUPERVISION
The proposed project is complex because it deals with three levels of decision-
making -- central government, municipal governments and communities -- and two managing
agencies: INIFOM and MARENA. Moreover most of the project funds will go to a large
number of small subprojects covering a variety of sectors in two different regions of the
country. Although the project preparation team put particular care in minimizing complexity,
by phasing in project activities and building into project design checks and balances to keep the
project on track, supervision needs are greater than for the typical Bank project. Furthermore
there must be opportunities, during project implementation, to revisit project design and
management and to introduce corrections if needed to enable it to meet its objectives. Hence
comprehensive Annual Reviews would take place during implementation.
Pilot Phase
The pilot phase would take place during the project's first year and would focus on
technical assistance for building capacity in INIFOM, MARENA and municipalities, project
promotion and dissemination, preparation of municipal investment plans, training of service
providers, environment and land use plans and continuation of preparation of associated GEF
project. A limited number of subprojects would be identified and prepared to test procedures.
Project launch workshops. Four project launch workshops would take place
immediately after effectiveness (month 1). All workshops would take place at approximately
the same time. The first workshop would be targeted at the management agencies - INIFOM
and MARENA -- and in addition to reviewing project objectives, implementation arrangements
and procedures, it would focus on procurement, disbursements, financial management,
monitoring and evaluation. The second workshop would include, in addition to INIFOM and
MARENA, other GON agencies expected to play a role in supporting project implementation,
namely MAG, INTA, INRA, PNDR, INAA and FISE. This short workshop would review
objectives and implementation arrangements and in particular discuss the roles of those
support agencies. The third workshop would be targeted at 32 municipal governments
already selected for the project. It would focus on reviewing the procedures described in the
Operational Manual (Reglamento Interno), Subproject Cycle Training Manual and Project
Implementation Plan. The fourth workshop would be targeted at community leaders and local
NGOs and would again review the above project management documents and in particular
clearly discuss community rights and responsibilities under the project. World Bank
participation in these workshops would be:
Task Manager
Procurement and Disbursements Specialist
Participation Specialist
Total Bank SW - 5 SW

promotion and dissemination of project; (5) community structures; (6) training of service

project design, implementation and management arrangements if necessary. It would consist

First Phase
The first phase of the project would take place during the project's first year. At
the start of this phase INIFOM, MARENA, municipal governments and some communities
would be familiar with the project procedures and would have received training and technical
assistance in key project areas. Procedures would have been tested. The environment and land
use plans would be ready. The focus of the first phase would be on continuing with the
technical assistance programs and on initiating the subproject cycle in selected communities in
the 23 municipalities of Leon and Chinandega.

Total Bank Resources for Pilot and First Phase

and another 30 SW would be needed at headquarters. The total staff requirements for the
project's first two years would be around 81 SW. Supervision requirements would decline
during the second phase once procedures are well internalized and key obstacles to
implementation removed.

Second Phase
The second phase of the project would consist of the last three years of the project.
This would be the full-fledged implementation phase with the number of subprojects in the 23
municipalities of Leon and Chinandega quickly increasing as experience with procedures
increases. The 9 municipalities of San Carlos and RAAS would initiate subprojects in this
phase. The Associated GEF project would also start at the beginning of this phase. A
supervision plan for the second phase would be prepared during the second-year review.



NICARAGUA
RURAL MUNICIPALITIES PROJECT
ANNEX 12
ECONOMIC IMPACTS
1. INTRODUCTION
Benefit estimation of investments in institutional strengthening, poverty alleviation
and environmental protection is not simple. On the one hand, ex-post cross-country
econometric studies have found time and again a clear positive relation between investments in
those activities and countries' economic development (see for example recent Bank's World
Development Reports on poverty, environment, health and infrastructure). On the other hand,
ex-ante estimation of the benefits of a particular investment of that sort is fraught with
difficulties, stemming from high levels of uncertainty, lack of engineering data on relationships
between inputs and outputs, limited price information and widespread externalities. These
difficulties are compounded in the proposed project because, given its demand driven nature,
which investments will be actually implemented is unknown. Hence the purpose of this Annex
is to more specifically identify project costs and benefits and to roughly estimate, as orders of
magnitude, the economic impacts of the project from a subset of activities given assumptions
of what kinds of subprojects will be demanded and implemented.
2. ECONOMIC BENEFITS
There are three dimensions of economic impacts from the proposed project: (1)
from decentralization; (2) from natural resource and environmental policy reform; and (3)
from subprojects.
The expected economic impacts from decentralization are primarily three: (a)
reduction in costs of delivering public services relative to the costs of these services if they
were managed centrally; (b) a better optimum (more efficient mix) of local investments
relative to the mix accruing if decisions were taken by a centralized agency; and (c) improved
community structures and thus increases in the social capital.
The expected economic impacts from natural resource and environmental policy
reform are primarily three: (a) economic efficiency gains from eliminating many spurious
regulations on natural resource use; (b) reduction in the costs of governing due to increased
government credibility from actually enforcing a clearer legal and policy framework than under
the without project scenario; and (c) better intemalization of environmental extemalities by the
public, private and NGO sectors.
The expected economic impacts from subprojects are primarily four: (a) through
leaming by doing and training reduction in the costs of municipalities and communities in
identifying, preparing, implementing and operating subprojects; (b) improved decision-making
by private farmers and other rural poor from the capacity building projects; (c) economic
gains from enhanced environmental protection; and (d) economic surpluses from economic



Annex 12
Economic Impacts
Page 2 of 10
infrastructure and higher productivity from on-farm and off-farrn economic subsistence
activities to be supported by the project.
B. SUBPROJECTS
Methodology Used. During preparation economic analysis focused only on a
subset of those benefits: the economic impacts of the expected portfolio of environmental and
productive subprojects. The rationale was that if this subset of benefits is sufficient to cover
all economic project costs, the project makes economic sense and it is not necessary to estimate
the other, more difficult to quantify benefits. Because sub-projects are demand driven and it is
not known ex-ante what community and municipality choices will be, two estimates of sub-
project demand were developed by polling municipal governments, NGOs and beneficiaries
directly (see Annexes 1 and 3).
Therefore, the present estimates should be regarded as an exercise, aiming only at
giving an order of magnitude of the project's potential benefits. The exercise looks only to the
more productive related components (environmental protection, rural infrastructure, and rural
production) which together account for some 50% of expected total project costs. No benefit
estimates are attempted for the other 50% of the project which would cover institutional
strengthening activities and capacity building at the national and local level. Within each
category a short list of possible subprojects was used to illustrate the potential economic
benefits and the environmental and poverty alleviation achievements of the environmental
protection, rural infrastructure and rural production components. The following types of
subprojects were evaluated: (1) erosion control; (2) mangrove protection; (3) rural water; (4)
rehabilitation of municipal and community roads; and (5) subsistence agriculture/livestock
subprojects.
Environmental Subprojects
Erosion Control. Although soil erosion is to a large extent a natural process, its
acceleration due to deforestation, agricultural practices, and other rural development activities
(see Annex 2), can have detrimental consequences both at the farm level (reduced crop yields)
and off-site (flooding, biodiversity losses, damage to infrastructure). Although this analysis
focuses only on on-farm soil erosion impacts there is substantial anecdotal evidence that in the
Pacific Region of Nicaragua off-site impacts of soil erosion are even more important, in
particular through the damage to infrastructure (roads and irrigation systems) and respiratory
disease due to volcanic dust from wind erosion.
Agriculturally induced soil erosion and resulting crop losses have surfaced as major
concerns in all the surveys carried out during project preparation. Available figures seem to
confirm this concern. Soils in the project region are to a large extent young soils from volcanic
origin, hence prone to erosion, particularly wind erosion, as a result of the region's
predominant semiarid climate, with a long dry season. According to De Franco, et al (1993)1
soil losses in Leon and Chinandega agricultural areas range from a low 35 - 45 ton/ha/year, for
permanent or semi-permanent crops, like rice or sugar, to 100 ton/halyear or more, in soils
' De Franco et al (1993) "Erosion and Economic Growth in Nicaragua" Central Bureau of Statistics,
Oslo, and Instituto Centroamericano de Administraci6n de Empresas, Managua.



Annex 12
Economic Impacts
Page 3 of 10
devoted to annual crops such as beans, corn, sesame and cotton. Soil losses in recently
deforested areas are higher.
De Franco, et al (1993) provide detailed erosion figures and associated yield declines
for six Nicaraguan regions, and eight different crops, from 1981 to 1991. Together with
production and yields figures (from Nicaragua's MAG) they were used to calculate:
(1) An average production value of US$179 per hectare/year. This is a weighted
average based on production and yield figures for the six most important regional
crops during 1993/94, valued at 1994 fann gate prices.2
(2) An average erosion induced yield loss of 1.5 % per hectare/year. This is also a
weighted average, based on 1981 - 1991 erosion figures, and 1993/94 production
figures.
Considering that the regional harvested area in 1993/94 was approximately 200,000
ha, the estimates from (1) and (2) can be used to roughly calculate annual erosion related
losses, and their present value over a multi-year period.
The potential on-farm gains of soil erosion control are significant. Arresting soil
erosion in the area currently under cultivation, over the next 20 years, would result in increased
crops with a present value of US$ 52 million dollars, or US$ 23 per ha (at a 10% discount
rate)3.
According to estimations developed during project preparation, conservation costs
could range from a low of US$ 81 dollars/ha (mostly cultivation and cropping changes, and the
use of vegetation barriers) to a high of US$ 405 dollars/ha. Estimated on-farm benefits rule out
the more expensive conservation methods. On the other hand, if an average cost figure of
US$137 is used, 75% (US$4.3 million) of the project's environmental protection component
would be enough to finance the protection of 27,400 ha, approximately 14% of the current
crop area. This would entail benefits of the order of US$ 8.6 million, and a benefit-cost ratio of
2.0. Benefits would be significantly higher if future increases in rural production (the
assumption is that production would continue at current levels except for gains from erosion
controls) or off-farm benefits had been considered as well.
These figures are only illustrative. A recent study conducted in Central America
(Lutz, et al, 1994) suggests that, in many instances, this is not the case even when erosion
control investments are provided at no cost. In Nicaragua, terraces were built on farns in the
Lake Xolotan watershed, at no cost to the farmers, with the purpose of reducing flood hazards
in Managua city. But since terraces interfered with cultivation practices, and did not result in
perceived benefits to the farmer, they were soon destroyed. Given the demand driven nature of
this project these problems would be avoided.
2 Hereafter "region" refers to both Le6n and Chinandega.
3      A manzana -mz- equals 0.74 hectare. Figures are net of incremental harvest costs. All USS
figures are based on an exchange rate of 7.5 Nicaragua's Co. per US$ dollar. Hereafter all present
value figures are based in a 20 year period and a 10% discount rate.



Annex 12
Economic Impacts
Page 4 of 10
Mangrove Protection. The larger portion of Nicaragua's mangrove is in the project's
region, mostly in the Chinandega department. Although nearly 80% of the regions 84,700
hectare of mangrove have been declared protected area, they are actually highly intervened by
small farmers and fishermen's activities (extraction of firewood, poles, shellfishes, shrimp
larvae, and mangroves' bark), and the deforestation of large tracts of mangroves to install
shrimp ponds, of which 7,000 hectares have already been granted, and authorization is pending
for another 10,000 hectares.4
If the process of mangrove deterioration is not arrested, it will soon reduce the
productivity of the very same activities that now breed on it, a process already at work in
nearby countries such as Ecuador and Honduras. Unfortunately there is no comprehensive
study on the economic value of Nicaragua's mangroves, which could guide the design of
technical alternatives and the estimation of benefits.' In the meantime, available local and
intemational experiences provide some evidence of what such benefits might be:
(1) Families living from small-scale extractive activities in Chinandega's mangroves
report an annual income ranging from US$ 1,000 to US$ 2,000 net of production
costs, based on a harvesting area of approximately 10 ha. per family (an annual
economic value per mangrove ha/year of US$ 100 - 200 dollars).
(2) Due to the incipient shrimp pond production, larvae collection has been modest,
averaging some US$ 33,000 dollars a year, but if the full potential 30,000 ha. of
ponds were in place they could represent a market for shrimp larvae of some US$
4 million dollars a year, or US$ 133 per mangrove ha/year. Since current prices
paid for wild larvae --US$ 2.5 per thousand are half the cost of laboratory
produced larvae, this figure could be considered representative of the net benefits
provided by the ecosystem supply of wild larvae. Unfortunately there is no
indication to assess if this would be a sustainable larvae's capture level.
(3) The 1993 harvest of shrimp off Nicaragua's Pacific coast amounted to 715,500
kgs, 90% of which was exported at an average price of US$ 1.2 per kg. It seems
that current capture is well below its sustainable level, considering that studies of
shrimp productivity in coastal Asian waters off mangrove areas suggest that 1
hectare of mangrove can be the early breading ground of as much as 862 kgs of
adult see shrimps (with a corresponding gross value of US$ 4,950 dollar). It
should be noticed, though, that early larvae capture would reduce this productivity
figures and, furthermore the region's mangrove are already highly deteriorated.
Also it is not clear that the Asia figures can be extrapolated to the Nicaragua case.
(4) Studies of several Caribbean areas have estimated net harvest values ranging from
a low US$ 50 dollars/halyear where only traditional extractive activities take
4      Although several references mentioned 7,000 ha of existing ponds, those in production seem
to be only a fraction --around 250 ha. to 300 ha.-- considering 1993 figures for shrimp production --
138,198 kgs - and average yields of 545 kgs/hectare.
5      In 1995 two small projects with international support began working on sustainable
development projects in region's mangrove areas.



Annex 12
Economic Impacts
Page 5 of 10
place, up to a high US$ 1,550 dollars/ha/year where intensive shrimp ponds are in
place (Hamilton/Snedaker, 1984).6
Considering the high range of extractive activities reported in the region's mangroves -
-US$ 200 per ha/year of net benefits-- the net present value of an hectare of mangrove over a
20 year period, would be around US$ 1,700-1,900. With a conservative mangroves'
deforestation figure of 3.5% per year, the present value of potential losses of mangrove
deforestation over a 20 years period would represent about US$33.8 million. It should be
remembered that these are "potential" net benefits, that only would accrue if and when the
mangrove is harvested on a sustainable base.
Costs of conservation cum sustainable production projects in mangrove areas varies
widely, with some estimates developed during project preparation as low as US$ 70/hectare
and two current projects in the area featuring costs around US$ 500/hectare. Allocating 25%
of the project's environmental component (US$1.5 million) to mangrove protection could result
in the conservation of some 3,000 hectare of mangrove, with a present value of potential
benefits of about US$ 3.6 million and a benefit cost ratio of 2.4.
Municipal and Community Infrastructure Subprojects.
Both provision of drinking water and roads improvernent have been singled out as the
most important infrastructure developments to ameliorate the plight of rural poor in Nicaragua
(World Bank, 1995). Therefore they are used below as examples of the activities that could be
funded by the project's infrastructure subprojects (US$ 7.4 million).
Rural water supply. According to end of 1993 figures, over 70% of Nicaragua's
rural poor lacked access to piped water, inside or outside their houses, or access to a public
standpipe. Almost all rural poor lacked any sewer system and over 156,000 rural poor (some
27,000 families) lacked a water connection or access to a public standpipe. The high social and
economic costs of this situation have been repeatedly highlighted:
"...children in households without water and sanitation are twice more likely to be
malnourished than children in households with water and sanitation.. Low coverage
of water and sanitation services.. explain why diarrhea and vector borne diseases are
still the main causes of morbidity and mortality in Nicaragua" (World Bank, 1995,
p.ii).
Poor people in rural areas pay for trucks delivered water more then ten times the urban
water rates: US$ 2 to US$ 3.3 per cubic meter, compared to an average of US$ 0.16 per cubic
meter paid in urban areas. Considering that, on average, poor rural dwellers without water
services use one hour a day for water collection, each family is investing the equivalent of US$
110 per year to obtain a very limited supply of poor quality water (this figure is based on a
salary of US$ 0.30 per hour, that is the lower end of hourly earnings of poor rural females).
Considering an opportunity cost correction factor for rural labor in Nicaragua of 0.6, the
economic cost of water collection at present per family-year would be US$ 66.
6      Hamilton L & Snedaker S. (1994) "Handbook for Mangrove Area Management". IUCN,
UNECO, UNEP, EWC, Gland.



Anncx 12
Economic Impacts
Page 6 of 10
Costs of rural water provision vary with the characteristics of the site and the
pretended level of service. Cost of house connection to piped systems (gravity or power
pumped water systems) ranges from US$ 45 to US$ 100 per person. Providing individual or
communal manual pumps is much cheaper, with per capita costs in the range of US$ 10 to
US$ 30. Using US$ 50 as an intermediate value (US$ 290 per family), providing water to all
the non-served rural poor in Leon and Chinandega, would cost US$ 7.8 million. Assuming
that US$ 5.9 million would be spent in community rural water projects, the present value of
costs including 5% per year of operation and maintenance would add up to US$ 8.2 million,
compared with US$ 11.0 million of family savings. The benefit cost ratio would be 1.3.
Rehabilitation of Rural Municipal and Community Roads. Between 1980 and 1991
Nicaragua lost 3,500 km of rural roads, almost 20% of its 1980 road system. Bad rural roads
have been blamed for agricultural production losses, high transport costs, and hence high
spreads between market prices and farmn prices (see World Bank, 1995, vol 1). During 1994,
wholesale prices were 39% higher than farm-gate prices, for a weighted average of the six
more important regional crops.
Le6n and Chinandega have 2,500 Km of roads, of which 950 are rural roads, suitable
for dry season use only. The costs of upgrading community and municipality roads to an
around-the-year use standard range between US$ 800 to US$ 1,500 per km. To revamp the
thousand kilometers of existing rural roads and assuming the higher figure cost, would cost
about US$ 1.5 million (some 20% of the project's rural infrastructure component).
If better roads could reduce the spread between farm gate and wholesale prices by 5%
(from 39% to 37%), that would represent an extra income to local farmers in the order of US$
2.9 dollars/mz/year, as much as US$ 0.8 million per year considering the current harvested
area.7 Over 20 years, and considering the higher investment cost (US$ 1,500 per km) plus 5%
annual maintenance cost, the present value of total cost would amount to US$ 2.1 million,
compared to the present value of benefits of US$ 6.7 million and a benefit-cost ratio of 3.2
Increases in farm-gate prices are not the only result from better rural roads. According
to the World Bank poverty report, poor rural roads, together with lack of storage and treatment
facilities, contributed to the loss of as much as 20% of the corn and 8% of the beans harvested
in Nicaragua (World Bank, 1995 vol I p.42). Better rural roads would result in an increase in
production and a decrease in crop losses. Hence the figures quoted are a low estimate of the
benefits accruing from better rural roads.
Community Productive Subprojects
Each category of municipal investments would have, directly or indirectly, a positive
impact on rural production. The category of municipal subprojects specifically aims at
increasing rural production from poor farmers. In total about US$7.4 million of community
productive investments could be financed through the project. Their financial and economic
costs and benefits are addressed in this section.
' Based in a Co. 1465,20 per hectare average value for the 1993/94 harvest of 274,000 manzanas.

is used in this estimation since the 39% price spread is an overall average.

To evaluate the economic returns to subprojects included in this category eleven
expected crop or activity budgets and three farm models were assessed. These budgets and
farm models compare the with and without project situation and forecast improvements upon
current production practice that would result in a 20% to 33% increase in crop yields,
according to the selected crop or activity. The yield increases would come about through soil
conservation measures and new inputs, specially better seeds. Summary information on crops,
family income per cropped area, and expected outputs are provided in Tables A 12.1 and
A12.2.

For the purpose of the financial and economic analysis, several representative farm
models where developed in order to show what would happen without the project and what
would occur with the proposed project. The models indicate present and projected values for
the main variables (investments, labor use, operating costs, production and gross and net
family revenues) considering market prices the financial analysis and economic (or shadow)
prices for the estimation of the overall impact of the project. For the latter, a standard
conversion factor of 0.85 was considered and a cost of labor conversion factor of 0.60 was
used in order to obtain an approximation of the opportunity cost of family labor. The seven
farm models represent a variety of farming systems expected to be chosen/promoted and the
expected changes in cropping patterns/family incomes. Table A. 12.2 summarizes the with
project situation for the seven farm models.

The summary results of the economic retums from the adoption of those farm models
is presented in Table A12.3. All models are financially profitable suggesting that beneficiaries
are likely to be interested in the adoption of the conservation measures and changes in cropping
patterns sampled. However in the case of smaller-income farms, although financially profitable
(positive NPV and attractive IRR), a decline in family income in the first years may prevent
households from adopting the conservation measures and agroforestry systems analyzed. In
such cases it is justified, given the project's poverty objective, that financial incentives be
offered through the project in order to facilitate the adoption of the new patterns provided that
the soil improvement interventions and positive environmental effects are achieved.

To estimate the economic impact of the productive projects' subcomponent, with and
without project scenarios were considered, assuming that 10,000 small farmers (households)
would be benefited.
3.     POVERTY ALLEVIATION
The project focus is on environmental protection and poverty alleviation. References
have already been made to both issues in relation to the environmental protection and to the
rural water components. Some further comments on the extent and composition of poverty, and
on the potential poverty alleviation impact of financing rural production follow.
According to the World Bank 1995 study, Nicaragua's poverty line as of November
1993 was Co 214.47 of per capita monthly income (with an extreme poverty line at Co. 101.32
of per capita monthly income). Since the average rural family size is 5.8 persons per
household, the rural family income poverty line, adjusted to 1995 prices would be al Co.
18,700 family/year or US$ 2,487 /family/year.8
As of the end of 1993, Nicaragua had a total population of 4 million people. Le6n and
Chinandega accounted for 702,000 people of which 312,000 people lived in rural areas. At
that time, more than half Nicaragua's population was under the poverty line, and almost 20%
of the population was in extreme poverty. Country-wide, poverty is concentrated in rural
areas. This pattern is even more marked in Ledn and Chinandega (see Table A 12.4)
8      The poverty line is defined as the total per capita monthly expenditure at which an
individual obtains the minimum daily caloric requirements. The extreme poverty line is defined as the
level of per capita monthly food expenditures required to obtain the daily minimum caloric
requirement (World Bank, 1995, vo. I p.2). Hereafter figures for poor population are inclusive of
extremely poor population.

As much as 83% of the rural poor male population is engaged in agriculture, in most
cases both as peasants and occasional wage earners. Although the majority of Nicaragua's
rural poor households are peasants, only half their income comes from farming and related
activities9 with another third coming from wages.
The US$ 6.7 million that according to demand estimates are expected to be channeled
to community productive subprojects could benefit between 1,450 and 10,000 such families. If
the higher figure were achieved it would signify benefiting 25% of the region's poor farmers.
Comparatively, in 1993 only 720 poor farmers in the region received some kind of assistance
(averaging $ 560 dollars).
Reaching 25% of the region poor farmers is probably difficult. According to some
estimates, for a farm to produce an annual income of US$ 2,500 dollars/year, it should have
between 4 and 14 ha in production (depending on the crop mix). But the majority of poor
farmers have holdings of less than 3 hectares. Significant increases in small farm sizes would
be called for in order to achieve above poverty incomes, based solely in the farm production.
On the other hand, farming is currently a marginal source of income for a considerable number
of poor peasants. Thus the project includes in the category of community productive
subprojects non-farm investments, such as microenterprises and small processing facilities,
which would be a way to complement, or in some cases substitute for, farn income, in order to
help a larger number of rural poor rise above poverty incomes.
4.     SUMMARY OF BENEFITS
Tables A12.5 through A12.7 give a summarv presentation of the findings in terms of
economic benefits, environmental improvements, and poverty alleviation. The proposed project
is expected to have a positive impact on the country's economy and on the sustainability of its
natural resources. The NPV of the incremental benefits, net of project costs was estimated at
9      These figures add up self-employment, imputed rent, and own production (World Bank,
1995 Vol 11 p.64)

Page 10 of 10
US$2.3 million and the Benefit/Cost Ratio at a 10% discount rate was 1.04. As for
uncertainties and the risks of failing to attain the proposed targets, rural infrastructure
development presents the lowest risks. While there is a large experience in rural projects, in
most cases rural development fails to attain the project's design figures. The environmental
component entails the highest risks due to Nicaragua's limited experiences with this type of
activities.
